MALAYSIA
NATIONAL EXTENSION PROJECT
SUMMARY AND CONCLUSIONS
1.        This proposed project is a follow-up to the Malaysian Government's
program to strengthen agricultural research and the extension services, which
was assisted under the Research and Extension Project (Loan 1115-MA) and
would strengthen agricultural services at the state level. The project is of
high priority because of its major contribution to achieving the Third
Malaysia Plan (TMP) objective of assisting the poorest rural sector of Malaysian
society. The project would endeavor to make the benefits of improved tech-
nology available to those farmers who have not benefited from specific devel-
opment projects, such as irrigation, land settlement and rubber replanting;
and particularly those farmers who presently lack the knowledge or resources
to adopt improved technology. This would be accomplished through an expanded
and intensified extension training and farmer education effort to reduce the
gap between known technology and the application of technology on the farm.
Major emphasis would initially be on improved crop husbandry.
2.        The proposed project's objective would be to increase agricultural
production, and raise the income and standard of living of about one million
rural families throughout Malaysia. These represent more than 80% of the
rural families served by the Department of Agriculture (DOA) extension service
and comprise the majority of the poorest group in the agricultural sector.
They are the padi farmers, coconut farmers, and mixed crop farmers who rely
on rainfed agriculture and cultivate about 1.5 million acres in Peninsular
Malaysia and 1.0 million acres in Sabah and Sarawak.
3.        The proposed project would achieve these objectives through:
(a) substantial recruitment of new extension staff;
(b) expansion and upgrading of pre-service agricultural
training; and
(c) expansion and upgrading of in-service extension training;
provision of training fellowships; improvement of extension
methods (including regular programs of training and farm
visits); expansion and improvement of quality seed production;
and technical assistance for the above activities.
4.        The proposed project would strengthen the existing state extension
services within the context of an established operational organization which
is basically sound, but deficient because of staff shortages and inadequately
trained and poorly equipped staff. Over the five-year project period, the
number of field level staff would be increased by 67% from 780 (a ratio of
1:1,300 farm families) to 1,340 (1:800). Provision would be made for main-
taining and increasing the technical knowledge of all extension staff through
periodic in-service training. Extension activities would also be strengthened
by the provision of additional supporting subject matter specialists; by the
construction of Extension Subcenters (ESCs) and Area Forward Bases (AFBs) to



- ii -
provide the operational base (including staff housing) for extension activities
where existing facilities are inadequate; and by the provision of equipment,
fellowships and technical assistance.
5.        Total project cost is estimated at US$46.3 million, of which
US$19.0 million (41%) would be foreign exchange. Extension, at a cost of
about US$26.0 million (excluding training), would be the major component of
the project. Total extension component capital and operating cost would be
US$26.0 per farm family under the project.
6.        The cost of extension in-service and pre-service training and related
agricultural services training would be US$15.3 million and would provide for
an average of three weeks' periodic training for more than 2,500 agricultural
field officers each year at five Regional Extension Training and Development
Centers (RETDCs); four in the Peninsula (serving all 11 states) and one in
Sarawak. The Agricultural Vocational School (AVS) in Sabah would be streng-
thened by the provision of facilities and equipment so as to better utilize
its present capacity for 190 students and to turn out 60 qualified three-year
certificate level operatives each year. Additional facilities would be
provided at the AVS for RETDC in-service training. An Agricultural Institute
(AI) with a capacity to train 125 operatives annually and a combined RETDC
in-service training facility with a 300 acre school farm would be provided
in Sarawak. One hundred and sixty-six man-years of fellowships would also be
provided for specialized undergraduate and postgraduate training to upgrade
management and technical staff.
7.        The seeds component would cost US$5.0 million and would have a
capacity to produce annually at full development 2,000 tons of improved padi
seed and 400 tons of minor crop seeds in Peninsular Malaysia; 200 tons of
padi seed, 150 tons of minor crop seeds in Sabah; and 250 tons of padi seed
and up to 50 tons of minor crop seeds in Sarawak. This would increase the
availability of improved seeds from the present 5% to more than 15% of the
annual requirement.
8.        Implementation would take five years and the Director General of
the Federal DOA would have responsibility for project implementation. The
Federal Director of the DOA's Extension Branch would become the National
Extension Project Director and would coordinate project implementation for
all states. The State Directors of Agriculture in Sabah and Sarawak would
be Assistant Project Directors. Each State Director of Agriculture would
have overall responsibility for the execution of the extension component by
the state's Chief Extension Officer.
9.        Civil works construction (about US$14.0 million) to be financed
under the proposed project would consist of relatively small and scattered
contracts (averaging less than US$100,000) and would be awarded following
local procedures which are satisfactory to the Bank. About US$8.0 million of
larger civil works contracts (costing more than US$400,000 each) would be
awarded under international competitive bidding in accordance with Bank Group
procurement guidelines. Contracts for materials, equipment and vehicles
(about US$7.5 million) would also be awarded under international competitive



bidding in accordance with these guidelines. Consulting services totaling 8.5
man-years would be engaged under terms and conditions of service satisfactory
to the Bank. A total of 166 man-years of fellowslt4-q for undergraduate,
graduate and postgraduate training would be managed under annual programs
acceptable to the Bank. The total cost of consultants and fellowships would
be about US$4.0 million.
10.       Implementation of agricultural subsidy programs is already a major
activity of the extension service and these programs are being increased with-
out adequate knowledge of their impact on agricultural production. In order
to establish operational and policy guidelines for extension and subsidy
programs the DOA would develop and implement an evaluation and monitoring
system. The UNDP/IBRD State Rural Development Project specialists would
assist the DOA in the development of a suitable monitoring and evaluation
system. The project proposes to progressively release extension staff from
subsidy programs and regulatory functions and emphasize educational activities,
i.e. training, visits and demonstrations.
11.       The proposed project would raise the productivity and income of
approximately one million rural families in Malaysia. Conservative calcula-
tions show that the incremental production that can be expected from the project
would generate an economic rate of return of at least 25%. Investment in exten-
sion and training components of the project would be financed from budgetary
allocations and would not be directly recoverable. However, the seeds component
of the project would eventually be self-financing and should generate a 14%
financial rate of return on investment.
12.       Risks inherent in this administratively complex project were fully
considered and were taken into account with respect to phasing, and estimates
of costs and benefits. In view of the high priority that the Government
attaches to assisting the poorest rural sector of Malaysian society and the
benefits that would accrue to a large number of rural families and the economy,
the risks are acceptable.
13.       The proposed project is recommended for a Bank loan of US$19.0 mil-
lion with a 17-year maturity and a grace period of 3-1/2 years. The borrower
would be the Federation of Malaysia.






MALAYSIA
NATIONAL EXTENSION PROJECT
i. INTRODUCTION
1.01      The Government of Malaysia (GOM) has asked the Bank to assist with
the expansion and upgrading of its agricultural extension, training, and
seed production programs for Peninsular Malaysia, Sabah and Sarawak over the
five-year period 1978-82. The proposed project would increase the number
and upgrade the training of extension field staff and specialists, and
provide additional buildings, staff quarters and equipment, which together
would improve the quality of extension services. Controlled and expanded
seed production would make improved seeds for the principal crops available
to all farmers. Project components would be designed to meet the more
varied agricultural requirements of Sabah and Sarawak and provide pre-service
as well as in-service training. This would be the twelfth Bank loan in
support of agricultural development in Malaysia, but only the first Pan-
Malaysian agricultural loan, since this project would include Sabah and
Sarawak.
1.02      The project was identified initially under Loan 1115-MA and prepared
by the Federal DOA, assisted by the FAO/IBRD Cooperative Program. This report
is based on the findings of an appraisal mission that visited Malaysia in
October and November 1976, composed of Messrs. C. Downing, P. Dax, N. Owens,
B. Woods (Bank) and G. Alcorn, M. Barker, and F. Jessup (consultants).
2. BACKGROUND
General
2.01      Malaysia covers an area of 333,000 sq km, of which 132,000 sq km
are in the 11 states of Peninsular Malaysia and the remaining 60% in the
Borneo states of Sarawak and Sabah. Of the total population of 12.3 million
in 1976, 10.2 million live in Peninsular Malaysia, 1.2 million in Sarawak,
and 0.9 million in Sabah. The ethnic composition of the population is approx-
imately 47% Malay, 34% Chinese, and 9% Indian, with the remainder chiefly
consisting of groups indigenous to Sabah and Sarawak (Dayaks, Kadazans,
Ibans, Bajaus, and others) and a small Eurasian com-aunity. While almost all
padi farmers in Peninsular Malaysia are Malays, in Sarawak and Sabah they are
members of indigenous tribal groups.
2.02      With a per capita income of US$720 in 1975, Malaysia is one of the
most prosperous countries in Asia. The economy has maintained fairly high
rates of growth, averaging 6% per annum during the last decade. This trend
slowed down to 1% growth in 1975, due to softening of international commodity
prices following the boom years of 1973 and 1974 when commodity prices and Gross
National Product (GNP) growth peaked. The estimated GNP growth in 1976 again
rose above trend to nearly 9%, and Malaysia's external reserves (US$2.3 billion
in SepteDn½er 1976) rose to a level equal to about nine months of net imports at
current levels.



-2-
2.03      The economy is export oriented.  In 1973, exports accounted for
almost 50% of GNP, principally rubber (US$1,045 million), tin (US$366 million),
timber (US$630 million) and palm oil (US$190 million).
Agricultural Sector
2.04      The agricultural sector holds a pre-eminent position in the Malaysian
economy. In 1974 it generated around 30% of Gross Domestic Product, provided
employment for about 50% of the economically active population and accounted
for upwards of 60% of Malaysia's foreign exchange earnings. Expansion of
rubber exports in the 1960s in the face of declining prices was achieved by
large gains in productivity. At the same time, diversification to oil palm was
vigorously encouraged and now accounts for 10% of the country's export earnings.
Malaysia has become the world's largest producer of palm oil. Rice production
has been increased through the provision of irrigation, high-yielding varieties
(HYVs) and double cropping, and Malaysia is now nearing self-sufficiency in
rice. Malaysia is one of the world's major producers of pineapples, though
it is finding it increasingly difficult to maintain its share of the export
market. Cocoa and pepper are also important export crops. Other major crops,
such as coconut, coffee, fruits and vegetables, are grown mainly for domestic
consumption.
Area Cultivated
2.05      Area Cultivated and Principal Crops in Malaysia
(a)  Of the 8 million cultivated acres /1 in Peninsular Malaysia in
1975, rubber occupied 4,237,000 acres, oil palm 1,280,000 acres,
coconut 528,000 acres. More than 6 million acres was under tree
crops. Of the remaining acres, 1,000,000 was under padi
(about 40% of it double cropped). Other significant crop acreages
are pineapple 63,000, cocoa 63,000, tapioca 32,000, sugar 25,000,
tobacco 20,000, coffee 22,000, groundnuts 15,000, and smaller areas
of miscellaneous crops.
(b)  Of the 750,000 acres cultivated in Sabah rubber accounts for
260,000 acres, oil palm 135,000 acres, coconut 130,000 acres, padi
128,000 acres and horticultural crops 82,000 acres. Tree crops
account for all of the agricultural exports and the state imports
about 40% of its food requirements.
(c)  Of approximately one million acres cultivated in Sarawak about
50% is devoted to tree crops (primarily rubber and coconut) and
there are 270,000 acres of padi (lowland and upland). Mis-
cellaneous crops include pepper, cocoa, maize and horticultural
crops.
/1 With double cropping and intercropping, about 9 million acres would be
cropped annually.



2.06      Large estates in the private sector account for about half the pro-
duction of rubber and 40% of the production of oil palm, but the smallholder
proportion is increasing, especially in oil palm, where government policy
strongly favors building up the smallholder sector. There are also some
large coconut estates and two or three large pineapple estates. Cocoa is
rapidly becoming an important intercrop with coconuts.
The Target Groups
2.07      The dissatisfaction with the distribution of benefits of economic
growth and with growing ethnic income inequalities provides the rationale
of the Government's New Economic Policy. The major aims of this policy, as
expressed in the Second Malaysia Plan (SMP) (1971-75), are: (a) to reduce
poverty by raising income levels and increasing employment opportunities for all
Malaysians, irrespective of race; and (b) to accelerate the process of restruc-
turing Malaysian society to correct economic imbalances so as to reduce, and
eventually eliminate, the identification of race with economic function. The
agricultural population includes some of the most economically depressed seg-
ments of the population and one of the results of the policy is to increase
the attention-given to raising rural incomes, especially those of smallholders.
Therefore, the proposed project would focus on strengthening and expanding
state agricultural services to the approximately one million rural families
that have not directly benefited from other development projects (paras. 2.08
and 2.09). The target groups for extension would include about 750,000 rural
families, mostly padi, coconut and other rainfed farm smallholders in Peninsular
Malaysia; 110,000 rural families in Sabah; 150,000 rural families in Sarawak;
and an undetermined number of farmers who practice shifting cultivation in
Sabah and Sarawak. The report "Problems of Rural Poverty in Malaysia" prepared
by the FAO/IBRD Cooperative Program, provides detailed information on rural
poverty in Peninsular Malaysia. These rural families make up more than 80%
of those served by the DOA (see Annex 1, paras. 3-5).
2.08      The major thrust of the Government's agricultural policy since the
early 1960s has been to develop new land for settlement of landless laborers
and marginal farmers mainly on rubber and oil palm smallholdings. These pro-
grams have been carried out mainly by the Federal Land Development Authority
(FELDA) and Bank finance has assisted five settlement schemes: Jengka I
(Loan 533-MA); Jengka II (Loan 672-MA); Jengka III (Loan 855-MA); Johore
Tenggara (Loan 967-MA); and Keratong (Loan 1044-MA).
2.09      Next to land development and settlement, government investment stra-
tegy has emphasized large-scale irrigation facilities to boost rice production
and incomes of padi smallholders. The two largest schemes have been assisted
by Bank loans: the Muda Irrigation Project (Loan 434-MA) and the Kemubu
Irrigation Project (Loan 500-MA). Although these major irrigation schemes have
been successful in introducing double cropping and greatly increasing yields,
there are few further opportunities for large-scale irrigation schemes.
2.10      With the formation of a semi-autonomous Malaysian Agricultural
Research and Development Institute (MARDI) in 1969, Malaysia embarked on a
systematic program to improve agricultural technology. The Bank is financing
the strengthening and expansion of MARDI's research programs for crops and
livestock under the Research and Extension Project (Loan 1115-MA). As a



- 4 -
follow-up to strengthening research and extension at the federal level, GOM
took the first step to strengthen extension and seed production at the state
level under the North Kelantan Rural Development Project (Loan 1294-MA). The
proposed national extension project would strengthen and expand the existing
agricultural services, extension, training, and quality seeds production in
all states of Malaysia with the objective of increasing agricultural production
and rural income (see Section 6).
2.11      The focus of the proposed project would be on those areas and rural
families that have not previously benefited from project-specific extension.
The beneficiaries would primarily be padi and coconut growers, many of whom
practice mixed farming; it would also include smallholder rubber growers who
practice mixed farming. Padi farmers in particular often grow vegetables and
groundnuts either in rotation or in the same seasonal period as their primary
crop. Livestock and cocoa are becoming increasingly important with coconut
growers. The SMP and TMP have emphasized crop diversification (rotation) and
intercropping as a means of increasing the income of small farmers, though as
the FAO/IBRD CP poverty report indicates, both are severely handicapped by
inadequate extension services. This project would aim to overcome this
constraint and assist more farmers to increase production and income from
primary as well as secondary crops.
Extension Services in Malaysia
2.12      Agricultural services, including extension, are state responsi-
bilities under the Malaysian constitution. In Peninsular Malaysia, the
Federal Government establishes agricultural policy and provides managerial,
technical and financial support to each of the states. The State Directors
of Agriculture and senior agricultural service officers are federal employees
under the administrative control of the states. Federal Government involvement
in management and its financial support provide sufficient control and influence
for planning and implementation of developmental activities. The intermediate
and operational agricultural staff are state employees. This arrangement
provides essential coordination and integration of the state function of
extension and the federal functions of agricultural policy, subject matter
support, research, training and education. At the same time authority and
responsibility for day-to-day activities remain at the local level and pro-
grams are implemented by staff who are familiar with local customs, problems
and procedures (details are contained in Annexes 1, 2 and 3). In the context
of the present project, the most important areas of institutional autonomy in
Peninsular Malaysia are: (a) all responsibility for rubber smallholder devel-
opment is delegated to the Rubber Industry Smallholders Development Authority
(RISDA); (b) input supply and marketing at the farm level are for the most part
handled by the private commercial sector, but are also carried out by the
Farmers' Organization Authority (FOA) (basically an input supply and marketing
cooperative organization); (c) the DOA is responsible for extension services;
and (d) research is the responsibility of MARDI, which is semi-autonomous.
2.13      In Sabah and Sarawak the organizational and functional structure of
the State Departments of Agriculture (SDOAs) is different and separate from
the Federal DOA. All staff, including the State Director of Agriculture, are
MOA state employees responsible only to the State Ministry of Agriculture.



- 5 -
Furthermore, such activities as research, farm cooperatives (FAs) and agri-
cultural education and training are the responsibility of the SDOA. The Sabah
Padi Board is responsible for input supply and marketing for irrigated padi
production and the Sabah Rubber Fund Board (SRFB) is responsible for all
rubber production and agricultural services including extension. However,
there is close cooperation between the SDOA extension service and the SRFB in
areas where rubber growers practice mixed farming. These organizational and
functional differences in East Malaysia broaden the scope of the project and
would facilitate the efficient implementation of the extension component of
the project.
2.14      The quantity and quality of extension services (farm, home and
community) vary from state to state throughout Malaysia depending on their
resources and stage of agricultural development. The state extension services
have one thing in common, however, an inadequate number of trained and equipped
field level extension officers to advise and assist the rural population.
Extension workers' educational functions are further constrained by the fact
that they are involved in regulatory functions and administration of subsidy
schemes. They also play an important role in ensuring fertilizer, seeds and
planting materials are available to many farmers. The project would aim to
strengthen and expand extension services in all states in the context of the
existing federal and state organizations, with major emphasis on the least
developed and poorer states (Annex 1, Tables 1 and 2).
2.15      The following weaknesses of the extension services have been
identified.
(a) There are too few staff engaged in extension at the farm level. The
present coverage of one extension agricultural technician (AT) to
1,300 farm families means that regular contact can only be maintained
with a small proportion of farmers.
(b)  The ATs receive insufficient training and technical updating to
enable them to offer adequate advice.
(c) In upgrading the scientific base of the extension service more
subject matter specialists (SMSs) are required to backstop extension
workers.
(d)  There is a general lack of planning, coordination and evaluation of
extension work. The supervisory staff, Agricultural Officers (AOs)
and Agricultural Assistants (AAs) do not receive any training in
personnel man&gcment or project planning.
(e)  ATs perform a number of statutory and regulatory duties in addition
to extension work.
(f) The present chains of command within the extension service are unclear.
The SDOA has no direct control over the SMSs who consequently do not
always give the necessary priority to extension work.
(g) Although SDOAs have ultimate responsibility for extension, several
states do not have a State Chief Extension Officer.



-6-
(h) There is still some duplication of effort among agencies carrying
out extension work (but this is being corrected).
(i) Research and extension still tend to operate in isolation but steps
to facilitate a closer working relationship are being developed
(paras. 2.19-2.21).
(j) The practice of applying rigid criteria, based on levels of academic
qualification, for appointment to different grades is a constraint
to the efficient deployment of agricultural staff.
Agricultural Subsidies
2.16      Agricultural input subsidies are a regular part of extension in
Malaysia (US$6.0 million under the SMP and more than US$23.8 million under the TMP
in Peninsular Malaysia alone). The principal objectives of the subsidy
schemes are: (a) to introduce farmers to new crops; (b) to demonstrate to
participants (and their neighbors) the benefits of improved agricultural
practices; (c) to assume some of the risk of innovation when farmers cannot be
expected to do so; and (d) to grant initial working capital to farmers who
have no opportunity for savings during the maturation period of investments.
Subsidies are primarily provided in the form of inputs (some free, and others
at reduced cost to the farmer) and consist mainly of seeds, planting materials,
fertilizers, insecticides and pesticides. The extension service is primarily
responsible for administration and implementation of most agricultural subsi-
dies, and although they do have a positive demonstration effect and serve as
an incentive to poorer and less progressive farmers to adopt new technology
and accept extension advice, in terms of time required for implementation,
subsidy programs detract from the extension workers' primary education function.
Moreover, subsidy schemes are being increased while there is a lack of informa-
tion on their benefits. The proposed project would establish an extension and
subsidy monitoring and evaluation program to assess achievement and provide
guidelines for future subsidy programs (see paras. 3.22-3.25).
Research
2.17      Agricultural research is carried out by MARDI, whose program is
assisted by Loan 1115-I5A. While MARDI is a semi-autonomous institution under
the federal 2IOA, the Sabah and Sarawak Agricultural Research Stations operate
as divisions of the respective state MOAs.
2.18      In compliance with the objectives of the TMP, MARDI has implemented a
"Development Oriented Research Program" providing greater emphasis to local
and regional research and on-farm trials and demonstrations. These activities,
normally considered an extension function, would supplement extension demonstra-
tion and at the same time make research more responsive to farmers' immediate
problems.
2.19      The extension and seeds components of the proposed project complement
the Agricultural Research and Extension Project (Loan 1115-M1A), which focuses
on strengthening crop research related to smallholder farming, i.e. rice, field
crops (groundnuts, maize, soybeans, cassava, etc.), coconut/cocoa and livestock.



- 7 -
Thus the research organization that Loan 1115-MA is assisting would provide
agricultural information as well as foundation seeds for existing and new
varieties of rice and field crops. Additional information on agricultural
research in Malaysia is contained in the appraisal report for Loan 1115-MA,
and in Annexes 1, 2, 3 and 4 of this report.
2.20      The Extension Liaison Unit (ELU), established under Loan 1115-MA as
an integral part of the Federal Extension Branch of the DOA, would be the main
channel of communication between MARDI, DOA and the SDOAs. An additional link,
the State Agricultural Research Development and Extension Committee (SARDEC),
has already been established in some states to bring local problems to the
attention of MARDI.
2.21      In view of the existing gap between known technology and that being
applied by most farmers, MARDI's research activities are already adequate to
support the extension and seeds component of this project. Additional measures
are being implemented to strengthen the support and coordination of research
and extension.  In particular the DOA and MARDI are organizing SARDECs to
coordinate activities between research and other agricultural services and
advise MARDI on state agricultural problems.
Training
2.22      Agricultural training is relatively advanced in Malaysia.  Profes-
sional training for degrees is offered at the Faculty of Agriculture of the
University of Malaysia in Kuala Lumpur and at the University Pertanian Malaysia
(UPM) at Serdang. Agricultural field staff receive pre-service operative-level
(vocational) training at six AIs which are well distributed throughout Peninsular
Malaysia. The ATs graduating from these institutes find employment principally
in government agencies such as DOA, RISDA, MARDI, FELDA and FOA. While ATs
receive a sound basic training, designed for a wide range of job careers, they
do not receive training specifically for extension. Furthermore, regularized
training programs and facilities are not available for in-service training of
extension or other agricultural service specialists.
2.23      The total residential capacity of the six Peninsular AIs is 1,680
but the institutes are only operating at 50% of capacity. A shortage of
scholarship funds has contributed to the inability to utilize the full capa-
city of the AIs. The Peninsular Malaysia extension component would require
400 additional ATs or about 15% of the projected output of the Peninsular AIs
during the project period. Funds for scholarships would be provided by the
Federal Government in order to ensure that an adequate number of trained
operatives (ATs) will be available for extension under the project. GOM is
taking action to strengthen the AIs and bring enrollment up to capacity
(Annex 5, para. 16).
2.24      Regularized in-service training programs to upgrade and update
agricultural service staff are urgently needed to improve and maintain the
technical knowledge of all field extension staff. Training programs and
facilities are needed to improve knowledge of extension techniques, communica-
tions and operational skills and farm management methods. The proposed



- 8 -
project would provide for this training through the establishment of four
RETDCs in Peninsular Malaysia and one RETDC facility in Sarawak. In Sabah,
additional RETDC facilities would be provided jointly with AVSs for in-service
training. Training of supervisory staff and SMSs is considered to be equally
important and therefore the project would provide for periodic courses in admin-
istration and personnel management as well as fellowships for overseas training.
2.25      The Education Television Program (financed under Loans 599-MA and
810-MA) has now established television receivers in almost every school in
Malaysia. While the primary function of these sets is to support formal
school education, they offer, at a minimum cost, a means of testing the use of
television in support of agricultural extension programs. In Malaysia, where
road communications provide a major constraint to extension work in many parts
of the country, the use of television may have very significant potential.
A pilot study would be conducted under the project to develop and test the use
of television for agricultural extension work, including in-service training
of field staff (Annex 5).
2.26      Programs and facilities for pre-service and in-service training
requirements, and for improving the quality of training programs, are discussed
in Annex 5.
Seeds Industry
2.27      Seed production is the responsibility of the Branch of Crop Produc-
tion in the DOA in Peninsular Malaysia. Most of the seed produced in Malaysia
is produced by five Crop Production Centers (CPCs) of this branch. In Sabah,
seed production is virtually restricted to padi, and is only about 15 tons per
year, produced by the SDOA and the Padi Board. In Sarawak, less than 40 tons
of padi seed are produced by the SDOA.
2.28      Present controlled padi seed production in all Malaysia is less
than 800 tons of improved seeds which would only plant about 5% of the total
annual padi crop area. In order to maintain varietal purity and sustain
yields farmers would on average purchase new seeds about once in five seasons;
progressive farmers would purchase new seeds more frequently. Raising padi
production to an acceptable standard would require at least a 300% increase in
padi seed production coupled with the normal farmer exchange of seeds. Con-
trolled production of minor crop seeds (maize, groundnuts, sorghum and soy-
beans) is negligible in Peninsular Malaysia, Sabah and Sarawak. Introduc-
tion of improved seeds would be one of the major tasks of the extension
service under the project.
2.29      There has been no effective seed certification and very limited
quality control. Testing facilities at the CPCs allow only simple checks on
germination and viability. The main constraints to improved quality are
inadequate processing and storage facilities, and a shortage of trained staff
(Annex 4).



- 9 -
3. THE PROJECT
Project Summary
3.01      The proposed project is designed to expand and strengthen the
extension services of the SDOAs throughout Malaysia and to develop and improve
seed production and quality control facilities. The project would consist of:
(a) Recruitment. Recruitment of about 80 senior staff (including
38 SMSs), about 580 ATs and about 400 support staff.
(b) Training
(i)   expanding and improving pre-service agricultural training
in Sabah and Sarawak;
(ii)   in-service training of extension staff; and
(iii)   provision of about 166 man-years of fellowships.
(c) Extension Methodology
(i)   improvement of agricultural extension methodology, including
implementation of regular programs of training and farm visits; and
(ii)   investigation of the use of television for extension purposes.
(d)  Seeds.  Expansion of seed production, improvement of seed quality
control and establishment of a seed fund for the purchase of premium
quality seeds from farmers.
(e)  Technical Assistance.  Provision of about 8.5 man-years of tech-
nical assistance for extension (3 man-years), seeds (2 man-years),
soil conservation (1 man-year), credit (0.5 man-year) and farm
mechanization (2 man-years).
(f)  Civil Works and Equipment.  The civil works and equipment under the
project would consist of construction of new buildings and expansion
and improvement of existing facilities, together with the supply of
related equipment, materials and vehicles. Details are as follows:
(i)   establishment of five RETDCs (four in Peninsular Malaysia
and one in Sarawak), one AI in Sarawak, and one Agricultural
Training Center (ATC) in Sabah and improvement of one AVS in
Sabah;
(ii)   establishment of about 40 ESCs in Peninsular Malaysia and
Sarawak, about 20 AFBs (village level extension posts) in
Sarawak, facilities for about 9 FAs and extension staff
housing and offices in Sabah;
(iii)   establishment of three CPCs (for seeds production), two in
Sabah and one in Sarawak, and expansion of five existing CPCs
in Peninsular Malaysia;



- 10 -
(iv)   establishment of one central seed quality control
laboratory in Peninsular Malaysia; and
(v)   establishment of one extension communications media
unit in Sarawak.
Project Formulation and Objectives
3.02      The existing federal and state DOA Extension Services are responsible
for all extension activities for all crops except rubber, which is legally
the responsibility of RISDA. The proposed national extension project would
aim to strengthen and expand the individual states' ability and capacity to
carry out this responsibility. The project would benefit about 80% of all
rural families, i.e. those which are not serviced by specialist extension
services (such as those of Muda Agricultural Development Authority, MADA, and
of Kemubu Agricultural Development Authority, KADA, for irrigation and FELDA
for land settlement) and part of the state of (Kelantan) services already
receiving assistance under other projects. The extension services in the
irrigation command areas and land settlement schemes, functionally the
responsibility of the SDOAs, are effective. Their effectiveness has been
achieved by the same means as proposed for the project, namely, (a) improved
management, (b) increased number of field level extension staff, (c) better
trained field staff, (d) more and better equipment and facilities, and (e)
regularized extension methodology. The proposed project basically follows
the pattern of the extension component of the North Kelantan Rural Development
Project (Loan 1294-MA). The only major difference are that the national
extension project would in addition provide for in-service training and seeds
production on a national basis. The basic aim of the project would be to
provide appropriate packages of technology (Annex 1, para. 10) to reduce the
gap between known technology and that now applied by the majority of smallholders.
It would focus especially on smallholders in poverty areas not yet benefiting
from project-specific extension upgrading.
3.03      The proposed project would be implemented by the DOAs under the
three Ministries of Agriculture in Peninsular Malaysia, Sabah and Sarawak.
The differences in project composition and in individual components, which are
described below, reflect the differences in agricultural development and
administration of the three regions as well as the varying scope of responsi-
bility of the three ministries. In Sabah and Sarawak, the relatively undevel-
oped state of agriculture and supporting infrastructure, as well as the more
comprehensive responsibility of their DOAs, requires a wider range of components
and a different emphasis than in Peninsular Malaysia.
Detailed Features
Extension (Overall Proposals)
3.04      The proposed project would build each SDOA into an effective institu-
tion to provide adequate extension services to smallholders and to ensure that
research results are translated into farm benefits. Total cost of this component
would be US$25.7 million.



3.05      The national average ratio of field level ATs (extension workers) to
farm families would be increased from 1:1,300 to 1:800. Field level extension
staff would be increased from 495 to 895 in Peninsular Malaysia, from 113
to 213 in Sabah, and from 171 to 248 in Sarawak. A total of 38 additional SMSs
would provide technical backstopping for the ATs. Housing and office facilities
would be constructed, including 32 ESCs in Peninsular Malaysian states; 10 ESCs,
20 AFBs (satellites of the ESCs) in Sarawak; and 9 FA facilities in Sabah. The
SDOA minor development schemes (Annex 2, page 5) also form nuclei for extension
in Sabah. Total staff requirements are detailed in Section G of the separately
bound staff supplementary tables to Annex 6.
3.06      The ESCs and AFB satellites would be located in remote areas where
agricultural services are inadequate and would serve as forward bases for
extension workers. The project would contribute vehicles, boats and motors
to make extension workers mobile and increase their coverage even to those
areas inaccessible by road. Appropriate extension methodology would be
applied and regularized (Annex 1). The additional ATs provided under the
project would be utilized to a large extent to release extension workers from
subsidy and regulatory functions and provide a substantial number of ATs who
would have primarily educational functions. In Sabah, the five existing and
nine new FAs would serve as a base for extension but would also provide input
and marketing services. Details are in Annex 2. Extension is an activity that
depends upon adequate numbers of trained and informed staff and satisfactory
assurances were obtained during negotiations on the creation and financing of
new positions and recruitment of staff to fill them.
Extension Training Details
3.07      Training under the proposed project (costing a total of US$18.4 mil-
lion) would strengthen the technical and operational capability of the extension
services by the means set out below. In addition, the increasing emphasis to
be given to training activities within DOA will be supported by the training
of staff of the ELU under Loan 1115-MA which will liaise with the DOA Extension
Branch.
(a) A new Al would be established in Sarawak. Facilities at the AVS in
Sabah would be improved and output of existing institutes in the
Peninsula would be increased to enable total DOA operative level
staff to be increased by 660 during the project. The quality of
training at the institutes would be improved by the training of
teaching staff and by closer integration of the institutes into
field extension programs.
(b) In-service upgrading and specialized training of field staff would
be achieved by the establishment of five new RETDCs (four in the
Peninsula and one in Sarawak), and the establishment of an ATC
in Sabah to perform the function of an RETDC. The total RETDC annual
capacity would be increased to the equivalent of about 2,500 courses
of three weeks per trainee, including short seminars and workshops.
Approximately 2,000 DOA agricultural technicians and 250 subprofessional
staff would receive training at the RETDCs in the form of annual train-
ing sessions and specialized courses. Selected suboperative staff

Extension MethodoloRv
3.09      The widely varyinR nature of aRriculture throughout the country
requires a flexible and practical extension aDDroach adiusted to the level of
develoDment of local aRriculture and suDDortinR services (credit, marketinR.
inDut suDply. etc.). The main emphasis of the DroDosed Droject would be on
Dre-service and in-service training of extension workers so that they will be
adeauatelv informed. Armed with information, the extension workers would
concentrate on counselinR individuals and RrouDs of farmers on aDDroDriate
inDuts and farming techniaues. Extension officers are Dresentlv involved in
reRulatorv activities and inDut suDDlies as much as in educational activities.
One of the maior Roals of the project would be to systematically separate
extension from reRulatory and inDut functions as raDidly as Dracticable.



- 13 -
3.10      The basis of the proposed project extension methodology would be
systematic planning and application of extension activities at the farm level
(training, visits and demonstrations), beginning with the day-to-day work of
the field level extension worker up through the area, district, circle, and
state levels of organization. The AT would plan his program on a two to four
week schedule, depending on local requirements, and would plan regular training,
demonstration (based on a package input approach) and discussion visits to
provide timely agricultural information relevant to the most important crops
and activities in his area at specific times of the year. He would conduct
regularly scheduled visits, discussions and demonstrations with farm leaders
and other farmers and maintain records of visits, information transmitted,
acceptance by farmers and their achievements. These reports would form part
of a cumulative record of activities and achievements from the area, district,
circle and finally the state level which would then form the basis for long-
term systematic monitoring, evaluation and planning. Farm models and indicative
inputs and yields for padi and coconut under the project are given in Annex 7.
3.11      In order to be efficient and effective, extension must be planned,
programmed and evaluated. This would be done at three levels:
(a) State Level - the State Director of Agriculture and State Chief
Extension Officer would establish agricultural production priorities,
identify the important crops and elements within those that would be
improved, and formulate the program for extension.
to) Circle Level - the agricultural officers in charge of circles will
have been involved in state level planning and would transmit
priorities and targets to the agricultural assistants in charge of
districts making up a circle. Previous experience in the districts
and farmer acceptance or rejection of innovation would be reviewed,
and as a result of these meetings and discussions an annual program
for the circle would be prepared.
(c) District and Area Level - detailed annual promotion and development
programs for each area in the district would be worked out by the
AAs and ATs for each area in terms of time and resources required.
Each AT would establish a regular and detailed information and visit
program so that individuals and groups of farmers would know his
movements and activities. At least half of his time would be
devoted to promotion and development, including demonstrations, and
the remainder would be utilized for consulting work and handling
seasonal crop crises. The AT would also schedule time for regular
reporting, meetings with his supervisor, and for training (see
Annexes 1, 2 and 3).
3.12      The study of the use of television for extension purposes (para. 2.25)
would open up a potentially very valuable communications medium for improving
agriculture in Malaysia. Assurances were obtained during negotiations that
the DOA, in cooperation with the Ministry of Agriculture and Radio T.V.
Malaysia, would conduct the study under TOR reviewed by the Bank and would
submit the study findings to the Bank for review by December 31, 1978, or
such later date as may be agreed.



- 14 -
The Seeds Component
3.13      This component of the project (total cost US$4.2 million) would
provide for facilities and capability for producing quality seeds of padi and
minor crops (Annex 4, para. 40 onwards). In Peninsular Malaysia five CPCs
(now producing 800 tons of seeds annually) would be expanded and strengthened
to produce, process and distribute approximately 2,500 tons of seed annually
by full development. Seed quality control would be developed through a
central seed testing and quality control laboratory to be established at
Serdang (in Peninsular Malaysia). In Sabah, two seed production, processing
and distribution centers would be developed, with the capacity to produce
annually 200 tons of padi seed and 150 tons of minor crop seeds (mainly
groundnuts, maize, sorghum and soybeans). In Sarawak, one seed production
center would be established with the capacity to process annually about
250 tons of padi seed. Production of up to 50 tons of minor crop seeds would
also be developed. The production centers would become the major suppliers
of domestic seed requirements.
3.14      Initial quality seeds production would be accomplished through
improvement of existing government seed farms (CPCs). Quality seed production
would progressively be extended to farmer outgrowers with 50% or more of the
seed being grown by farmers by full development (Annex 4, para. 80). A seeds
fund would be established and maintained by the DOA to provide adequate opera-
ting capital to purchase seed at a premium price from farmer growers and
cover operating deficits. Assurances to this effect were obtained during
negotiations.
3.15      MARDI would supply foundation seed to the seed production centers.
The respective research institutes of Sabah and Sarawak would also produce some
foundation seed for local requirements. Seeds would be multiplied by the CPCs
and farmer outgrowers. Seeds would be sold at cost to the more progressive
farmers with adequate resources. However, initial emphasis would be on distri-
bution through subsidy programs, administered by the SDOA, to introduce and
demonstrate the benefits of improved seeds to small farmers and farmers with
inadequate resources and incentive to purchase seeds. With a view to
establishing seed production on a self-financing basis, assurances were
obtained during negotiations that the GOM's pricing policy for seeds would be
reviewed and that the Bank would be advised of the GOM seed pricing proposals
by January 31, 1980 (Annex 4, paras. 92 and 93). Seeds pricing policy is
closely linked to GOM's subsidy program and policy to be reviewed under the
project in the same period (para. 3.25). Also, assurances were obtained
during negotiations that during the project implementation period, the Bank
would be given reasonable prior notice of the Government's intention to
enact any federal or state legislation concerning seed production. The
Malaysian seeds industry is in the initial stages of development, with the
GOM as the only producer of improved seeds at present; therefore, it is
considered that legislation at this time would be premature and would serve
little useful purpose (see Annex 4, para. 39).
Technical Assistance
3.16      The Federal and State Governments have requested technical assistance
(foreign exchange cost US$0.74 million) for the extension, training and seeds



- 15 -
components of the project to assist with specialized activities in relation
to planning, organization, design and implementation as described below.
Assurances were obtained during negotiations that this technical assistance
would be acquired under terms and conditions satisfactory to the Bank.
3.17      Extension (3.0 man-years).  There is a requirement for assistance
in extension organization and planning in the early stages of the project to
deal with such matters as: (a) formulating programs of extension work;
(b) determining in-service training course content and drawing up training
programs; (c) planning the establishment and operation of training facilities;
and (d) organization and management of extension activities at the State and
District level (Annex 1, pages 12 and 13).
3.18      Seeds (2.0 man-years).  The DOA has limited experience in the tech-
nical aspects of the seeds industry and therefore assistance would be
provided for the planning and design of seeds processing facilities (Annex 4,
para. 70).
3.19      Soil Conservation (1.0 man-years).  The problems of soil conservation
have largely been ignored as an extension activity in the past. The project
would provide technical assistance to train staff, develop soil conservation
programs and implement a pilot soil conservation scheme (Annex 1, para. 51).
3.20      Credit (0.5 man-years).  There are no effective institutionalized
credit organizations in Sarawak and most agricultural credit is obtained from
local merchants to whom farmers sell their produce and from whom they buy
their supplies. The subsidy schemes do not and should not take the place of
necessary available credit arrangements. Improved credit systems are required
for an advance in agricultural development. A consultant, for six months,
would be provided to assess credit problems and make recommendations for
remedying the situation (Annex 3, para. 24).
3.21      Farm Mechanization Extension (2 man-years).  In Sabah there is a
great need for assistance to FAs, the Padi Board and other agencies to keep
agricultural machinery operable. Local skills in farm machinery operation and
maintenance are, at best, minimal or totally lacking. Relatively new tractors
in FAs lie idle because of improper maintenance or lack of simple repair. A
consultant (an extension and training specialist in farm machinery) would
advise the Sabah DOA, the Padi Board and farmers on more efficient machine
utilization. He would also assist with the training of ATs in basic mechaniza-
tion skills at the Al and RETDC.
Monitoring and Evaluation
3.22      The success of the DOAs' extension activities under the proposed
project will ultimately be measured by the increased productivity of small-
holders in Malaysia. However, there is inadequate information regarding
present agricultural practices, achievements of subsidy programs, farmers'
attitudes, and the constraints that keep smallholders from increasing their
productivity. Therefore, there is a need at this time to establish a moni-
toring and evaluation capability within the DOA for the implementation of the
project in the short term and assessing the benefits of extension and subsidies
ii the long term.



- 16 -
3.23      Monitoring would require a systematic framework of data collec-
tion, analysis, and periodic reporting. Consultants employed under the
UNDP/IBRD State and Rural Development Project would assist the federal and
state DOAs in the development of an appropriate system. Terms of reference
to this effect which have been agreed with the Bank are included in the
State and Rural Development Project. A computer specialist and statistician-
economist with experience in conducting agro-economic surveys would also be
available as part of the computer activity technical assistance for the DOA
and MARDI, financed under Loan 1115-MA. The monitoring and evaluation studies
would examine: (a) present agricultural practices of farmers (including
the use of improved seeds, fertilizers and pesticides); (b) credit mechanisms
available to the farmers and their use; (c) marketing channels available to
farmers, particularly for the new crops that are encouraged under crop-
diversification schemes; and (d) seed pricing policies.
3.24      A comprehensive review of subsidy schemes by the DOA would result in
operational and policy guidelines regulating the subsidy programs, and modi-
fying them if necessary to bring them into line with their objectives. Almost
any crop now qualifies for a subsidy. Their respective justifications and
impact of subsidies will be found to be different, and policy recommendations
emerging from this review are likely to differ from crop to crop and from area
to area.
..25      Assurances were obtained during negotiations that by January 31, 1980
the initial findings and recommendations of these studies would be submitted
to the Bank for review and comment.
4. ORGANIZATION AND MANAGEM ENT
Federal and State Government Extension Relationships
4.01      Extension is a state function carried out under the State Director
of Agriculture (a federal officer). Federal Extension, a branch under the
Director General for Agriculture, provides a subject matter advisory service,
and financial and. material assistance to the states (see Charts WB17603
and WB17602). The Director of the Extension Branch, DOA, would be the National
Project Director responsible for project administration and implementation.
The State Directors of Agriculture in Sabah and Sarawak would be Assistant
Project Directors for their states. The National Project Director would be
responsible to the Director General of Agriculture. All SDOAs would be
involved in project planning and implementation and the State Directors of
Agriculture would assist the National Project Director and share responsibility
for execution and operation within their respective states. Appointment of
qualified staff to the positions of Project Director, and three Project
Leaders for RETDCs, Seeds and Soil Conservation would be a condition of
effectiveness of the proposed loan. Each state would have a Chief Extension
Officer responsible for day-to-day operational and administrative matters
associated with the project. Assurances were obtained during negotiations
that all states would designate their Chief Extension Officer as responsible
for day-to-day project operations by June 30, 1978, or such later date as
may be agreed. The main overall functions of the National Project Director
would be to:



- 17 -
(a) plan and coordinate the construction of physical facilities created
by the project;
(b) control and disburse project funds in accordance with GOM's estab-
lished procedures;
(c) procure the goods and services as required by the project;
(d) ensure that the staffing requirements of the RETDCs, the Sarawak
AI, and other training institutions are met;
(e) arrange for the phased recruitment of additional field extension
staff as envisaged by the project;
(f) ensure that the organization and work programs as proposed under the
project are implemented;
(g) administer and manage the fellowships program and arrange for recruit-
ment of foreign technical assistance;
(h) provide the main focus of liaison between the project and other DOA
branches, the ELU, MARDI, the FOA and other development agencies and
other agencies involved in credit,
input distribution and marketing; and
(i) maintain records of the progress of project planning and imple-
mentation.
4.02      Seeds.  The Director of the Crop Production Branch would be respon-
sible to the Director General of Agriculture through the National Project
Director for implementation of the seeds component of the project. The
Director General would establish a separate unit to be responsible for seed
quality. The National Seeds Assessment Committee would approve new varieties
for release, and MARDI would produce, maintain and supply breeder and founda-
tion seeds of approved varieties.
4.03      An Assistant Director for Seed Production, responsible to the
Director of the Crop Production Branch, as head of the Office of Seed Produc-
tion would be appointed. The Office of Seed Production would be divided into
three units each with a Senior Agricultural Officer in charge; these three
units would be as follows: (a) Seed Production; (b) Seed Processing, Storage
and Marketing; and (c) Seed Certification and Testing. The heads of the
first two units would be responsible to the Assistant Director, Office of
Seed Production, whereas the head of the third unit would be directly
responsible to the Director of the Crop Production Branch.
4.04      Training.  The RETDCs to be established under the project would be
the responsibility of the Director of the Extension Branch of the DOA.
Training of agricultural staff is a federal responsibility. An Assistant
Project Leader, Training, would be appointed to assist the Director of Extension
to administer the AIs and the RETDCs (para. 4.01). A working level committee
of all user agencies would be formed to provide policy guidelines to the
RETDCs and to coordinate the RETDC programs with the training courses offered
by the user agencies.



- 18 -
4.05      Soil Conservation.  Soil conservation would be a functional unit
of the Soils and Analytical Services Branch, and a Project Leader would be
appointed (para. 4.01).
Administrative and Functional Linkages
4.06      The proposed project has been designed to be implemented within
the existing organizational framework of the federal and state DOAs and line
functions of the DOA would not be affected by the project. The administration
of the proposed project would be carried out by the National Project Director,
who is also the Director of the Extension Branch in the DOA. The training
and seeds components of the project would be implemented by the Directors of
the Extension Branch and Crop Production Branch respectively, and each com-
ponent would be headed by a Project Leader (para. 4.01). These Project
Leaders would remain functionally responsible to their own Branch Directors,
but for project administration they would be responsible to the Project
Director (refer to organization charts WB17277 and WB17604).
4.07      This distinction is necessary because during implementation of the
project, the tasks of these units involve management of funds, supervision
of building construction, purchase of equipment, mobilizing and recruiting
personnel, and other administrative activities, which are quite different from
the technical task of operating the functional units.
Organization at the End of the Project Implementation Period
4.08      At the end of the disbursement period, the Federal Extension and
Advisory Services would consolidate the various units along more functional
lines since there would no longer be a need for separate staff or units to handle
the ELU and the state extension loans. The newly acquired expertise of the
staff and the extended infrastructural facilities would be deployed within the
framework of the present federal and state agricultural extension, crop produc-
tion, and training organizations at the end of the project implementation
period.
Implementation Schedule
4.09      The proposed project would be implemented over a period of five
years (Annex 6, Part I, Implementation Summary). The capacity to implement
this project varies among the 12 states, but with adequate coordination and
cooperation the federal DOA is confident that administrative constraints would
be manageable. The monitoring and evaluation system (para. 3.22) would assist
project implementation and would be extended to all states during the course
of the project.
4.10      The seeds component of the project would be implemented slowly
in the first two years to allow the seeds consultant time to finalize design,
production and processing requirements, with the objective of reaching full
development by 1982 or as soon thereafter as practicable. Implementation
details for the extension, training and seeds components for Peninsular
Malaysia, Sabah and Sarawak are contained in Annex 6.



- 19 -
Accounts and Audit
4.11      The DOA and the SDOAs are all subject to normal government financia.
control and audit procedures. Assurances were obtained during negotiations
that adequate accounts would be kept for all project expenditures through all
concerned agencies and specifically that:
(a) separate accounts would be kept and financial statements would be
prepared in respect of the project by the DOA;
(b) such accounts and financial statements for each fiscal year would
be audited, in accordance with appropriate auditing principles
consistently applied, by independent auditors acceptable to
the Bank;
(c) the following would be furnished to the Bank within nine months of
the end of each fiscal year:
(i)   certified copies of these audited financial
statements for each year; and
(ii)   the audit reports;
(d) any other information concerning these accounts which the Bank
reasonably requests would be provided; and
(e) that all CPCs, and FAs in Sabah, financed under the project, would
adopt cost accounting procedures acceptable to the Bank.
5. COSTS, FINANCING, PROCUREMENT AND DISBURSEMENTS
Project Costs
5.01      Total project costs are estimated at US$46.3 million, of which
US$19.0 million would be foreign exchange. All prices and salaries are
expressed at current levels. Physical contingencies of 15% for civil works
and 10% for vehicles and equipment are included, and amount to 5% of project
costs. Costs due to expected price increases amount to 24% of project costs,
and are calculated assuming the following inflation rates:
1976      1977-80        1981 and on
 _ __ __ __ __ __ (0) _____________
Domestic (Malaysia)          7          7                  7
Foreign exchange
Civil works               8           7.5               7
Vehicles/equipment       10           9                 8



- 20 -
5.02      Unit costs for civil works in Sabah and Sarawak are estimated to be
about 25% to 30% higher than in Peninsular Malaysia for similar items, reflecting
higher local contractor and transport costs.
5.03      Project costs for the seed subproject include an estimation of work-
ing capital (seeds fund) required to (a) purchase seeds from farmer outgrowers
and (b) cover operational deficits during the development phase of the seeds
industry. Working capital is calculated on the basis of the above costs less
revenues generated from seed sales. In Peninsular Malaysia existing and new
staff are federal employees, and their salaries are paid by the Federal
Government. In Sabah and Sarawak staff are state employees, and the cost of
staff salaries is borne by the state. Therefore staff salaries are included
as an operational expense in the working capital estimation for Sabah and
Sarawak, but they are not included in the same estimation for Peninsular
Malaysia. All staff salaries (federal and state) are included as an operational
expense in cash flow and financial analyses for the seed subproject. Assurances
were obtained during negotiations that GOM will create the posts and appoint
staff incrementally to conduct, support and maintain project activities.
5.04      Details of project costs, including the incremental build-up of
staff and operating costs in DOA and the SDOAs, are presented in Annex 6 and
are summarized below.
