Procurement
5.06      The Project Director, who is also the Director of Extension under
the Director General of the DOA, would have overall responsibility for the
procurement of goods and services under the project. The Project Director
would utilize and expand staff and facilities already established for the
ELU under Loan 1115-MA to carry out procurement and disbursement administration.
For the 10 states on the Peninsula, the National Project Director would be
directly in charge of procurement and approval of requests of the individual
SDOAs under the project.
5.07      Procurement of goods and works in Sabah and Sarawak would be carried
out by the individual states in accordance with procedures that are acceptable
to the Bank. Under the project the Project Directors (SDOAs) Sabah and
Sarawak would submit an annual budget, implementation program and procurement
schedule to the National Project Director and through him to the Bank.
5.08      Contracts for construction of civil works would generally be small
(averaging less than US$100,000), dispersed in time and place, and could not
be grouped for similtaneous tendering. All contracts estimated to cost less
than US$400,000 would be awarded following local procedures which are satis-
factory to the Bank; all contracts estimated to cost US$400,000 or more would
be carried out under international competitive bidding in accordance witli
Bank guidelines.  Vehicles and equipment would be procured under international
bidding in accordance with Bank guidelines. A 15% preference margin, or the
prevailing customs duty if lower, would be extended to local manufacturers.
Small off-the-shelf items, such as extension and office equipment, would be
procured through normal government procedures.



- 23 -
5.09      The Bank's prior approval would be required before invitations to
bid are issued and contracts are awarded for vehicles, equipment, and materials
purchases where contracts are estimated to be in excess of US$50,000 equivalent.
Disbursement
5.10      Disbursements would be made for 100% of expenditures for consultants'
services and for fellowships; and 100% of foreign expenditures for directly
imported equipment, materials and vehicles; 100% of ex-factory cost of items
manufactured locally; 85% of total cost of locally procured imported items; and
40% of total expenditures for civil works, including site development. Dis-
bursements by the Bank are expected to be completed by June 30, 1983. The
estimated quarterly disbursement schedule for the project is given in Annex 6,
Part II, Section G, Table 1.2.
6. BENEFITS AND JUSTIFICATIONS
Introduction
6.01      The proposed project would provide the infrastructure and extension
services necessary to increase the productivity and income of smallholder
farmers engaged in the production of padi, coconut, and other crops, throughout
Malaysia. An estimated one million rural families will be reached by the
increase in extension and seed production services, with particular emphasis
on increasing extension services in rural poverty areas. The cost of the
project's extension component would be US$26.0 per rural family. The project
is closely in line with the following objectives of the Malaysian Government:
(a) to improve the productivity and incomes of the
poorest segments of Malaysian society in general;
(b) to reduce urban-rural and ethnic (Bumiputra/non-Bumiputra) /1
income disparities; and
(c) to reduce the national dependence on imported foodgrains.
6.02      It is not practical to separate and quantify the influences of all
the subproject components on the total project benefits, except for financial
analysis of the seed production component. The benefits from the use of the
improved seeds to be produced under the project would not be fully effective
L1 Bumiputra refers to the indigenous peoples of Malaysia, including the
Malays and the indigenous Borneo people.



- 24 -
without appropriate extension services. For these reasons the project eco-
nomic benefits are treated as the result of a combined package of an increase
in extension, training and seed production services. Padi has been used as
the base crop for the economic analysis, but substantial benefits would also
be realized from coconut and minor crops. The impact of the extension project
would be far greater in irrigation command areas, including those areas to be
served by the Small-Scale Irrigation Project.
Financial Analysis (Annex 7, Section A)
6.03      However, the potential financial benefits of the project in terms of
increased production and farm incomes would be expected to adequately justify
the project financial cost (see para. 6.05). It is expected that the govern-
ment CPCs would become financially self-sustaining institutions at full devel-
opment (Year 5). For the seed subproject, the estimated financial rate of
return is 14%. For the Peninsula's CPCs the calculated rate of return is
26%, which shows the centers can become financially self-sustaining and
eliminate the need for present GOM subsidies. The following assumptions have
been made for this analysis:  (a) of total seed produced, distribution would
be for commercial sales to farmers and sales to GOM for subsidy schemes;
(b) fifteen percent of seed produced would account for reject and damage,
and will be sold on a private consumption basis. The distribution of final
production would be as follows:
1978   1979   1980   1981   1982 and on
 _______ ________  %  _
Commercial sales          35      45     55      65        75
Reject and damage         15      15      15     15        15
GOM subsidy               50      40      30     20        10
Total                  100    100    100    100         100
6.04      At present, there is no set pricing policy for the sale of seeds
(see Annex 4, para. 75). The financial analysis of the CPCs is intended to
develop a format by which the CPCs can become financially independent of
present GOM subsidies for seed production. Prices used are current seed market
.prices. In accordance with proposed GOM policy (Annex 4, para. 94), a 30%
premium has been added on to prices. The price premium is charged only for
seed sold on a commercial basis, from 1982 onwards. It is estimated that by
full development the CPCs will produce between 35 and 40% of total Malaysian
seed requirements (Annex 4, paras. 58, 61 and 62).
Economic Analysis (Annex 7, Section B)
6.05      In recent years Malaysia has imported between 150,000 and 200,000 tons
of rice per annum. The project will reduce this dependence on imported rice,
thus generating substantial foreign exchange savings. The improvement in the
efficiency of utilization of existing resources, which would be brought about



- 25 -
by the project, would generate a high rate of return. Padi production increases
necessary to generate a 25% economic rate of return have been calculated. It
has been assumed that the maximum production increase that the project will
have contributed will be reached in Year 10. Up to that year attributable pro-
duction increases are assumed to rise to peak at 20% of total in years 5 and 6
and then to decline to Year 10.
6.06      The analysis in Annex 7 (Section B, Table 1.1) shows that to realize
a 25% economic rate of return, annual incremental production value by Year 10,
at 1977 prices, would need to reach about M$44 M (US$17.5 M) above the base
period 1974-76. This represents an increase in production value of 8.4%, but
an increase of only 3.8% in production volume when the Bank's projected rise
in the real price of padi to 1988 is-discounted./l Experience elsewhere with
extension and training programs indicates that the project's impact is likely
to exceed the modest level required to achieve a 25% rate of return. Reversing
the conclusion of the analysis and stating it in more conventional form, it
would be reasonable to assume that with only a minor padi total production
increase (attributable to the project investment) of 3.8% by Year 10 the
project would realize an economic rate of return of 25%. This rate of return
would be achieved by the 3.8% increase in only padi production, and any
production increases in coconuts, maize, other crops and livestock would raise
the rate of return. There is little risk that these production increases
would not be attained, since the best farmers in Malaysia already obtain much
higher average yields and research yield levels are even higher. This rate of
return should be treated with caution, however, since as improved agricultural
practices spread, further investments in infrastructure and services would
become necessary.
Annual Incremental Extension Operating Costs
6.07      To cover annual incremental extension operating costs, only a small
increase in per acre padi production at current prices is required. In
Peninsular Malaysia, for example, the annual cost of the additional resources
required will be approximately US$3.6 million when the projected improvements are
fully implemented. This figure is derived from totaling the yearly staff and
operating costs, 10% of the building costs and 20% of the equipment costs.
When spread over the smallholder occupied area of 2 million acres it represents
an annual "charge" of US$1.80 per acre. Alternatively, it may be expressed as
US$5.20 per farm family when spread over the 750,000 smallholdings or US$3,970
per field extension worker, of which there will be around 900 when the new
staff are employed.
/1  Base period padi production in Malaysia averaged 2.0 million tons and
Bank projections translate to padi value increasing from M$260 (base
period average) to M$580/ton (in 1977 prices) over the ten-year benefit
build-up period.



- 26 -
6.08      The requirement to generate an additional US$1.80 net income per
acre from extension activities is a small target. It represents only 5 kg
of padi at current 1977 prices in Year 10 or similar small quantities of other
products. But it is not sufficient that extension should break even and it is
expected that returns would amply exceed costs.
Farm Incomes
6.09      The majority of Malaysian padi farmers have incomes below the national
absolute and relative poverty levels, chiefly because of the small (though
evenly distributed) sizes of padi farms./I Recent national surveys indicate
that 78% of all farm families primarily dependent on padi production have
incomes below the poverty line (US$162 per capita) in 1974, while the incomes
of operators of mixed farms are estimated to be much lower. Among such farmers
on double-cropped farms, 64% were estimated to live in poverty at the end of
1975,12 and eliminating the states of Kedah and Perlis (which include the
relatively prosperous Muda Project area), 78% of double-cropping specialist
farmers were below the poverty line. This figure still understates the propor-
tion of poor farmers in small irrigation areas. According to national surveys,
double-cropping farmers in states which are totally dependent on small-scale
irrigation were almost universally poor,/3 and the larger number of farmers
dependent on a single crop of padi received proportionally lower incomes.
6.10      MOA detailed socio-economic surveys of the Kerandang Timur /4 and
Kota II /5 scheme areas were carried out in October 1976. Average total
family income in Kerandang Timur is currently about US$390 (US$91 per capita).
Median farm family income is US$331 (US$77 per capita) and income is fairly
evenly distributed with no family surveyed receiving over US$1,150 (US$267 per
capita) from all sources. Ninety-five percent of Kerandang Timur families
/1 Ninety-three percent of all Malaysian padi farmers operate on less than
three ha of padi land.
/2 Problems of Rural Poverty in Malaysia, IBRD Report No. 838-MA, Annex E,
p. 16.
/3 Between 95% and 100% of double-cropping padi farmers in Johor, Pahang,
Malacca, Negri Sembilan, and Trenggami were living in poverty, according
to the surveys.
/4 Trengganu Scheme.
/5 Kedah Scheme.



- 27 -
earned total incomes below the national poverty line./l In Kota II, average
total farm family income is currently US$648 (US$122 per capita). Median
family income is US$504 (US$95 per capita) and income is again fairly evenly
distributed with the upper 10% of families receiving an average income of
US$1,902 (US$359 per capita).  Eighty-seven percent of Kota II families
earned total incomes below the national absolute poverty line./l
6.11      Over the period 1978 to 1987 the project would contribute to raising
net padi farm incomes of project farmers by US$80-100 per family (US$15-19 per
capita)./2 This increase in income is contingent upon the use of improved
seeds to be produced under the project, and on the use of associated inputs
such as fertilizer, and upon the increased and improved extension services
providing rural families with access to information about appropriate use of
the above inputs. Over the ten year period a few padi farmers would rise
above poverty income levels, but in general a 14 to 26% increase in income
over present levels would be achieved by the largest identifiable poverty
group in Malaysian society.
Risk
6.12      In calculating the economic returns for the proposed project invest-
ment, the various components were treated as an integrated package. Proper
implementation of the project will require a high degree of organizational
effectiveness and coordination between the federal and state DOAs, as well as
with other public institutions in Malaysia. In particular institutional
services such as credit and marketing will have to be progressively streng-
thened in order to obtain the full benefits of the project. Much of the
initial emphasis will be on raising the subsistence level of rural families.
The major risks involved would be delays in implementation (including untimely
availability of staff, funds and equipment); administrative ineffectiveness
and slow farmer acceptance.
6.13      The federal and state DOAs are well aware of the organizational,
management and administrative requirements of the project and are confident
that adequate coordination and cooperation can be achieved to overcome these
potential constraints. This confidence is justifiable.
/1 The current national per capita income is US$720 and the national per
capita absolute poverty line is estimated to be about US$210 in 1977.
Average family size is 4.3 members in Kerandang Timur and 5.3 members in
Kota II.
/2 The US$150 per family increase in income represents the national average.
For regional (Peninsular Malaysia, Sabah, and Sarawak) family and per
capita income increases, see the farm models in Annex 7, Section B.



- 28 -
Environmental Effects
6.14      It is unlikely that any components of the project would have a
significant adverse effect on the environment of the country. The use of
high yield seed varieties will require the application of chemical fertil-
izers and sprays for pest control, but the level of application in the
country will be relatively modest.
7. RECOMMENDATIONS
7.01      At negotiations it was agreed that a condition of effectiveness
of the proposed loan would be that the Borrower shall have authorized the
following posts and appointed qualified staff to such posts:
(i) Project Director, Peninsular Malaysia;
(ii) Project Leader for Rural Extension, Training and Development
Center;
(iii) Project Leader for Seeds; and
(iv) Project Leader for Soil Conservation.
7.02      Satisfactory assurances having been received on all major points,
the proposed project is recommended for a Bank loan of US$19.0 million with a
17-year maturity and a grace period of 3-1/2 years. The borrower would be the
Federation of Malaysia.

MALAYSIA
NATIONAL EXTENSION PROJECT
Peninsular Malaysia - Agricultural Extension Component
Introduction
1.        The Department of Agriculture (DOA) at the federal level consists
of seven technical branches (a) Crop Production Branch, (b) Crop Protection
Branch, (c) Soil and Analytical Service Branch, (d) Agriculture Education
and Training Branch, (e) Development and Training, (f) Farm Mechanization
Branch, and (g) Extension and Advisory Branch (see Chart WB16756).
2.        This project would be concerned with extension at the state level
(a state function) directed and assisted by the federal DOA. The state
extension services are responsible for all extension activities, including
providing extension support to the Farmers' Organization Authority (FOA).
Background
3.        There are less than 500 field level extension workers (agricultural
technicians) in the 11 states of Peninsular Malaysia to service 750,000 rural
families and a cropped area of 1,500,000 acres - 2.0 million acres when multiple
cropping is included (see Tables 1 and 2). The intensity and quality of exten-
sion services vary widely between states and within each state. In general,
the poorer states and more remote agricultural areas have the most inadequate
service. The numbers of agricultural technicians (ATs) are inadequate, in-
service training facilities and programs are not available, transportation and
equipment are in short supply, and housing and offices are only available in
the larger towns and villages.
4.        Limited resources and difficult communications have resulted in
services being primarily available to the more accessible, and often more
progressive and prosperous farmers near the urban centers, roads and highways.
The Department of Agriculture has already strengthened and expanded extension
for about 20% of the rural families which it serves in several irrigation
command areas and land settlement schemes, such as MADA, KADA and FELDA, with
highly satisfactory results. Extension services are also being strengthened
in Kelantan under Loan 1294-MA, the extension component of which forms the
basis for the National Extension Project.
S.        Eighty percent of Malaysia's smallholder agriculture, other than
irrigated padi and tree crops, is scattered in relatively small pockets beside
the rivers and among the valleys. Many areas are only accessible by boat
requiring a journey of several hours. The State Department of Agriculture
extension service advises and assists these rural families to the ability of
its present limited resources. However, the Government of Malaysia recognizes
that these services are presently inadequate. Because of the remote and



ANNEX 1
Page 2
scattered nature of much of its agriculture and its rural families, as a
matter of economics, it is necessary that agricultural extension workers also
perform regulatory functions and administer subsidy programs as part of their
effort to serve the rural families.
6.        Specific indications of the poor quality of life among rural people
are as follows:
(a) 69% of the houses occupied by smallholders and farm workers
have been found to be substandard, while only 15% of urban
housing is in the same category;
(b) in spite of adequate per capita levels of calorie and protein
availability, pockets of malnutrition exist in some states
especially among the lower income groups;
(c) 63% of houses in rural areas have no piped water and 69% have
no electricity;
(d) underemployment and unemployment are prevalent with only a
1% increase in jobs in 1975.
7.        In an effort to improve this position a two-pronged approach was
adopted in the Second Malaysia Plan (1971-75) to increase incomes and living
standards in the rural areas. Resettlement of farmers from densely populated
farming communities was combined with direct income raising activities on
existing farms. The main objective of new land settlement is to create employ-
ment while at the same time easing the population pressure in existing communi-
ties. So far the government agencies responsible for this development have
resettled 37,500 farm families comprising over 214,000 people.
8.        Simultaneous activities to improve incomes and living conditions
in established farming communities follow the concept of package programs and
area development. Selected growth areas have been provided with the full
complement of physical and institutional infrastructures such as drainage and
irrigation, transport network, storage and marketing facilities, input
supply, etc. so as to give a better opportunity for realizing their full
production potential.
9.        Technology Base for Extension.  The project would develop a close
working relationship with the Malaysian Agricultural Research Institute (MARDI)
as the base for new technology and backstopping support for the solution to
local and seasonal crop problems. The Extension Liaison Unit, established
under Loan 1115-MA as an integral part of the Federal Extension Branch of the
DOA, would be the main channel of communication between MARDI, DOA and the
SDOAs.
10.       MARDI's Project Development Unit is applying and evaluating a system
of "standard packagef' recommendations provided by community research teams;
similar "standard package" recommendations would be developed by the technical
branches and staff of the Department of Agriculture for demonstration and



ANNEX I
Page 3
application by the extension service. Under the "package" approach, recommenda-
tions would be made on appropriate varieties, seeds rates, fertilizer rates,
plant population, plant spacing, etc., that would be compatible with local
resources. The working relationship between MARDI and the extension services
is being worked out through such joint efforts as the State Agricultural
Research Development Committee (SARDEC), which brings research and extension
staff together to discuss and resolve local problems, including "standard
package" recommendations.
11.       The DOA and the mission recognize that the extension project, in the
short term (the next 5-10 years) would not be constrained by the regular and
continuing availability of new technology emanating from MARDI and the Research
Centers in Sabah and Sarawak.
12.       A tremendous gap still exists between known technology and that
being applied by the majority of farmers in Malaysia; therefore, this project
would aim at narrowing the production gap primarily through the dissemination
of known and proven technology. New technology would replace and supplement
known technology as it becomes available.
13.       Potential for Production Increases.  Further improved practices
flowing from research will continue to increase potential. The padi crop
serves as a good example of the general level of achievement. The current
average yield of padi is less than 1.25 tons per acre (less than 1.0 ton/acre
excluding controlled irrigation) for both the main and off-season crops on
irrigated land. Production in the better areas such as the MADA Irrigation
Command Area is significantly above this, but even here the average is still
low compared with the potential. Although detailed local calculations are
not available it is probable that the physiological production limit is in
excess of 10 tons dry matter per acre given the annual availability of solar
energy to crop plants. This maximum is unlikely ever to be achieved since
the optimum combination of growth factors can never be sustained on a commer-
cial basis. Nevertheless, it puts into perspective the present level of
performance and indicates that there is significant room for improvement by
following sound farming practices. Similar relationships between what is
presently being achieved and what is possible also apply to other crops.
There are three possible areas of improvement in the physical production from
crop plants, these being in genotype, growing environment and nutrition. In
all three areas there is abundant improved technology already available for
extension workers to communicate to farmers, as well as innovations resulting
from research.
14.       Improved Genotypes.  The introduction of better, higher-yielding
varieties has already made a significant contribution to increases in crop
yields in Malaysia. The extent to which this improvement can be continued
depends on the skill of the plant breeder and the rate at which improved
varieties and planting material can be multiplied and made available to
farmers. If all farmers were to adopt the improved varieties and selections
which are already available overall yields could rise by 25% solely as a
result of introducing these improved genotypes. Extension effort is therefore
likely to be well rewarded in this field.



ANNEX 1
Page 4
15.       Better Growing Environment.  The full genetic potential of a variety
cannot be realized unless the crop is grown in an environment in which it
thrives.  This means that physical soil conditions must be favorable to root
development, and planting must be done at the correct time and in suitable
conditions.
16.       Most farmers would claim that they seek to achieve optima on every
crop they grow but the realization is usually short of the ideal, often
seriously so. The reasons for this are many. There is often a lack of
knowledge about the optimum treatments which should be given, sometimes
insufficient money or credit to purchase the necessary inputs. Frequently
management is inadequate and, for example, weeds are not removed until well
past the time when competition has already lowered the crop yield. The
extension opportunity here is high, particularly when these improved farming
practices are applied to new, higher-yielding varieties and selections, since
the higher the level of yield the greater the proportionate increase. In
Peninsular Malaysia as a whole the potential yield increase is probably around
50% of current levels from improvement of the general environment in which
crops grow.
17.       Crop Nutrition.  The final opportunity for improving crop yields
lies in better nutrition. Only a small part of Peninsular Malaysia has a
mean annual rainfall of less than 70 inches and in places the precipitation
is more than double this. As a result soils are highly leached and most
cultivated areas are deficient in lime and phosphate with little carryover of
residual nitrogen from year to year. Fertilizer use has increased substan-
tially during the past ten years, largely as a result of extension activities.
Rates of fertilizer application are, however, still well below the optimum on
many farms and there is considerable scope for raising crop yields by higher
fertilizer inputs. Although no very precise data are available, local esti-
mates suggest that yield responses up to 25% over present levels are likely
to result from recommended fertilizer treatments.
18.       The sum total of the above potential improvements in crop yields
gives an ultimate extension "target" for doubling the present output of crops
in Peninsular Malaysia. New crop opportunities are being developed and farmers
face an increasing choice as to the balance of enterprises on their farms and
the technology which should be applied in order to maximize income. The
development of the farm management advisory function is a relatively new role
for the extension service, but it is an essential complement to the promotion
of new technology and will lead to the more effective use of resources in the
agricultural sector of the economy.
19.       As the network of extension agents is improved and their capability
is increased it will become possible for them to play their full part in a
two-way communication with farmers and in providing information services to
other organizations. In one direction, farmers can receive relevant and
topical guidance on technology, credit availability, agricultural policy,
cropping opportunities, economic relationships, etc. which is vital if they
are to increase the viability of their holdings. In the other direction, the
extension service will be able to supply intelligence to the DOA, identify



ANNEX 1
Page 5
problems for research, indicate potential areas for development, etc., thus
making it a vital component in the overall program to reduce poverty and to
raise living standards in the smallholder sector.
Present Organization and Management of the Extension Service
20.       General.  Extension work of the DOA (Chart WB17602) is carried out
at two levels. At the federal level, the extension and advisory services
provide skills and financial and material assistance to the state departments
of agriculture and to all other agencies dealing with small farmers including
RISDA, FELDA, FOAs, etc. At the state level the DOA carries out the actual
extension work with farmers. The nature, scope and emphasis of extension
work is thus the responsibility of the individual states, but the Federal
Extension Service provides graduate staff for supporting technical service
posts in the states and also assists with the financing of field extension
work.
21.       Federal Organization.  An outline of the present organization of
the Federal Department of Agriculture is shown in Chart WB17602. The Director
General is responsible, through his assistants, for the HQ divisions which
cover coconut development, training, extension and advisory services, statis-
tics and communications, crop production, crop protection, and soil and
analytical services. The State Directors of Agriculture (SDOAs) report to
the Director of Extension on all matters concerning extension which have a
national bias but they are also responsible to their state Government for
general extension work within the state territory. The Directors of the Crop
Production, Crop Protection and Soil and Analytical Divisions are responsible
for the staff in their disciplines who are located in the states for national
projects, such as surveys, data collection, field investigations, situation
reports, etc. These staff also serve as subject matter specialists to back
up the field extension workers. The SDOAs can call on their services to
assist with extension work, but the extent to which this is provided varies
widely between the eleven states depending on staff numbers and the priority
accorded to other work.
22.       The Extension Liaison Unit (ELU), Federal DOA.  The ELU was estab-
lished within the Extension Branch of the DOA as part of Loan 1115-MA as an
initial step toward strengthening communications between Research, the
Federal Extension Service, and the State Extension Services.
23.       The ELU is organized into four separate functional groups:
(a) communications, (b) data processing, (c) projects, and (d) subject
matter. Under the project communications would be the most important aspect
of ELU's work and would primarily be concerned with preparing extension
documentation, mass media communication materials, visual aid systems,
development of teaching materials, and training of extension management and
subject matter specialists. The data processing group would be primarily a
support function, collecting, processing, and evaluating agricultural data
and information. The projects group would collaborate closely with MARDI's
Project Development Unit in the evaluation of research findings. The subject
matter group would provide technical support for the other groups.



ANNEX 1
Page 6
24.       ELU became operational in May 1976 after the establishment of 31
posts and now consists of a Director and six subject matter and communication
specialists. The Unit is now housed in a temporary building, and a permanent
building has been designed and will be built adjacent to the DOA complex in
Kuala Lumpur.
25.       The major functions of ELU would be the collection, evaluation,
processing and dissemination of research and agricultural information from
all sources including MARDI. ELU staff will process this information into a
usable form for extension officers, subject matter specialists and farmers.
The combination of subject matter and communications specialists in ELU would
perform the essential function of making agriculture information usable by the
farmers. ELU staff would be specialists in writing and presentation techniques.
ELU would also conduct regular information courses for DOA subject matter spe-
cialists and senior state extension officers. Sabah and Sarawak would also
establish State Extension Liaison Units (SELUs) to carry out this function in
East Malaysia. Although the SELUs would not be part of the Federal Extension
Branch ELU, they would participate fully in its activities.
26.       ELU is set up as a separate administrative unit for the purposes of
Loan 1115-MA; however the DOA has given its assurance that ELU functions
would ultimately be absorbed by existing units of the Federal Extension
Branch, and ELU is already considered as an integral unit of the Extension
Branch.
27.       The "ELU would gradually evolve into the DOA's planning and evalua-
tion arm of the extension branch, thus taking on a function which is sorely
needed in the Malaysia context." All principles of administration clearly
indicate, on the basis of experience, that the planning and evaluation
function should be an integral part of the administrator's office and not
part of an operating unit on a level with other operating units.
State Organization
28.       The present organization of the State Extension Services in Peninsular
Malaysia is shown in Chart WB17603. There are three levels of staff under the
SDOA:
(a) Agricultural Officers (AOs). These are all graduates and
some have a higher degree obtained on an overseas fellowship.
Their first degree involves a four-year course at the University
of Malaysia.
(b) Agricultural Assistants (AAs). These have a diploma after
completing a three-year course at the Agricultural University
of Malaysia.
(c) Agricultural Technicians (ATs). These would have a certificate
from one of the six agricultural institutes in Malaysia.
29.       The SDOA has overall responsibility for extension work and in some
states he executes this through an agricultural officer who is designated as



L- 
Pag& ;
Chief Extension Officer. When no such post exists the SDOA himself directs
and coordinates extension work.
30.       Extension activities are organized on a circle, district and area
basis. A circle consists of from 3 to 6 districts and is supervised by an
agricultural officer. Each district contains 3 to 5 Mukims and is supervised
by an agricultural assistant who is appointed and paid by the state. Each
area comprises 3 to 6 Mukims and are staffed with an agricultural technician
who is also appointed and paid by the state. The number of farm families
covered by each AT within an area varies widely depending on the density of
population and the intensity of staffing. The average is calculated to be
one agricultural technician to 1,500 farmers and this is clearly a very
inadequate coverage. The project would increase this ratio to 1:800.
31.       The effectiveness of the agricultural technician is vital to the
overall success of the extension service. With the present organization
each agricultural technician has to undertake a number of functions in his
area as well as agricultural extension work. These include:
(a) informing the agricultural community about government
policy and schemes;
(b) dispensing government subsidies or grants to farmers;
(c) regulatory work such as implementing the Poisons Act,
investigating gun license applications, etc.;
(d) collecting agricultural statistics;
(e) liaison work such as participating in Farmers' Association
activities, etc.
32.       There are at present just under 500 agricultural technicians (ATs)
doing field extension work in Peninsular Malaysia. Many of these have been
recruited in the past few years and lack experience and general technical
knowledge. They tend to live near their office rather than out in the areas
where they work, largely because housing is easier to acquire near towns and
facilities for family living are better than in the rural districts. The
project would increase the number of ATs from 500 to about 900.
Operating Procedures
33.       The SDOA decides the extension strategy for the state in consultation
with his senior extension personnel. This policy is taken to the local level
by the circle officers who discuss with the agricultural assistants the type
of extension programs which should be undertaken in the districts. The AAs
then implement the programs through the ATs. Subsidy assistance is given in
kind and includes fertilizer, crop protection chemicals, and seed and planting
materials of new varieties. The state also supplies subsidized mechanical
services to farmers. The duration and scope of the input subsidies vary between
states: in some they are only given once to each farmer but in others the aid
may continue for several years particularly where perennial crops are involved.



ANNEX 1
Page 8
34.       The extension programs are frequently built around subsidy schemes.
Meetings of farmers are arranged to inform them of the benefits of new
technology and the subsidies are then held out as an inducement for them
to try the improved techniques. This has attractions for the extension
worker who can thus offer some incentive for farmers to follow his advice.
35.       The Extension Service is making use of the usual mass and group
communication (visit, training and demonstration) techniques and also is
working through leading farmers in the agricultural communities. They are
short of some demonstration aids such as film and slide projectors and
broadcasting equipment, although these facilities are available in a limited
form in all areas. Use is made of demonstration plots (often as part of
subsidy programs) and similar means to show farmers the benefits of improved
practices.
36.       In an effort to bridge the gap between the extension service and
the other agencies, particularly FOA, has encouraged the creation of farm
development centers as a base for all agencies providing extension and
similar services to farmers. These development centers would include space
for DOA staff as well as for RISDA and FELDA staff in the areas where these
agencies operate. There are instances where farm development centers are
working well and where there is close and frequent liaison between the var-
ious organizations. There are however also examples where conflicts between
these organizations have negated the apparent advantage of housing them
together.
37.       The following weaknesses of the extension services have been
identified.
(a) There are too few staff engaged in extension at the farm level. The
present coverage of one extension agricultural technician (AT) to
1,300 farm families means that regular contact can only be maintained
with a small proportion of farmers.
(b) The ATs receive insufficient training and technical updating to
enable them to offer adequate advice.
(c) In upgrading the scientific base of the extension service more
subject matter specialists (SMSs) are required to backstop extension
workers.
(d) There is a general lack of planning, coordination and evaluation of
extension work. The supervisory staff, Agricultural Officers (AOs)
and Agricultural Assistants (AAs) do not receive any training in
personnel management or project planning.
(e)  ATs perform a number of statutory and regulatory duties in addition
to extension work.
(f) The present chains of command within the extension service are unclear.
The SDOA has no direct control over the SMSs who consequently do not
always gi-ve the necessary priority to extension work.



ANNEX I
Page 9
(g)  Although SDOAs have ultimate responsibility for extension, several
states do not have a State Chief Extension Officer.
(h)  There is still some duplication of effort among agencies carrying
out extension work (but this is being corrected).
(i)  Research and extension still tend to operate in isolation but steps
to facilitate a closer working relationship are being developed
(paras. 2.19-2.21).
(j)  The practice of applying rigid criteria, based on levels of academic
qualification, for appointment to different grades is a constraint
to the efficient deployment of agricultural staff.
38.       The project would ameliorate many of the above weaknesses, notably
by providing more people and better training.
The Project
39.       The basic objective of the extension component of the project would
be to improve the financial position of smallholders by providin)g better tech-
nical and economic information through ihe medium o-f the Eztevsio)) serv:ie.
This would assist the farmers to improve preste;t f, ~Kng pacties and in some
cases alter the balance of farm enterprises, bcttb oi which would lead to higher
incomes.  The elements within the extension comV;>V  are as foJlows:
(a)  recruiting about 580 additional ATs anid about 38 (Vol. II:  Project
File, Part II) subjeci. matt-- s pccialist.- (nd strategically locating
these staff throughout Penins- MtJaysia;
(b)  providing better in-service training for staff both at local
level and by the establishment of four regional extension
training and development centers;
(c)  granting local and foreign fellowships to selected staff
members;
(d)  providing two man-years of foreign ter-o< cal assistance in
extension and soil conservation;
(e)  improving ihe facilities availahie toi exteDsioni staff by
developing about 40 exte-nsion subce-,Lers   i providiug additional
eq,iipment; and
(f)  introducing a more positive and direct style of management
for planning extension activities.
Purpose and Organization of the New Resources
40.       Increase in Staff Numbers.  An additional 580 ATs are needed to
improve the ratio of extension workers to farmers. The present estimated
ratio is approximately one field extension worker to 1,500 farmers and this
represents a very inadequate ground coverage. One extension agent covers
about 3,000 acres of crop land which is too wide an area for him to cover
effectively given the communication problems caused by the scattered nature



ANNEX 1
Page 10
of the agriculture and the attitude of farmers in Peninsular Malaysia.
At present there are 800 ATs in the 11 states, only 495 of whom are working
directly with farmers. The project would increase this group by about 580
and the ratio would, on the average, be one extension worker to about 800
farmers. The 580 ATs to be provided under the project represents over half the
total number requested by the 11 states of Peninsular Malaysia in the Third
Malaysia Plan. It should be emphasized that the extra 580 ATs represent a
substantial expansion of the field extension force during the project period.
Although a further increase might be possible, the present proposal represents
the maximum increase which the service can effectively absorb and utilize.
41.       The present distribution of ATs among the 11 states, the intended
deployment of new ATs, and the present and future ratio of ATs to farm
families are shown in Tables 1 and 2. The ratios are most favorable in some
of the east coast, southern and central states where poverty problems are
greatest. There are however poverty problems in all states which would be
met by concentrating staff and other resources at local discretion within the
individual state concerned./I Table I shows the acreage per extension worker
before and after the new staff are appointed and indicates the improved
ratios which result. A more detailed picture of the deployment of new ATs
within each state is given in the Supplementary Tables (Report No. 1555a-s-MA).
42.       Subject Matter Specialists (SMSs).  In addition to ATs the project
would also provide for about 38 SMSs to be based in the 11 states. The major
role of these specialists would be to support the extension staff. SMSs
would be available as consultants to the extension workers and be responsible
for local trials and investigations to test and develop new technology. The
new specialists to be appointed will be decided according to local need. All
states require a soil scientist to concentrate on extension work in soils and
most also require an agronomist wbo specialized in crop production and who
would concentrate on the range of crops most important in each state. In
some states a crop protection specialist and farm management specialist are
also required. All states should have three specialists, except Perlis which
will have two. Probably the final balance between the disciplines would be
11 soil scientists, 10 agronomists, 8 crop protection specialists and 3 farm
management specialists. This group of staff would perform an invaluable
function in upgrading the scientific base of the extension service and
ensuring that the guidance given to farmers by the ATs is both technically
accurate and in line with the latest research findings.
43.       Improving In-Service Trainir.g.  The lack of forwalized and regu)ar
in-service training has been identified as a major weakness limiting the
present effectiveness of the extension service.  New SMSs will have responsi-
bility for the technical updating of all extension staff within each state.
/1  Provision for additional staff in most of the state of Kelantan has
already been made in a previous project (North Kelantan Rural Deve,oVmeuA
Project - Report No. 1137-MA).



ANNEX 1
Page 11
They will do this by arranging local training sessions within their respec ive
spheres and also by the regular dissemination of new information both in
written form and at regular local staff meetings. Approximately 10% of the
ATs', AAs' and AOs' time, i.e. about one day every two weeks, would initially
be spent on local training.
44.       Training would also be supplied through the establishment of
Regional Extension Training and Development Centers (RETDCs). The project
would provide four RETDCs to serve the north (Telok Chengai - Kedah), central
(Titi Gantomg - Perak), southern (Air Hitam - Johore) and eastern (Telaga
Batin - Trengganu) regions (see Annex 5). The RETDCs would provide broader
background training of two types: (a) courses in basic agricultural science,
crop technology and practical skills, and (b) training in operational and
management skills.
45.       The technical and scientific courses would be basically crop and
commodity oriented. Emphasis would be placed on crops and subject areas
relative to their present and potential contribution to the national economy
and their place in the eradication of poverty and the redistribution of income.
46.       In addition, technical courses will be needed on soil management,
conservation and reclamation, seed production and marketing.
47.       Fellowships and Study Tours.  Although the training described in
Annlex 5 would upgrade the knowledge and operational ability of staff at all
levels in the extension service there is also a need to complement local
training with overseas training. The project would provide for a total of
141 fellowships allocated to the 11 states, totaling 61 man-years and costing
M$2.1 million in the five-year period. The breakdown is as follows:
Category             Number of fellowships        Man-years
Postgraduage                   10                     20
Undergraduate                   6                     24
Short courses                 125                     17
Study tours
Total                       141                     61
48.       Of the above, the postgraduate scholarships would be allocated to
AOs who would be employed as SMSs when they have linished their postgraduate
training.  The undergraduate scholarships would be allocated  _o AAs with
demonstrated ability for higher grade work who wou]d be em-,, (yed as AOs when
they have finished ihis training.  The short courses and stud\  tours wculd be
allocated to staff at all levels with a high proporiion going to A's.  jhe
subjects for the short courses and fellowships would cover the following:
Technology - visits of up to three months (but some of only
two weeks or so) to study new technical developments in crop-
which are important in lMalaysia.



ANNEX I
Page 12
Extension Techniques - short visits to study extension programs
nM operation which have proved successful and which may have
relevance to Peninsular Malaysia.
Farm Management - visits to study farm management extension
programs in farming situations similar to those in Malaysia.
Home Economics - visits similar to the above but which cover
the home economics area.
49.       On their return from the short courses or study tours the staff
would be expected to produce a report which will be widely circulated in the
extension service.
50.       The aLove program of overseas fellowships makes no provision for
further training to selected ATs to enable them to obtain a diploma and thus
achieve promotir' io the AA grade.  Mechanisms for doing this would be
explored by the DQ> and the University.
51.       Soil Conservation.  The problems of soil conservation have been
overloo-ed in the past, but as the pace of agricultural development has
increased, the effects of soil erosion have become more noticeable through
loss of soil fertility, crop yields, and siltation and flooding. The DOA
has recognized that a critical stage has been reached in many areas and would
implement a pilot soil conservation scheme as an integral part of extension.
The main constraint to soil conservation is the lack of staff and equipment.
The project proposes LI provide and train staff and implement a pilot scheme
in the states of Penang and Pahang. These staff would be assigned to the
states with line responsibility to the SDOAs and ultimate responsibility to
zh: Soils ond Analytical Branch of the federal DOA.  The project would also
-cvide fellowships, technical assistance and equipment to support the soil
~-onservation extension effort.
52.       Extension programs for soil conservation would be developed and
;imp -ented as expertise and information become available.  Extension workers
wo-ld make farmers aware of the consequences of soil erosion and advise them
on appropriate control measures. The extension effort would be largely based
the transfer of known technology and techniques.
53.       Foreign Technical Assistance.  The federal and state DOAs face a
dau-nting task in upgr-ading their extension work and in establishing the
training programs required for extension staff; thus, the project would
pro, de three man-years of foreign technical assistants (including soil
conservation) scheduled for 1978 and 1979.
54.       The foreign expert(s) would be responsible to the national project
director. General terms of reference would be to assist in:
(a) laying the groundwork to launch the four RETDCs;
(b) determining the course content and developing the curricuJum
of the RETDCs;



ANNEX 1
Page 13
(c) formulating the work program for extension and soil
conservation;
(d) training counterparts in extension and soil conservation;
and
(e) assisting in the design and implementation of a pilot
soil conservation project.
Essential qualifications of the expert(s) are:
(a) extensive training or experience in extension work and
soil conservation;
(b) experience in directing, monitoring and evaluating extension
activities, preferably from previous work in developing
countries;
(c) very good knowledge of English and ability to write and speak
lucidly on the technical and economic aspects of agriculture.
55.       Improved Extension Facilities.  The work of the extension service
is greatly hampered by the lack of adequate facilities. The main shortages
are office accommodation and meeting rooms, staff housing, vehicles and the
personal equipment which an extension worker needs for his day to day acti-
vities.
56.       Offices and Meeting Rooms.  The project would provide for construc-
tion of 32 extension subcenters (ESCs) in the various states. These would be
located in poverty areas which have agricultural potential and where existing
extension facilities are inadequate. Two ATs would be stationed at each ESC
and the district AA would supervise this office.
57.       The ESCs would complement the existing extension coverage.  The
provision of these facilities will provide farmers with a more convenient
point at which to see the extension worker and will keep the extension workers
closer to the area of operation. The distribution of the ESCs among the
various states is shown in the Supplementary Tables (Report No. 1555a-s-MA).
58.       Living Quarters.  The project provides for a major increase in the
living quarters for extension personnel. A total of 166 houses are required
and the distribution of these by state is shown in Annex 6, Section A. This
is considered an essential feature in improving the extension coverage by
reducing travel distance and time. Most of the housing would be provided in
conjunction with the ESCs so that extension officers could live and work in
the rural areas.
59.       Vehicles and Boats.  The project would provide for additional
vehicles and boats in states where communications are particularly poor and
the existing facilities are inadequate. Hired craft would continue to be
used for sea travel.

62.       Improved Management.  Deficiencies in management would be overcome
by giving the appropriate instruction and requiring that a formal management
program be followed at circle and district level.
63.       The management structure would be improved by giving line responsi-

field extension workers. The management structure for the staff located in

Peninsula Extension Component Financing
64.       The project would assist GOM with financing the foreign exchange
component (US$5.50 million); of which US$1.54 million is civil works,
US$1.56 is vehicles and equipment, and US$0.96 million is technical assis-
tance and fellowships. Physical contingencies are US$0.39 million, and
price contingencies are US$1.05 million. All capital costs (including
additional extension staff for the purposes of the project) would be borne by
the Federal Government. Extension (other than in-service training) is a
state function; thus operating costs would be borne by the state. Detailed
financial information is contained in Annex 6, Section A and is summarized on
the following page.
Extension Methodology and Evaluation
65.       The increase in staff and better training would provide the means
to carry out better extension among a greater number of farmers. The project
would introduce a more formal method of working which would involve better
planning of activities, more systematic operation and regular monitoring
and evaluating of extension programs. At state level this would be achieved
as follows.
66.       The SDOA/Chief Extension Officer would be responsible for planning
the state's overall extension priorities in the light of national policies
for agriculture and the particular problems and requirements within the state
itself. These chief extension officers would ensure that the necessary
resources are available for extension and in consultation with the agriculture
officers and SMSs would plan extension objectives. They would also be respon-
sible for monitoring and evaluating extension programs on a state-wide basis
and reporting, as appropriate, both to the state authorities and the federal
DOA.
67.       The Agriculture Officers would be responsible for deciding the
emphasis of extension work and broad advisory programs at circle level within
the framework of the state priorities. They would do this in consultation
with AAs and this group would also decide the training requirements of the
field extension workers and the assistance required from SMSs both in training
and in carrying out promotional work. The AOs must also monitor and evaluate
extension programs on a circle-wide basis and will report to the state's chief
extension officer.
68.       The Agriculture Assistants would work out detailed extension
programs in consultation with the ATs and with the help of the SMSs when
necessary. They would have to organize and direct the field staff and also
give support to the ATs in their field work. They would be responsible for
monitoring and evaluating extension programs at district level and would
report to the AO.
69.       The Agricultural Technicians would be responsible in their areas
for executing extension programs which they helped to design. They would use

their initiative to vary programs according to local needs and seasonal
variations and would also assist the SMSs in local investigations and trials.
They would monitor and evaluate the programs carried out in their area and
report to the AA.
70.       The Subject Matter Specialists would be responsible for injecting
specialist knowledge into the extension system wherever it is needed. Prin-
cipally this would embrace training field staff, circulating technical informa-
tion, assisting in identifying problems and deciding priorities, and acting
as a consultant to local extension workers. The SMSs would also have a trial
and development role and would be the main channel for communicating research
requirements to the SARDEC and thence to MARDI. The SMSs would report to the
SDOA or the Chief Extension Officer according to local arrangement.
Extension Philosophy
71.       The purpose of extension is to give objective guidance to farmers
on the availability of new and improved practices and the way in which these
may be incorporated into farming systems so as to increase farm productivity
and profitability. It is not sufficient merely to tell farmers of these new
developments or to demonstrate them and to expect farmers to adopt them
automatically. As a community farmers are cautious. They have to be sure
that the practice will work on their farm. Unless they know and trust the
extension agent they will place little reliance on his advice. Moreover new
farming practices are of different kinds and will need different extension
approaches: some, such as new varieties or new chemicals, are easy for
farmers to adopt while others, such as new cultivation systems or a change of
enterprise are much more difficult. Finally, farmers themselves are a hetero-
genous population, varying in attitude and personality. Some are highly
motivated to make changes whereas others require considerable stimulation.
Some have the credit resources to make innovations; the rate at which others
can change is limited by lack of funds.
72.       The extension worker needs to recognize all these factors and must
accept that he is working with farmers of different abilities, attitudes and
resources. He will know that some can progress more quickly than others and
that some farmers have the managerial ability to handle more complex systems
immediately whereas others must be brought along more slowly. He must curb
any temptation to work only with more capable farmers because his function is
to improve agricultural productivity in a total sense and to give emphasis to
those farmers who are in the lower income groups so that rural poverty may be
reduced. There needs to be a variety of programs, some aimed at the more
progressive farming element, while others of a lower standard are designed to
help the less able farmers improve the fundamentals of their present farming
practices.
73.       Basically there are four extension functions, and these may be
described as information gathering, problem identification and solution,
promotional activities, and consultancy work.
74.       The field extension worker plays a valuable role in problem identi-
fication and solution. The extent to which he will be concerned with problem



ANNEX 1
Page 18
solution depends on the time available. Ideally all extension workers would
be encouraged to have some involvement in local trials and investigations,
these being done in association with the specialists.
75.       Promotional work is a major activity for extension staff; it is
the educational phase of extension work.
76.       The final component in extension is consultancy work:  the extension
agent counsels individual farmers or groups of farmers who have approached
him seeking information.
Proposed Extension Methodology
77.       The basis of the project extension methodology would be systematic
planning and application of extension activities (training, visits, package
inputs and demonstrations) at the farm level beginning with the day-to-day work
of the field level extension worker (agricultural technician) up through the
Area, District, Circle, and State levels of organization. The field agricultural
technician would plan his program on a two to four week schedule, depending on
local requirements, and would plan regular training, demonstration and discus-
sion visits to provide timely agricultural information relevant to the most
important crops and activities in his area. He would conduct regularly scheduled
visits, discussion and demonstrations with farm leaders and other farmers; main-
tain records of visits, information transmitted, acceptance by farmers, and
achievements of farmers. These reports would form part of a cumulative record
of activities and achievements from the Area level, District level, Circle level
and finally the State level where these cumulative records would form the basis
for long-term systematic monitoring, evaluation and planning. It will be a
formal system but not a rigid one, thus leaving room for sufficient flexibility
and initiative at local level to meet particular situations. The broad pattern
of planning for extension will be as follows:
78.       State Level.  The SDOA and his senior technical staff will set
extension priorities in broad terms (see State Extension Organization Chart
WB17603). They will identify the important commodities and the elements
within these where improvement should be sought. Regular annual review at
state level would indicate the average yield for each main crop within the
state and point out which of these are still low and the areas which require
further technical improvement. It would also discuss the prospects for
various crops and particularly the opportunities for expanding the acreage of
some commodities or the reasons for contracting the acreage of others. It
would also make reference to the level of use of some of the main inputs,
e.g. fertilizer, lime or mechanical services and comment on the benefits
which would result from increased usage.
79.       Circle Level.  The AOs in charge of the circles would be involved
in the discussions with the SDOA and SMSs in deciding the broad extension
priorities and opportunities within the state. These in turn would be dis-
cussed at the circle level with the AAs who are in charge of the districts
making up the circle. A list of the most important topics requiring pro-
motional effort in each district would be decided and priorities established.
The group would also review the impact of past extension activities and



ANNEX 1
Page 19
consider which innovations were not fully adopted by the farming community.
Following these discussions each AA would prepare an outline of the extension
programs to be carried out in his district in the next year.
80.       District and Area Level.  Detailed promotional and development
programs for each area in the district would be worked out by the AA and ATs
based on the discussions which have taken place at the circle. The AT
concerned would work out the number of demonstration plots needed and their
location. He would identify where and when to hold meetings with farmers and
the regularity with which these are needed with various farming groups during
the season. He would specify what help he needs from the SMSs and the
facilities required in terms of mobile units and other advisory aids such
as subsidies. The remainder of his time would be free for consultancy work
and would allow opportunities to cope with seasonal crises such as pest
outbreaks, etc. He will also require some time for office duties, meeting
with his supervisors and training. Regular discussions among the staff at
district level and the AA would be held at 2 to 4 week intervals - depending
on need - for formal review of progress and exchange of experiences; the AO
and one or more SMSs would also be present. Their function will be to
comment on the review of progress and indicate any particular technological
input which may be appropriate in the immediate future.
Evaluation
81.       Evaluation of the effectiveness of extension activities would be
a continuous long-term process extending through and beyond the disbursement
period; therefore, emphasis in the project period would be on designing an
effective system. The impact of extension work would be measured in
various ways, e.g. changes in farming practice, increases in crop yield,
changes in crop acreage, improvements in farm incomes, etc.
82.       An essential feature of evaluation is that the degree of success
of extension activities can only really be assessed against some previously
determined objective. Thus, extension workers would have a target for
their programs. These targets would be broadly defined at national and state
level and closely specified at local level. The progress of the extension
programs would be reviewed at the regular staff meetings, and as each program
is completed the AT will make an assessment of its success.
83.       Each year the AA would aggregate the target data and the assessed
achievement from some of the programs in his district. This would be highly
subjective; the AA would list the main problem areas, the crop acreage
involved, and, where possible, an estimate of the physical yield benefits
which resulted from the advice.
84.       Having assembled these data the AA would pass them to the AO who
would aggregate the information on a circle basis adding his own comments on
the outcome of extension activities within his territory. He would then
forward the evaluation reports to the state's chief extension officer who
would prepare an aggregated statement for all extension work. This information
would then be used at state and national level for planning extension priorities
for the next year.



ANNEX 1
Page 20
85.       The extension service would be responsible for evaluating its own
efforts rather than this being a function of some separate agency within the
DOA. Evaluation is an integral part of extension work and the staff need the
information on a continuing basis to assess their priorities and plan their
programs most effectively. Evaluation during the disbursement period would be
of an indicative operational and implementation planning nature although the
evaluation and monitoring system would also be designed to provide policy
planning information to the I4inistry in the long run.
Subsidy Schemes
86.       A brief mention is made above of the need to take subsidy schemes
into account when planning extension programs. The subsidies may be regarded
as an aid to extension rather than a general mechanism to support farm incomes.
On the whole the subsidy schemes seem to be favored by the extension workers
who regard them as an incentive which they can use to promote better technology.
In the Third Malaysia Plan provision is made for increasing the subsidy input;
however, this would not be regarded as an extension function. Provision would
be made for this work to be phased out as a function of the extension staff.
Relationship Between the DOA and Other Extension Agencies
87.       Although the DOA is the main agency responsible for extension work
among smallholders in Peninsular Malaysia there are a number of other organiza-
tions which also operate in the extension field. Chief among these are the
rubber smallholders development authority (RISDA), and the Federal Land Develop-
ment Authority (FELDA).
88.       RISDA is responsible for extension work among smallholders whose
main enterprise is rubber but who may also have a variety of other crops.
The RISDA agents cover extension on these other crops as well as dealing with
the rubber enterprise.
89.       FELDA agents are responsible for carrying out extension among new
land settlers who have been placed on holdings reclaimed under government
development schemes.
90.       The FOA has its origins within the DOA.  The Farmers' Association
Act of 1967 estabished the Farmers' Associations as a component unit of the
DOA with the role of providing input supply, credit, marketing and other
services to support the extension work of the Department. In 1973 the
Farmers' Organization Act superseded the Farmers' Association Act and created
the FOA by merging the Farmers' Associations with existing rural agro-based
cooperatives. By the end of 1975 the DOA staff who had been running the FAs
or carrying out extension work within the FAs either came back to the DOA or
became employees of the FOA. This split has created difficulties. Although
FOA staff are still under the aegis of and paid by the Ministry of Agriculture,
and they remain on the grade level they had in the DOA, the management line
is now separate without any formal linkage between the activities of the FOA
and the DOA extension service.



ANNEX 1
Page 21
91.       Membership of the FAs (farmers' cooperatives) includes the more
progressive farming element in many areas since it is these people who have
recognized the advantage of institutionalized credit and low priced inputs
which the FAs can supply. The remaining farmers have to rely upon the
private sector to obtain credit, this usually being both more difficult to
raise and more expensive.
92.       The FAs have attempted to continue to provide extension to their
membership. This is not part of their official function but something of a
vacuum in extension activities was created when the FAs were separated from
the DOA and it was natural that the FA staff should attempt to fill this gap.
93.       The GOM is aware of the problems and duplication, and it is intended
that the FOA would be developed as an agency supplying credit, input supplies,
and marketing services, and that its incursion into the extension field would
be checked. This mission was satisfied that action was being taken to alleviate
this problem.
94.       RISDA and FELDA will look to the DOA for technical information and
will also make use of the extension service specialists when required. Their
approach to the specialists would, however, be through local extension workers
who may themselves be able to answer many of the problems posed by these other
agencies.
Benefits and Justification
95.       This project aims to benefit the smallholder sector of the farming
community, particularly those smallholders within the poverty group. By
strengthening the extension service and inputs (particularly improved seeds),
both in quantity and knowledge, the information services available to
smallholders will be improved and they will receive more frequent counseling
and guidance on how best to increase the productivity and output of their
holdings. In seeking to raise the income of this basically Malay group of
farmers, the project is in line with the Government's objectives of reducing
rural poverty and income disparities between various sectors of the community.
The prospective increases in crop output will help reduce dependence on
imported foodgrains which is another aim of national policy. Conceptually,
therefore, the project is well justified.
96.       The annual cost of the additional resources required will be approxi-
mately M$9 million when the projected improvements are fully implemented.
This figure is derived from totaling the yearly staff and operating costs,
10% of the building costs and 20% of the equipment costs. When spread over
the smallholder occupied area of 2 million acres it represents an annual
"charge" of M$4.50 per acre. Alternatively, it may be expressed as M$13 per
farm family when spread over the 750,000 smallholdings or M$10,000 per field
extension wo.:ker, of which th re will be around 900 when the new staff are
employed.
97.       Viewed simply, the requirement to generate an additional M$4.50 per
acre from extension activities is a small target. It represents only 8 kg of



ANNEX 1
Page 22
rice at current prices and similar tiny quantities of other products.  But it
is not sufficient that extension should break even. The aim should be to look
for a return on the investment in additional production which is several iu1s
the cost and preferably more.
98.       In a five-year span a competent extension worker would expect that
his leader group of farmers would be making fairly good use of existing techno-
logy, growing the best varieties, using the correct fertilizers and employing
the correct growing techniques and crop protection methods. Rate of growth
towards doubling yields could be in excess of 10% per year, far short of the
theoretical maximum. The next group would be following and could achieve at
least 8% growth. The fringe farmers could expect a 5% annual improvement and
the subsistence farmers' output would have increased by 2% or less.
99.       Calculation of the above benefits in financial terms is inevitably
imprecise. Nevertheless, it gives some guide, and a conservative one, of the
likely impact of improved extension. The average growth in output over the
whole farming community is over 6% and this towards a commercially attainable
target using existing technology and taking no account of the benefit from
further research findings on improved infrastructure.
