MALAYSIA
NATIONAL EXTENSION PROJECT
Sabah - Extension Component
Introduction
1.        There are major differences between the organization and institu-
tional framework of the Department of Agriculture in Peninsular Malaysia on
the one hand and those of Sabah and Sarawak on the other. Nevertheless, the
basic constraints to the effectiveness of extension services are so similar
as to warrant a unified project concept and strategy that is valid on a
Pan-Malaysia basis.
2.        To illustrate the communality of problems, the following features
characterize both West and East Malaysia. Farmers receive little or no
extension guidance, because the extension activity is subordinated to a host
of other activities. Extension workers in direct contact with farmers lack
the required competence and are often uninformed about new developments in
their field both because of the absence of in-service training facilities for
field staff, and poor or inadequate contacts between the research worker and
the extension field staff. The ratios of extension worker to farm families
are far from adequate to make a noticeable impact, especially in the more
depresssed areas. Moreover, they all suffer from severe mobility problems
and lack other facilities, (e.g. transportation, extension equipment, and
housing) that could increase their effectiveness and motivate them in their
tasks.
3.       eIn Sabah, the Department of Agriculture, the Padi Board and the
Rubber Fund Board are the main agencies concerned with services to small-
holders. In contrast to the Mainland, the Sabah State Government is fully
committed to Farmers' Associations as a vehicle for SDOA agricultural programs,
including extension aimed at raising small farmer incomes. Research, extension
and Farmers' Associations are directed and administered by the Department of
AgJIiculture_
4.        With the organization and policy directives in mind, the Sabah
project calls for measures to strengthen not only the extension services of
the SDOA but also the SDOA controlled FAs so as to expand their largely agro-
business operations into new "organic areas". While accommodating the priority
of the FAs' influence zones, the DOA would also provide a more intensive
extension service to the other areas. The DOA's extension responsibility would
embrace all crops except rubber. A similar approach would be taken as on the
mainland, to strengthen the grass roots level staff with specialized extension
education.



ANNEX 2
Page 2
5.        The manpower constraints that limit Sabah's capacity for implement-
ing agricultural development programs could be removed if existing services
were augmented and new facilities created to provide preservice agricultural
training and in-service training on a continuous basis for all DOA field staff,
as the RETDCs do on the mainland. The training programs for farmers and home
economics demonstrations would be extended not only to increase farm production
and income, but also to increase the quality of rural life.
6.        Both Sabah and Sarawak are part of Malaysia but in many respects
they operate as separate, independent states. The Federal Government is
anxious to have a Pan-M4alaysia Extension Project and has agreed to fund all
capital costs including those for additional staff. Only the SDOAs from
Sabah and Sarawak would directly "assist" the National Project Director (NPD)
in project implementation; they would manage their own project components and
would defer to the NPD on matters directly related to project financing and
procurement. They would regularly submit annual program budgets and project
reports to the NPD.
Background
7.        Sabah, or old North Borneo on the tropical island of Borneo  lies
roughly between latitudes 4  and 7  north and longitudes 1150 and 119  east.  It
is bounded on the north by the South China Sea and the Sulu Sea, on the east by
the Sulu Sea and the Celebes Sea, on the south by Indonesia and Sarawak, and on
the west by Sarawak, Brunei and the South China Sea. It is largely mountainous
with coastal plains and valleys. Rainfall is normally heavy and vegetation
tropical.
8.        There are many ethnic groups in Sabah.  Farming communities include
Kadagans, Chinese, Muruts, Bajans, Suluk, Runggus, Lundayah, and many others,
each with their own language and varying agricultural skills. These ethnic
groups plus a variety of dialects and different languages make effective
communication difficult. Resistance to change is further complicated by
different cultural traditions and religious beliefs.
9.        Sabah covers 18,257,302 acres of which 92% is forest land and
only 4% is cropped (page 3). Only about 8% of the land is suitable for
diversified agriculture, of which only about 10% is currently under culti-
vation. There is, therefore, great potential for agricultural development.
10.       About 70% of the total (estimated) population of Sabah (737,500
people) were engaged in agriculture in 1974. About 112,000 farm families
are engaged in sedentary agriculture, many of them in subsistence agriculture
but with an increasing number entering the commercial sector. Of the economic-
ally active population 80% are involved in agriculture, forestry, hunting and
fishing (page 4).



ANNEX 2
Page 3
11.       MIalaysia is one of the most prosperous developing countries of Asia
with Sabah reportedly having the largest per capita income of any Malaysian
state, but the income distribution there is so skewed that the great majority
of people subsist on a very low income.

ANNEX 2
Page 5
12.       Exploitation of timber resources is progressing at an alarming
rate. Timber exports in 1974 were in excess of M$870 million and accounted
for 80% of the export value of principal agricultural products. The principal
exports of cultivated crops in order of value are palm oil, rubber cocoa beans,
palm kernel, and acid copra.
13.       The Land Settlement Schemes are important in promoting sedentary and
commercial agriculture. In 1974, the Sabah Land Development Board had settled
2,391 families with 50,486 planted acres under the Major Settlement Schemes and
the SDOA 9,105 families with 33,088 acres planted under the Minor Settlement
Schemes.
14.       Much of the 750,000 cultivated acres of Sabah is utilized
for export crops - rubber 260,000 acres, oil palm 140,000, coconut 130,000
and cocoa 20,000. Sabah imports 40% of its padi while only 120,000 acres
are planted to hill wet and Kendinga swamp varieties.
15.       About one third of the rubber acreage is owned by estates and
two thirds by smallholders. Yields and prices vary greatly, but returns
for most smallholders are considered unsatisfactory. Most of the oil palm
is owned by estates and returns are considered good. About 80% of the coconut
acreage is cultivated by smallholders - over 10,000 of them with an average of
about 8 acres - and returns are generally considered unsatisfactory. About 40%
of the cocoa acreage is cultivated by smallholders with an average of 8 acres.
Current prices are high and per farm returns to smallholders are around M$16,000,
about 10 times those for coconut. All padi is grown by smallholders. About
10,000 acres of irrigated padi is double cropped, and the average smallholding
is about 3 acres. Yields are about 2,000 lb per acre with local varieties and
up to 3,200 lb with HYVs. Maize is grown by about 6,500 smallholders averaging
2 acres each, with yields of about 3,000 lb per acre. Commercial vegetable
production for the local markets is the main occupation of about 1,100 families
on about 1,500 acres.
Oil Palm
16.       Oil palm is cultivated on 134,000 acres by estates while only 2,820
acres are planted by smallholders; 86,609 tons of palm oil and 20,123 tons of
kernel, valued at M$120,629,000 were exported in 1974. The average smallholder
with 6 to 8 acres has an income of about M$9,000 per year. About 400 families
are oil palm smallholders.
Rubber
17.       There are about 260,000 acres of rubber in the State; 87,748 are
cultivated by estates and 169,485 by smallholders. Total exports in 1974



ANNEX 2
Page 6
were 31,105 tons of rubber of all types, valued at M$50,464,000. Yields
vary from 650 lb to 1,650 lb rubber sheet per acre. The average size of a
smallholding is about 5-3/4 acres and there are about 29,647 rubber small-
holders.
Cocoa
18.       Approximately 24,000 acres of cocoa have been planted in Sabah,
14,616 acres by estates or cooperatives and the remainder by smallholders.
In 1974 a total of 4,432 tons of beans valued at M$16,030,000 were exported.
There are about 1,200 cocoa smallholders with an average holding of about
8 acres.
Coconut
19.       The present acreage of coconuts in the State is estimated at 130,888
acres, of which 31,654 acres are cultivated by estates or cooperatives and
99,234 acres by smallholders which together produced 9,672 tons of copra valued
at M$10,683,000 and 377 tons of coconut oil valued at M$813,000 in 1974. There
are about 10,600 smallholders cultivating coconut, with an average holding of
about 8-9 acres. Research into varietal selection of coconuts has just started,
but no releases have yet been made to farmers.
Rice
20.       There are 93,000 acres of wet padi in Sabah, with an additional
2,638 acres of Kendinga (swamp) padi and about 29,000 acres of hill padi (the
latter mainly cultivated by the hill tribes practicing shifting agriculture).
About 10,000 acres of padi was double cropped in 1975. The entire acreage is
cultivated by about 39,000 smallholders. The State Government plans to
substantially increase rice acreage and yields during the TMP by providing
improved seeds, extension, and irrigation.
Maize
21.       The 13,000 acres planted to sedentary maize in the State is culti-
vated by about 6,500 smallholders with yields averaging nearly 3,000 lb.
Farmers can plant three crops a year; however, they normally grow maize in
rotation with groundnuts or other crops which leaves the land fallow for one
crop season. A substantial increase in maize acreage is planned during the TMP
period.
Vegetables
22.       There are about 80,000 acres of horticultural crops in the state and
1,100 smallholders planting 2,127 acres to vegetables for their primary source
of income.

Agricultural Exports and Imports
23.       The principal agricultural exports in 1974 are shown below. Timber
accounts for 80% of the value of cultivated crop exports with palm oil exports
valued at M$105 million accounting for almost half. Rubber exports at 14$50 mil-
lion account for less than one quarter and cocoa beans at M$16 million are
in third place.
24.       Agricultural imports into Sabah in 1974 were about M$110 million
as shown below. Rice is by far the largest item, amounting to almost M$48 mil-
lion, followed by cereal products and dairy products. The Government gives
high priority to reducing the food and feed crop deficit.

Existing Organization of the Department of Agriculture
25.        As shown in Chart WB16925 there are three assistant directors of
agriculture (research, development and extension); also two officers in charge
of training, and personnel and headquarters administration.
Organization of the DOA Extension Branch
26.        The line of administration in the Extension Branch is as shown in
Chart WB16926.
27.       Under the project, the Extension Branch of the Department would be
fully responsible for all aspects of development and extension for all crops
except rubber (Chart WB16924). Apart from some administrative duties, such
as land inspection, station operation and maintenance, and subsidies, the main
activity of this branch is extension. Each of the four Residencies (a
political subdivision), has a Senior Agricultural Officer (SAO) in charge who
is a professionally qualified man with managerial experience. The SAO
supervises two or more academically qualified agricultural officers (AOs) who
are in charge of the Districts. Under the AOs are assistant agricultural
officers (AAOs) and the assistant development officers (ALDOs) (diploma
holders) who are the main supervisory staff. The front-line workers are the
agricultural assistants and the junior agricultural assistants. Where
Farmers' Associations (FAs) exist in a District, the agricultural officer is
also in charge of the FA and extension work carried on by the FAs. Extension
staff, if not actually working for the Farmers' Associations work in very
close liaison with the FA staff. Most of the Agricultural Assistants and
Junior Agricultural Assistants are posted to villages throughout the District.
An active and highly developed unit of the state extension service carries
out an effective nutrition, child care, family planning and home demonstration
program.
28.        Certain political and welfare appointments exist in the DOA.  These
appointments are not held against established posts nor are they included in
the Federal pension program. The mission was assured that these positions
are financed from a special fund and would not directly affect the Extension
budget.
29.        Detailed analyses of the number of staff per district together with
their respective quarters are given in Table 2. Extension officers are
presently assigned to manage and operate Farmers' Associations (Table 3);
staff for the additional nine FAs would also be provided under the project.



ANNEX 2
Page 10
Present Activities of the Extension Branch (DOA)
30.       Chart WB16926 shows the six main activities of the Extension Branch
of DOA. These are the District Administration and Extension, Information
Services, FAs, Rural Training Centers, Home Economics, and Minor Settlement
Schemes.
District Administration and Extension
31.       The District Administration is set forth in Chart WB16926.  The
present Extension effort is an essential component of development programs
which are themselves good vehicles to carry on extension.
Farmers Associations
32.       Five FAs offering a range of services-input supply (credit, marketing,
consumer goods, extension) have been established since 1971 in the more inten-
sive farming areas. Produce marketing has been the main activity supported by
supply of consumer goods and chemicals. The FAs are organized by the Extension
Branch of DOA which supplies the staff, a loan for a revolving fund of M$50,000
and transport. Government policy is to develop the FAs as the main thrust of
future agricultural development amongst smallholders.
33.       The five area FAs consist of 79,347 acres of which about 33,770 acres
were planted with padi, rubber, coconut, fruits, maize, and other annual crops.
In October 1974, they had 3,792 members. The FAs are a major vehicle through
which extension in agriculture would operate. Nine more FAs would be organized
under the project.
34.       The FAs are really quasi-government organizations which enjoy semi-
monopolistic privileges. The Sabah Government provides the FA with staff, the
office building, vehicles, farm machinery, etc. and does control competition
to the FA by withholding a license from would-be competitors. Further, the
DOA appoints the General Manager of the FA and one third of the Board of
Directors of the FA.
35.       Chart WB17601 shows the organization of the FA system within the
extension branch. While all FAs have made profits in some years, four of the
five associations have lost money in at least two of the six years (see Table 4).
Increased emphasis on management would help resolve financial problems.
The Sabah Padi Board
36.       The primary aim of the Sabah Padi Board is to improve the socio-
economic conditions of the rice growers by:



ANNEX 2
Page 11
(a) encouraging farmers to practice modern methods of rice cultivation,
i.e. farm mechanization, proper use of farm machinery and farm
inputs, and post-planting husbandry;
(b) increasing farm production by using short-term HYVs in areas with
adequate irrigation and drainage facilities; and
(c) stabilizing market prices for padi produced in Sabah for the
local market.
The Padi Board confines its activities to the main rice growing areas, namely
Kudat, West Coast and Interior Residencies. The Board has staff based at
Kudat, Bandau, Kota Belud, Tuaran, Penampang, Papar Beaufort, Tenom, Keningau,
Tambunan and Ranau, in these areas with offices, stores, staff quarters
and transport. Rice schemes would be expanded during the TMP.
Rubber Fund Board
37.       The Rubber Fund Board restricts its activities to the main rubber
growing areas, with extension officers based in Tambunan, Keningau, Beaufort
and Tuaran to cover these and other contiguous districts.
38.       Its objectives are:
(a) raising the rubber smallholder's income by increasing the produc-
tivity of his smallholdings;
(b) ensuring adequate marketing facilities for the smallholders, and
providing them with "know-how" on modern methods of rubber cultiva-
tion and processing;
(c) training unemployed youths in rural areas as rubber tappers;
(d) increasing employment opportunities through the sound exploitation
of existing rubber acreages; and
(e)  expanding the production of technically specified rubber (SMR).
39.       Facilities for the training of youths as rubber tappers are pro-
vided at the Board's Estate at Sungei Damit, and employment opportunities
would be provided by rehabilitating abandoned areas of moribund rubber and
converting them to productive rubber schemes.
40.       Where the Board encourages the planting of other crops, the exten-
sion work is done by DOA. The Board also maintains a close liaison with the
FAs which organize the purchasing of rubber on behalf of the Board within their
organic areas. In these areas, the FAs carry out extension work for rubber.



ANNEX 2
Page 12
The Project
41.       The project would finance the development of state extension services
within the context of the existing organization of the State Department of
Agriculture (Chart WB16925), and the Department of Veterinary Services (Chart
WB16930). The project would cover all crops and livestock and focus on the
poverty groups of smallholders and shifting cultivators.
42.       The objectives of extension would be to assist farmers to mobilize
their resources by employing new and improved techniques at an optimum level
to increase production and income. To accomplish this, extension would estab-
lish effective communications with the rural community by frequent contact
between extension workers and farmers. An effective extension service must
therefore have adequate numbers of informed workers in the field. An element
of paternalism would be utilized and accepted in the relatively undeveloped
agricultural situation of Sabah where the extension worker would take the
initiative and go to the farmer because the farmer would not go to the exten-
sion service until it has become well established and known. This would
require: (a) strengthening extension services in numbers, quality, and support-
ing services and facilities; (b) better organization; (c) better management;
(d) better training; and (e) better planning of priorities. To meet these
objectives and priorities, the project would provide:
(a) Staff - an increase of 129 extension staff (100 JAAs [Table 5], 25
AAs and 4 AOs). Table 3 shows staff, housing and location. FA staff
would be increased to a total of 70; 5 for each of the 14 FAs (9 FAs
to be financed under the project). No additional home eonomics staff
would be recruited. Instead, emphasis would be placed on training
and upgrading the existing 18 home demonstration HDs and 72 AHDs.
(b) Civil Works - staff housing (63 units) and quarters, offices and
stores and all the buildings and facilities for 9 FAs (see Annex 2,
Tables 1 and 2 for numbers, location and implementation schedule).
(c)  Transport - 4 WD vehicles, trucks, motorcycles, boats and motors
(see Annex 2, Table 1 for numbers, distribution and implemen-
tation schedule).
(d) Equipment - extension kits, laboratory equipment, etc.
(e) Fellowships - 32 man-years for short-term study tours, degrees and
postgraduate degrees.
(f) Consultants - 2 man-years for extension consultants.
(g)  Training - in-service training facilities (RETDC) in conjunction
with the Agricultural Vocational School (AVS), and strengthening
of the Farm Mechanization Training Unit, all at Timbong Menggaris
north of Kota Kinabalu (Annex 5).



ANNEX 2
Page 13
Project Component Financing
43.       The project would assist GOM with financing the foreign exchange com-
ponent (US$2.65 million), of which US$0.48 million is civil works, US$0.80 mil-
lion is vehicles and equipment, and US$0.47 million is technical assistance
and fellowships. Physical contingencies are US$0.17 million, and price
contingencies are US$0.83 million. All capital costs (including additional
extension staff for the purposes of the project) would be borne by the
Federal Government. Extension (other than in-service training) is a state
function; thus operating costs would be borne by the state. Sabah extension
component financial details are contained in Annex 6, Section A and are
summarized in the table on page 14.
State Organization and Management
44.       The project would be implemented through the existing Government
channels (Chart WB16924). The Director of Agriculture would be the Project
Manager responsible for all aspects of project implementation including those
undertaken by DVS. He would be responsible to the National Project Director
at the Federal level only insofar as the project is concerned.
45.       The Extension Branch would be responsible for all Project implementa-
tion activities and it is envisaged that an area approach would be adopted,
using the district minor development scheme, and FA organic area basis. During
the first year the project would concentrate on 5 Districts, the remaining 13
districts, primarily with sedentary agriculture, would be strengthened in the
subsequent 4 years.
District Level Organization
46.       The District Agricultural Officer (AO) would be responsible for all
DOA extension branch and home economics activities except those involving dis-
trict FA staff activities in the district. He would have three types of staff:
(a) Extension staff, almost totally involved with giving advice and
conducting demonstrations at kampong level. Where there are FAs, the
JAAs (Extension) would be deployed to serve these organic areas, on
the basis of 1:500 farm families. Other JAAs (Extension) would
be used for servicing areas outside the FA organic area on the same
basis; farmers in the organic areas but not members of the FAs would
also be serviced. Staff deployed in the FA organic areas would be
supervised by AAs, AAOs and AOs outside the FAs, but would liaise
closely with FA management. Soil conservation and farm mechaniza-
tion activities would be developed as a normal function of extension.
(b) Administrative staff responsible for land inspections, subsidies,
pest and disease control campaigns and other administrative duties.
(c) Home Economics.
DOA staff assigned to FAs would assist them with other administrative duties,
credit input, supply and marketing activities. They would be responsible
through the FA General Manager to the AO (FAs) in DOA Headquarters.

District Staff Dealing with Extension
47.  (a)  JAAs deployed in FA organic areas would service 5-10 Small Agri-
cultural Units (one SAU is usually a kampong) on a regular basis.
Their contact point would be the SAU chief who is usually a progres-
sive farmer elected by the farmers themselves. They would visit each
SAU on a given day once a fortnight when they would carry the relevant
extension message for the season. They would also attend the weekly
market.
(b) JAAs not deployed in FA organic areas would work in a similar manner
except that they would organize less formal farmers' groups and work
through a contact farmer who would usually be the village headman.
AAs
48.       AAs would supervise all JAAs on a basis of 2-3 JAAs:l AA. They
would spend much of their time with their JAAs, meeting them on a fixed day
fortnightly to provide the appropriate extension message and undertake field
demonstrations and trial plots. They would spend most of their time in the
field.
AAOs
49.       AAOs would be responsible for all non-FA affairs of a subdistrict
and would spend about 30-50% of their time supervising the subdistrict exten-
sion effort, and helping to organize programs and training sessions.
AOs
50.       AOs would spend around 30% of their time overseeing the District
extension effort; their main role would be coordinating all Extension Branch
work within subdistricts and assisting with the coordination between agencies.
Organization and Operation of the Department of Veterinary Services
51.       This Department is newly established and until recently was a branch
of the Department of Agriculture. At Headquarters, there is a Director assisted
by two Senior Veterinary Officers, one responsible for administration and
Veterinary Services and the other for the development of research and production
of cattle and pastures.
52.       There is a Veterinary Officer at Residency level, with AVOs under
him at District level; field-level extension work is carried out by the JAHA.
53.       The Veterinary Department has officers in each district, with
an office and staff in most subdistricts. While they mainly extend a service
for disease control, vaccination and treatment, a considerable amount of exten-
sion work on animal husbandry is also done. The Department would also serve as
a source for the supply of improved breeding stock, day-old chicks from the
Likas Poultry Unit, breeding pigs from Tuaran, Sandakan, Kudat and Tenom as well
as artificial insemination service from Tuaran and Sandakan. Goats are supplied



ANNEX 2
Page 16
from Sandakan, Kudat and Bongawan and cattle from livestock stations at Sebrang,
Rosok and Tawau. A considerable effort of the extension service, particularly
in the Interior, would be on the improvement of grazing reserves. This would
include fencing the reserves with the Department providing the material and the
farmers who use the reserves doing the work. Whereas the main effort would be
in the Interior, work would also be carried out in Beaufort, Papar, Kota Belud
and Ranau Districts. The project provides for an increase in staff, facilities
and equipment to strengthen and expand these extension activities.
Extension Methodology
54.       Regularization of contact between extension workers and farmers is
important in establishing an effective program. Each month, the contact exten-
sion worker would visit every village within his area at least twice, spending
three or four days a week on such visits. He would plan to spend every Saturday
morning in his office making up his diary and completing paper work. He would
spend one day a week looking after special projects, such as demonstration
plots. In addition, most districts have a "Tamu" or market day at least once
a week, when all the surrounding farmers come to town to sell their produce and
meet their friends. The extension worker would make a point of being at the
Tamu where he would contact the farmers he has been unable to see on his routine
visits, and, more important, the farmers can contact the extension worker.
55.       While the front-line extension worker is performing his duties, the
AAO and ALDO would also be visiting each of the subdistricts checking up on
the work being carried out in the field. They would also visit a different
subdistrict each day for three or four days of the week, often accompanying
the extension worker on his visits to the kampongs.
56.       When visiting a village, the extension worker would first make contact
with the village headman. This is necessary both from a protocol point of view,
as by ignoring the headman, he may find that on future visits, all doors are
closed to him, and because the headman knows everything that is happening within
the tight circle of the village community and is the best person to give informa-
tion on the agricultural activities of his villagers. He would then visit some
of the village smallholdings, talk to the farmers themselves, and give agricul-
tural advice. Before leaving, he would inform the villagers of the date and
time of his next visit, so that they will be expecting him.
57.       The problem of the Extensions' close liason with the Subsidy Schemes
are not as overburdening in Sabah as in some other parts of Malaysia. These
subsidy schemes do give an entry to Extension work and a demonstration impact
to Extension.
58.       Subject matter Extension specialists based at the Research Center
would not be necessary because of (a) the embryonic stage in development of
the Extension program, and (b) work done at the Research Headquarters is all
of an applied problem-oriented nature and the researchers are expected to do
the necessary Extension backstopping work in their field. While in some states
this would not be possible it should work in Sabah as the Research organization
is under the direction of the Director of Agriculture who is also in charge
of Extension through his assistant director for Extension.



ANNEX 2
Page 17
59.       In districts where there are Farmers' Associations, the area is
divided up into small agricultural units (SAUs). Each SAU consists of one large
village, or a number of small adjacent villages. The members of the FA in each
SAU elect one farmer to be the SAU Chief. He is not necessarily the headman,
but is generally a progressive farmer who gets on well with his neighbors, and
is able to obtain their cooperation. This man would be contacted by the FA's
Extension Assistant when he visits the area. A set program would be made out so
that the contact man knows when he may expect a visit from the Extension Assist-
ant and would make himself available at the given time and place. Before the
visit, he would inform the other SAU members of the FA so that any of them with
problems can attend.
60.       The organization of the Extension Branch from Headquarters down
to field level is shown in Chart WB16926. The project would be supervised by
the Assistant Director (Extension) from Headquarters. He would give instructions
to the SAOs in charge of Residencies as to what crops should be emphasized in
their areas, availability of funds, additional staff, and so on. The SAO would
call regular meetings of all his District AOs and area staff (AAOs and AAs) to
pass these instructions on to them and to discuss the best methods of implementa-
tion. The District AOs would then be responsible for implementation. They
would set targets for all their AAs in charge of subdistricts or areas, and
maintain constant supervision, with the help of their AAOs and ALDOs to ensure
that close liaison is maintained with the farmers and the extension tasks are
efficiently carried out. The AAs and JAAs would be in direct contact with the
farmers.
61.       Each AA or JAA would have an area allocated to him, and it would be
his task to ensure that he knows the head of every farm family in his area,
the location of their farms, what crops are being planted, and what their
problems are, both agricultural and social. He would visit regularly each area
under his control. His first contact would be with the village headman of
each farming community. He would hold regular meetings with the headman and
with the other senior villagers to talk over their problems and to put forward
any Government views on agriculture, such as pushing the production of sorghum,
or stressing the desirability of intercropping coconuts with cattle or other
crops. He would also advise on technical matters such as planting distances,
best manurial methods, pest and disease control, soil suitability (with assist-
ance from the Research Soils Section) and so on. In addition, he would listen
to any problems the farmers have, and if they are beyond his technical ability,
he would raise them with his AO. The AO would give the necessary advice, or
would refer the matter back to Headquarters to be dealt with by the AD(E) or his
staff, or if necessary, referred to the Research Branch. The answer would then
be handed down through the same channels.
Farmers Associations
62.       The Sabah state government is fully committed to FAs as the main
vehicle through which agricultural programs, aimed at raising small farmers'
incomes, are to be executed. With these policy directives in mind, the project
would not only strengthen the extension services of the SDOA but also finance
the establishment of nine additional FAs. While accommodating the priority
claims of the FAs' influence zones, the DOA would also provide intensified
extension services to adjacent areas.



ANNEX 2
Page 18
63.       Unlike Peninsular Malaysia, where the Farmers' Organizations have
been separated from the DOA and are now separate entities under the Farmers'
Organization Authority, the FAs in Sabah would remain an integral part of the
DWA. The staff of the FAs in Sabah are members of the Extension Branch of the
Deparment. Chart WB16929 shows the organization of the FAs within the Extension
Branch.
64.       Three FAs would be established at Kudat, Lahad Datu and Semporna in
the first three years of the project. The following year a further three FAs
would be set up at Tuaran, Penampang and Beaufort, with an additional three
being established during 1979/80 at Tambunan, Kinabatangan and Labuk. The
project would finance staff, equipment, and office and storage facilities for
the FAs.
65.       The details of these proposed FAs are as follows:
No. of     Total no. of
Area              villages       farmers           Crops to be dealt with
Kudat                -             13,073       Coconut, rubber, padi, maize
Lahad Datu           29             5,565       Coconut, rubber, cocoa, padi
Semporna             78             3,655       Coconut, rubber, cocoa, maize
Tuaran              199             6,682       Coconut, rubber, cocoa, maize
Penampang            24             4,814       Rubber, padi, maize
Beaufort            101             8,575       Coconut, rubber, padi, maize
Tambunan            238             7,254       Rubber, padi, maize
Kinabatangan         60             2,076       Coconut, rubber, padi
Labuk                62             5,384       Coconut, rubber, cocoa, padi
66.       The numbers of farmers indicated in the above figures are those for
the entire District. About 45-50% of the farmers in the Kudat, Tambunan and
Labuk districts and 60-70% of those within the other districts live inside these
organic areas.
67.       Each FA would be provided with an office/store, quarters for the
staff, one Landrover, and one five-ton truck. New FAs would be started with a
staff which includes a General Manager, an Accounts Chief (who will also double
as Credit Chief), an Economic Chief (who will also carry out the duties of
Extension Chief) and two EAs.
Minor Settlement Schemes
68.       Major Settlement Schemes are the responsibility of the Sabah Land
Development Board while the Department of Agriculture Extension Branch has
jurisdiction over the Minor Settlement Schemes.



ANNEX 2
Page 19
69.       There are 84 minor schemes (one of the most successful aspects of
land development) to assist both farmers who lack resources and the landless
and shifting cultivators to obtain titles and develop land. These farmers are
supplied with seedlings, fertilizers, chemicals, etc., and are shown the best
methods of developing and caring for their land and crops.
70.       Minor Settlement Schemes are designed to help groups of at least
10 settlers with preference going to groups of around 50 or more individuals and
longhouse communal groups.
71.       There are now 45,298 acres planted under minor development schemes
and more than 20,000 acres should be added under the Minor Settlement Schemes
during the project period. Under the TMP these development schemes would
require an investment of M$9.0 million.
72.       These Minor Settlement Schemes are aimed at the eradication of rural
poverty. There are 7,825 families actually engaged in new schemes under develop-
ment, with another 5,976 families benefitting from schemes already completed.
Thus 10% of the rural population of Sabah are benefitting from the schemes. The
goal of these schemes is to assist settler farmers earn at least M$300 per month
when the scheme is in full production; some are already earning up to M$1,000
per month. The program is reasonably well managed and has high priority as a
channel of extension activities under the project.
Veterinary Department
73.       The Veterinary Department has officers in each district and an
office and staff in most subdistricts (see Chart WB16930).  While veterinary
extension provides a service for disease control, vaccination and treatment, a
considerable amount of extension work on animal husbandry and pasture development
is also done. The Department also serves as a source of improved breeding stock.
A considerable effort of the extension services, particularly in the Interior,
is expended on the improvement of grazing reserves. The project would provide
staff, quarters, equipment and training to strengthen extension activities of
the Department of Veterinary Services. The main effort has been in the interior,
but also at Beaufort, Papar, Kota Belud and Ranau Districts.
Soil Conservation
74.       The Soils Division is responsible for the provision of soil services
through the Extension Branch; however, very little attention has been given to
soil conservation in Sabah, in spite of the obvious detrimental effects of
shifting cultivation and uncontrolled logging operations. The State Department
of Agriculture has recognized these problems and plans to strengthen its soil
conservation extension efforts by training extension staff, encouraging sedentary
agriculture, and controlling shifting cultivation and logging operations. Soil
conservation would thus be developed as one of the activities of the extension
service. Fellowships would be utilized to strengthen expertise in the field,
and an immediate effort would be made to familiarize selected extension officers



ANNEX 2
Page 20
with known conservation techniques that would be applied immediately. This
knowledge would be obtained through short visits and study tours to areas in
the region where soil conservation techniques are being effectively applied.
Home Economics and Home Demonstration Extension
75.       The staff of the Home Demons.tration Section consists of two AAOs,
18 HDs and 72 JHDs. The equipment issued to them is inadequate and consists of
sewing machines, portable cooking stoves, cooking utensils, medical equipment
and scales. They have no transport, and have to rely on the goodwill of the
District Agricultural Officer to get them to their destinations. The project
would provide equipment and transport for home demonstrations. Home demonstra-
tion staff would be given periodic in-service training. No increase in staff
is envisaged under the project. Equipment, transport and training would
enable the Home Economics unit of extension to strengthen and expand their
activities in child care, family planning, hygiene, sanitation and improvement
of rural living conditions and rural life.
The Agricultural Information Service
76.       The functions of the Aricultural Information Service (equivalent to
ELU in Peninsular Malaysia and SELU in Sarawak) with a staff of 27 provide the
necessary agricultural information and research findings to the Extension Branch
Officers, home demonstrators and to the farmers. They also produce audio-visual
aids for use of the Education Branch institutions and give agricultural informa-
tion through the mass media. The aim of this section is to disseminate informa-
tion to the farmers that will increase their knowledge and so improve their
managerial skills and productivity. The AIS would play a major role in streng-
thening extension services under the project. The Agricultural Information
Service is organized as shown in Chart WB16928.
Agricultural Mechanization
77.       Shortage of farm power and draft animals is one of the major con-
straints to agricultural production in Sabah. In many areas of the state, land
development and production are directly related to the availability of tractors
for land preparation. The SDOA provides mechanization services through the
FAs, and encourages the private ownership of tractors. Mechanization has
been costly and inefficient because of the shortage of mechanical skills and
inadequate supporting infrastructure. The Department of Agriculture proposes
to relieve this constraint and protect the large investment (estimated
to be more than M$5.0 million by the DOA alone) in machinery by improving
training facilities for operators and mechanics and providing mechanization
extension services under the project.
78.       The project proposes to alleviate the mechanization constraint and
waste of resources by financing the establishment of a Farm Mechanization
Training Unit in association with the Agricultural Vocational School and RETDC
(see Annex 5). It would also provide the services of a mechanization extension
specialist to advise and assist the training unit and the farm machinery units
operated by tihe FAs and the Padi Board.



ANNEX 2
Page 21
Training Component
79.       The training component would comprise mostly the expansion of the
following facilities at Timbang Menggaris north of Kota Kinabalu:
(a) The Agricultural Vocational School (AVS) for 3 years' preservice
training with a capacity (total) of 180 students would be completed
and a 300 acre farm established;
(b) The Agricultural Training Center (ATC) currently in aged facilities
at Tuaran, near Kota Kinabalu, equivalent to an RETDC and used
for in-service training with a capacity of 60 students per course
would be re-established;
(c) A Farm Mechanization Training Service for tractor drivers with
a capacity of 10 per course of 3 months would be established.
80.       The AVS completion would involve staff quarters, an improved road
network, school equipment, library, transport, farm equipment, and telephone
services. The ATC would comprise a classroom block, 2 student hostels, staff
quarters and services, and the FMTC an office/workshop, staff quarters and
machinery.
Rural Training Centers
81.       There are two Rural Training Centers to provide farmer training, one
located at Tamu Darat, near Kota Belud, and the other at Damai, in the Kota
Marudu District of Kudat. There are also two centers for specialized crops at
Ulu Dusun Oil Palm Research Station and Quoin Hill Cocoa Research Station. The
program and facilities of these centers are inadequate and would be renovated
and upgraded during this project. The project would provide new classrooms,
hostels, staff quarters, training of staff and training materials (see Annex 5.)
Fellowships
82.       The project would provide for 32 man-years of fellowships to upgrade
the operational and management ability of agricultural services in Sabah.
These fellowships would provide for specialized overseas study tours, degree
and postgraduate degree training and would be complementary to the relatively
large local training component.
83.       Management and subject matter specialists are considered to be the
key to extension throughout Malaysia, thus fellowships would serve to relieve
these constraints. In addition, fellowships would provide the mobility and
incentives for career development within the agricultural services of the DOA.
The categories of specialization being considered are (a) extension methodology,
(b) agricultural mechanization extension, (c) general agriculture (crops),
(d) plant protection, (e) farm management, (f) soil conservation, and (g) soils
technology.



ANNEX 2
Page 22
Technical Assistance
84.       The project would provide 4.25 man-years of technical assistance
for Sabah. Four years would be utilized for extension and 0.25 years for
seeds (Annex 4). Short-term organizational technical assistance would be
required but the most urgent requirement is for farm mechanization extension
now being financed under a bilateral program scheduled which terminated early
in 1977.  Terms of reference would be generally as follows:
(a)  Mechanization Extension Specialist.  To assist in practical training
for farm machinery operation and maintenance, to advise the FAs,
Padi Board, and farmer owners in the selection, application and
maintenance of farm machinery.
(b) Extension Specialist. To advise and assist the Department of Agri-
culture in all aspects of extension organization, operation and
management.
Benefits and Justification
85.       The major benefits and justification for this project would be in
the establishment of an infrastructure for an effective extension service
which will contribute to increasing agricultural production and farmers
incomes on a continuing basis. Although it can be expected that many farmers
will obtain a substantial increase in production and income during the term
of the project, considering the level of agricultural development in the
State, the general benefits will be obtained over a much longer period. The
following examples are indicative of what could be achieved with the major
crops of coconut and padi.
86.       At present, a typical coconut smallholder with (say) 8 acres of
coconuts will produce about 4 tons of copra per year, which will give him
an income of approximately M$1,280 per year, or slightly over M$100 per
month, at current prices. With proper manurial practices, constant weeding,
and proper care, his yield could be increased by some 60%, giving him an
income of a little over M$2,000 per year. If, in addition to this, he could
be persuaded to interplant his coconuts with cocoa, he would eventually be
harvesting around 1,000 lb of dried beans per acre, or a total of 3.5 tons
per year, which would increase his income to a total of about M$8,000 per
year.
87.       With padi farmers, the adoption of new techniques and the use of
the newer, short-term HYVs will roughly double their incomes. If they can
also be persuaded to double crop (dependent on water availability), this
figure can be doubled again.
88.       To discuss the actual number of farmers who may be affected by
this project, and what their increase in income would be, is rather like
discussing the length of a piece of string. However, there is no doubt at
all that this project would benefit a very substantial number of farmers in
Sabah, or that the increase in production could be anything from 25-100%.

MALAYSIA
NATIONAL EXTENSION PROJECT
Sarawak - Extension Component
Introduction
1.        In Sarawak, the extension component project concept closely parallels
that of the mainland project. In designing the Sarawak subproject, considera-
tion was given to the state's large geographic area and severe communications
problems. Use of the state's very limited manpower resources in agriculture
must be maximized while taking into account the needs of the more depressed
areas of the state. Accordingly, the state's Department of Agriculture (DOA),
working closely with field staff, identified priority areas for concentrating
extension efforts. These priority areas reflect both the potential for growth,
incidence of poverty, and also provide a representative cross section of the
state's major farming types.
2.        Measures to reinforce the extension services would be taken to ensure
better communication between extension agents and farmers, and ensure greater
staff mobility.
3.        The severe staff constraint calls for special efforts to generate
the required output of front-line extension staff by providing preservice
training facilities. The utilization of such facilities would be maximized by
merging them with facilities to provide in-service training for staff, so that
new technology can be taught on a continuing basis; the project proposes to do
this (see Annex 5).
4.        The overall project proposal includes a small component specifically
designed to tackle soil conservation, seeds and in-service training problems as
an integral part of extension services on the mainland and in Sarawak. The
build-up of state extension services necessitates a corresponding strengthening
of the technical support services to effectively backstop all extension efforts.
Background
5.        Sarawak, lying between latitudes 0050' and 50 north and longitudes
109030' and 115040' east, covers a total land area of 47,000 sq miles (almost
40% of Malaysia).
6.        Sarawak has three roughly parallel geographic zones - coastal, midland,
and hilly interior - which are traversed by numerous wide, meandering rivers.
Four major soil types are distinguishable: hilly, red-yellow podzolic; rolling,
red-yellow and grey white podzolic; past and recent alluvial; and swamps, saline
grey and grey-white podzolic. Sarawak experiences heavy rainfall of 120-160"
per year, high humidity, and mean temperatures of 85 degrees Fahrenheit. From
October to March, the northeast monsoon brings the "landas" season of heavy
rains, strong winds and stormy seas.



ANNEX 3
Page 2
7.        The nature of the terrain and soil types imposes severe limitations
on large-scale agricultural development. Cultivable land is estimated at
less than 30% of the total land area and excludes the poorly-drained coastal
swamps and the infertile soils of the highlands. There are about 160,000
farm families on an estimated 6.7 million acres (from the 1966 planimetric
measurement). Some sources estimated that about 20% of the state's land area
is farmed under shifting cultivation, which has resulted in a serious reduction
in soil fertility, especially since population pressure decreased the fallow
period to 5-6 years instead of the traditional rotation of 12 years; a major
effort of land development schemes and extension is aimed at-encouraging shift-
ing cultivators to adopt sedentary agriculture. The problems of agricultural
development are also compounded by a complex land tenure system based on the
1958 Land Code which divides land into the following types: Mixed Zone
(about 10%), Native Area (5%), Native Customary (over 20%), Reserved (State),
and Interior Area land (not under title).
8.        Traditional crops in Sarawak are rubber ("kampong" type), pepper,
coconuts, padi and sago. Details of the area farmed, production and exports
are given in the following table. Existing mean yields are estimated at:
13,000 lb of green berries/acre for pepper; 340 lb sheet rubber/acre for
rubber; 2-3 pikuls copra-equivalent/acre for coconut; 1,515 lb/acre for wet
padi; 696 lb/acre for hill padi; and 1.2 tons flour/acre for sago. Much
higher yields could be achieved with improved farming practices. Prices
differ greatly between regions and in most cases comparisons are meaningless
due to the lack of standardized grading criteria. Credit, supply and marketing
services are most commonly provided to the rural population by the rural
shopkeepers (retailers), family enterprises which undertake the triple
functions of marketing, merchandising and money-lending. The project would
provide six months of technical assistance to review credit systems and
requirements with the objective of establishing regular institutionalized
credit.



ANNEX 3
Page 3

ANNEX 3
Page 4
9.        .rop combinations and incomes vary between the five major farm types
associated th the geographical regions of Sarawak. Poverty groups exist among
all farm types, usually ir remote areas which depend heavily on traditional
subsistence agriculture and are thus subject to the vagaries of weather, limited
pest control, limited technology, and lack of alternative incomes. The wide
distribution of poverty areas necessitates an agricultural extension program
which would eventually have statewide impact.
10.       Agriculture is the mainstay of the economy, employing about 80% of
the economically active population and contributing about 17% to GDP. In 1974,
primary exports were valued at M$1,385.9 million and imports at M$1,005.3 mil-
lion. Agricultural exports amount to 12.1% (1974 statistics) of principal
exports; important nonagricultural exports are petroleum and its products
(68.8%), and timber (12%).  The main agricultural exports are pepper, rubber,
coconut products and sago flour; statistics for 1974 show that pepper
exports were valued at M$102.9 million compared to M$50.4 million for rubber,
M$7.9 million for coconut products and M$7.1 million for sago flour.
11.       The population of one million is concentrated in the coastal and
riverine zones. Average population density in Sarawak is 20 persons per sq
mile, the lowest in Malaysia and one quarter of the national average. Major
ethnic groups are Iban (31%), Chinese (30%), Malay (19%), Land Dayak (9%),
Melanau (5%) and other indigenous groups (6%).
Existing Organization of the Sarawak Department of Agriculture
12.       The Ministry of Agriculture is divided into two departments, the
largest of these being the Department of Agriculture (DOA). The organization
of the DOA is shown in Chart WB16936. It is headed by the State Director of
Agriculture who is assisted by his Deputy and the heads of the various
branches. There are five main branches of the DOA (Sarawak), the first
four headed by Assistant Directors (Field Branch, Research Branch, Education
Branch, Veterinary and Animal Husbandry Branch) and the fifth by the Senior
Fisheries Officer (Inland Fisheries and Aquaculture Branch).
13.       Most of the DOA's extension services are handled by the Field and
Education Branches (Chart WB16937); other agricultural advisory services are
centered on the land development schemes, agricultural subsidy schemes, and
the home economics program. The livestock schemes are supervised by the
Veterinary and Animal Husbandry Branch and the inland fisheries schemes by
the Inland Fisheries Branch.
14.       The DOA has offices and staff in all 7 administrative divisions and
26 districts of the state.
15.       District Organization.  Each district is divided into subdistricts and
these are further divided into smaller agricultural administrative areas (Chart
lB 16938). The Assistant Agricultural Officer (AAO) is in charge of the district
HQ and under him are the subject matter specialists (AA-Padi, AA-Veterinary, AA-
Pepper, AA-Fisheries etc.) who assist the area staff on specific technical matters.
Each subdistrict is supervised by an AA who has a number of Junior Agricultural
Assistants (JAAs), which are the same as ATs, and Home Demonstrators (HDs) who



ANNEX 3
Page 5
do the grass-roots extension work. The work of the area JAAs is mainly concerned
with implementing the subsidy schemes and providing extension advisory services
to farmers. In addition, they are responsible for selecting farmers from their
area for training at the Agricultural Training Centers (ATCs). Based at the
district HQ and subdistrict HQ are at least two HDs who have a fixed home
economics extension piogram for the subdistrict areas.
16.       The district and area staff, mainly JAAs and HDs, are in direct
contact with farmers at the grass-roots level. District organizL fion of the DOA
is shown below:
District Organization
AAO in charge of district
AA-Veterinary         AA-Padi
AA-Fisheries          AA-Pepper, etc.
AA i/c                        AA i/c                    AA i/c

JAA     JAA      JAA              JAA    JAA           JAA      JAA     JAA
HD_  _    HD     HD               HD      HD           HD      HD       HD
Policies and Objectives of the DOA
±7.       The agricultural development policy of the Sarawak DOA is committed to
development of the poverty and smallholder sectors. This policy in general aims
to improve the farm income of the smallholders through (a) improving their
agricultural practices, (b) introducing new technology, and (c) diversifying
their agricultural enterprises.
18.       At present the field services aimed at improving farmers' incomes tend
to concentrate heavily on the implementation of the various development and
subsidy schemes together with emphasis on agricultural extension. The overall
objectives of these development schemes are:
(a) to inject modern inputs, techniques and ideas with
the aim of raising productivity;
(b) to provide investment capital to farmers for them
to purchase modern inputs;
(c) to absorb part of the investment risk involved with
agricultural enterprises;
(d) to increase farmers' incomes.



ANNEX 3
Page 6
19.       The present agricultural service does not effectively relay improved
farming techniques to the farmers for a number of reasons: firstly, much of
the field workers' time is taken up by the administration of subsidy schemes
and enforcement of rules and regulations. Secondly, as the subsidy scheme
component is not separated from extension work, it is often doubtful whether
farmers adopt new practices out of conviction or just for material gain.
Lastly, being deeply involved in the implementation of these schemes, field
staff, who generally lack training in agricultural extension, tend to be
oriented more towards the administration of these schemes than the provision
of agricultural extension services; however, it is also recognized that a
large measure of the so-called subsidy program is in fact an essential
demonstration component of the extension function. The achievements of the
extension and subsidy programs would be reviewed as part of the proposed
Extension and Subsidy Monitoring Program that would be implemented under the
project.
20.       The DOA implemented various subsidy schemes under the Second
Malaysia Plan (SMP); new Proposals for the Third Malaysia Plan (TMP), 1976-80,
are summarized in Annex 3, Table 2.
21.       The DOA has other programs covering:  Farmer Training (,aith a
throughput of 750 rural youths per year); Farmers' Organizations (7 have been
established and 10 more are planned for the TMP); Youth Settlement (one
scheme under the SMP, another planned for the TMP); Farm Mechanization
(renamed Farm Modernization for the TMP) under which 247 units of tractors
and associated equipment would be made available for hire to farmers at
subsidized rates; and expansion of Agricultural Research. The DOA alone
spent M$79.67 million or 93% of its total allocation under the SMP; an
ambitious expansion program envisages the need for M$287.7 million in the DAP
period.
The Project
22.       In view of the weaknesses of the present agricultural services
provided by the Sarawak DOA, the project would reorient the existing extension
service to include more agricultural teaching and demonstration services
focused on selected regions. The change in emphasis from a subsidy scheme/
agricultural service to expanded education-oriented extension services would
generally involve (a) improving the quality and increasing the number of
extension staff; (b) introducing new, effective extension services at all
levels of the DOA without disrupting the present departmental structure; (c)
reorienting the functions of the field level organization (i.e. the district
and area agricultural centers) giving information, education, demonstration
and consultation, and (d) increasing the productivity and incomes of farmers.
particularly the smaller, more depressed and more remote farmers.
23.       These objectives would be achieved by:



ANNEX 3
Page 7
(a)  reorganizing relevant personnel in DOA into the State Extension
LJiaison Unit (SELU) which would become the operational arm of the
Departmental Steering Committee (DSC) dealing with extension matters;
(b)  establishing an Agricultural Institute (AI) with a capacity for 250
trainees and an annual output of 125 to provide two-year preservice
training for newly recruited front-line extension staff. The AI
will be developed in conjunction with the establishment of an RETDC
(see Annex 5);
(c) establishing a Regional Extension Training and Development Center
(RETDC) where new technology can be taught to extension and agricul-
tural service workers on a continuing basis;
(d) developing a network of 10 Extension Subcenters (ESCs) with 20 "area
forward bases" (Vol. II: Project File, Part I, Section A.3.1) to
establish better communications between extension workers and farmers;
(e) providing local and foreign training fellowships for extension workers
at all levels of the DOA;
(f) increasing the numbers of field level extension workers and thus
substantially improving the ratio of extension workers to farmers;
(g) establishment of an extension communications media unit;
(h)  expand seed production facilities to make improved seeds available
to most farmers.
Project Components
24.       The components would be incorporated into the overall structure of the
Sarawak DOA with minimal disruption of the organization. At the same time the
system is designed to permit continuous evaluation and change as the two-way
flow of action and feedback becomes operative. Such built-in flexibility will
permit the DOA and its components to change policies and modify programs in
response to changing needs. With clear-sighted policy and corresponding commit-
ment of decision-makers, DOA would be able to initiate development of a self-
reinforcing growth system, which gains its strength from its built-in mechanisms
for trial, implementation, evaluation and adaptation. The project would achieve
its objectives through the provision of the following:
(a) Staff. The project would provide a total of 284 staff. By components
this would include 131 for ESCs, 74 for the AI/RETDC, 8 for soil
conservation extension, 12 for extension communication and 6 subject
matter spe-ialists.



ANNEX 3
Page 8
(b) Civil Works. In order to place staff in the Districts and areas
wlhere they will have close and regular contact with the farmers,
the project would provide office space, staff quarters, storage
facilities and boat sheds at each of the 10 ESCs and 20 AFBs
(Annex 6, Section A).
(c) Equipment. The staff would also be supported by the provision of
transport - four-wheel drive vehicles, boats and motors; laboratory
equipment and extension equipment such as extension kits, plant
protection equipment and visual aid equipment (Annex 6, Section A).
(d)  Fellowships.   The project would provide 54 man-years of fellow-
ships to upgrade the knowledge and operational ability of the staff.
These fellowships would be utilized for short-term study tours and
specialist degree and postgraduate degree training.
(e) Technical Assistance. The project would provide 2.75 man-years of
technical assistance; 2 years for extension organization and opera-
tion and communications, 6 months for a credit specialist to review
the agricultural credit system and credit requirements, and 3 months
for seeds in Sarawak (refer to Annex 6, Part II, Section D, Table Dl).
The services of a credit specialist would be required early in the
project to evaluate existing credit facilities and make recommenda-
tions on how to regularize, strengthen and expand credit facilities,
both in the public and private sectors. He would pay particular
attention to the availability of adequate credit required for
smallholder agriculture development. His report would be submitted
to the Bank for review and comment.
Sarawak Extension Component
25.       The project would assist GOM with financing the foreign exchange
components (US$2.82 million), of which US$1.20 million is civil works,
US$0.27 million is vehicles and equipment, and US$0.42 million is technical
assistance and fellowships. Physical contingencies are US$0.16 million, and
price contingencies are US$0.77 million. All capital costs (including
additional extension staff for the purposes of the project) would be borne by
the Federal Government. Extension (other than in-service training) is a
state function; thus operating costs would be borne by the state. Details are
contained iD Annex 6, Section A and in the supplementary report No. 1555a-s-MA.



ANNEX 3
Page 9

Proposed Reorganization for Extension in the DOA
26.       State Level.  In order to strengthen the agricultural extension
service of the Sarawak DOA, the information delivery function (extension) would
be incorporated into the existing Field and Education Branches; these would be
renamed the Field Extension Branch and the Extension Education and Training
Branch respectively. Their proposed organizational structuse is shown in Chart
WBI7101.
27.       The Agricultural Institute (AI) would be involved in preservice
training of front-line extension workers and the RETDC in providing refresher
training courses; both would come under the existing Education Branch, while the
ESC dealing with farmers at the grass-roots level would be under the existing
Field Branch.
28.       The Veterinary and Animal Husbandry Branch and the Inland Fisheries
and Aquaculture Branch which deal more with technical matters would provide
support services to these two extension branches.
Organization of ESC and its Area Forward Bases
29.       As shown in Chart WB16936 and WB16938, the ESC would be an extension
component of the district HQ. With the addition of the ESC to the district HQ,
its functions would be: overall administration; extension (which includes its
services to the Farmers' Organizations); planning & evaluation, subsidy scheme
implementation, home economics extension, rural youth services, farmer training,
and veterinary services.
30.       In view of the size of the districts, the scattered nature of agricul-
ture, and the difficulties of communications in Sarawak, an ESC would have
"area extension forward bases" (AFBs). Each AFB would be in a location where
farmers and extension workers could conveniently contact one another. An AFB
would also be located at the district or subdistrict HQ to service nearby areas.
One AFB would serve one or more areas of the district depending on the size of
the areas, the ease of communications and the distribution of the villages.
31.       There are 26 districts in Sarawak, but initially only 10 ESCs would be
established with 20 AFBs, or 2 per district. The location of these ESCs and
AFBs together with the extension coverage ratios they would achieve (by number
of households, villages, population) are shown in the supplementary report.
32.       The AFB at Nanga Kesit would represent a typical forward base area.
The area can only be reached by river boat and is located about one hour upstream
from Ridan; the nearest point accessible by road. The area consists of 25
longhouses with 320 families within a three hour range by riverboat. Existing
facilities consist of a primary school and clinic. An AFB would be established
here to be staffed by one AA Extension, one JAA and one HD.
State Extension Liaison Unit
33.       The State Extension Liaison Unit (SELU) would be a unit of the Sarawak
DOA comprises the Director, Deputy Director and the Assistant Directors with



ANNEX 3
Page 11
connections to the federal ELU and other technical branches of the DOA. The
SELU would collate and present information received from these and other
sources in a form which can be used by extension agents. At the same time,
the SELU would also transmit back to these various branches information on
field problems faced by extension workers so that technical solutions could
be found.
34.       The SELU would be the agricultural extension unit of the Departmental
Steering Committee (DSC). The various sections of the DOA concerned with
communications and information, and planning and evaluation (handled by the
Planning Division) would extend their technical services to SELU.
35.       The SELU would have such functional units as (a) Communications,
(b) Project Planning and Studies, (c) Data Processing, and (d) Subject
Matter. The Assistant Director (Extension, Education and Training) would be
responsible for the activities of these functional units and would be a
member of the DSC. Units (a) and (d) above would be the responsibility of
the Communications and Information section of the Extension Education and
Training Branch, while units (b) and (c) would be the responsibility of the
DOA's Planning Division.
Technology Base for Extension
36.       The project would develop a close working relationship with the
Malaysian Agricultural Research Institute as the base for new technology
and backstopping support for the solution to local and seasonal crop problems.
The Extension Liaison Unit (ELU) established under Loan 1115-MA, as an
integral part of the Federal Extension Branch of the DOA would be the main
channel of communication between MARDI, DOA and the SDOAs. An additional
link, the State Agriculture Research Development Committee (SARDEC) has
already been established in some states to bring local problems to the
attention of MARDI.
37.       The Department of Agriculture and the mission recognize that the
extension project, in the short term (the next 5-10 years) would not be
constrained by the regular and continuing availability of new technology
emanating from MARDI and the Research Centers in Sabah and Sarawak.
38.       A tremendous gap still exists between known technology and that
being applied by the majority of farmers in Malaysia; therefore this project
would aim at narrowing the production gap primarily through the dissemination
of known and proven technology. New technology would replace and supplement
known technology as it becomes available.
The Agricultural Research Center (ARC) at Semongok and its Extension Output
39.       The project would link ARC's research efforts to the SELU, and
research officers from the Semongok ARC would then transmit new ideas and
discoveries to the extension workers. Research officers would also lecture
in certain specialized subjects at the AI and RETDC.



ANNEX 3
Page 12
40.       Discussions were hald with the Sarawak DOA and their agreement
was obtained on the necessity of these research-oriented extension officers.
These would be added incrementally during the project implementation period.
Extension officers proposed would be specialists in the following - which are
not necessarily in order of priority: crop protection, pepper agronomy,
vegetable horticulture, soils, fresh water fisheries and livestock.
Soil Conservation
41.       The Extension component of the project in Sarawak would establish
a soil conservation section jointly with the Soils Branch. A pilot soil
conservation scheme would be implemented in Serian District among pepper
growers where soil erosion is particularly severe because pepper is grown on
steep land without any concern about soil erosion. Soil conservation in
Sarawak would initially be strengthened by exposing extension and subject
matter officers to known techniques being applied in the region; fellowships
for study tours would provide needed information. Specific programs and
projects would be formulated as knowledge and expertise are developed; thus
soil conservation would primarily be concerned with training in the early
stages of the project.
Home Economics
42.       The Extension Service already has a fairly competent and well
organized home economics unit and provision is made to recruit 4 Home Economics
Supervisors and 26 Home Demonstrators under the project. Most important of
all, the project would provide the equipment and facilities to enable the
home economics unit to become more effective. The main activities of the
home economics unit are nutrition, child care, family planning, sanitation
and hygiene.
The Agricultural Institute (AI) and the Regional Extension Training ancd
Development Center (RETDC)
43.       The project proposes the establishment of an AI and an RETDC in
close proximity to the ARC at Semangok (see Annex 5 for details).
44.       Syllabuses for the two-year AI courses would be compiled for the
approval of the Departmental Education Board, subject to review as and when
necessary. Practical work would be done on the AI farm, and also at the
research farms and agricultural station nearby. The practical side of
learning would be stressed since extension agents are severely limited if
they cannot demonstrate to the farmers what they are trying to teach. At the
Al provision would be made to give students ample opportunity for practical
work. They would be split into groups of 5-6 for easy supervision and
assigned practical work related to their subjects, e.g. laying down nurseries,
bud grafting, etc. Students would also be expected to work in nearby agricul-
tural establishments - brackish water stations, pig and poultry farms, and
c'v.ni in research farms for such work as harvesting cocoa, etc. Another
effective form of practical work would be student cooperative farms. Each
group would be required to undertake a farm project related to their discipline
of studies, e.g. vegetable growing, poultry keeping, etc. Full records would



ANNEX 3
Page 13
be kept of time spent, inputs and outputs. Profits would go to the students.
This arrangement for practical work is perhaps the best, since it gives
students the opportunity to gain experience in managing farm businesses by
themselves.
The Regional Extension Training and Development Center (RETDC)
45.       The AI is intended to provide a preservice training program.  The
RETDC is to be a center for in-service training.
46.       The first RETDC would be connected to the AI.  This sharing of
facilities would save on capital investment, overheads and operating costs,
and would allow fuller utilization of teaching manpower and facilities. The
University Pertanian, the ARC, the Agricultural Station and the ATC are all
near this site.
47.       The RETDC would be a center where basic agricultural information
and new research findings would be passed on to the extension workers and
thence to the farmers. It would be an arm of the SELU with the functions of
(a) providing the extension workers with refresher courses on extension
methodology, and updating them on the latest research in crop husbandry, home
technology, soil conservation, farm mechanization, fisheries, animal husbandry
or farm management, to be supported by information on extension methods,
communications, journalism, data processing, documentation, rural sociology,
planning and evaluation methods; (b) project planning and evaluation; and (c)
improving communications between farmers and extension workers.
Agricultural Training Centers (ATCs)
48.       The ten ATCs in Sarawak provide two levels of training for young
farmers: seven "Junior" ATCs would take in young men and women with primary
school education and the other two ATCs would take young men with secondary
school education. The location of these centers is shown below:
