Extension Organization and Staffing Functions
49.       In the 10 selected districts the SAAU's job would be to coordinate
the activities of all the staff in his district. In additior, he would be
involved in planning, evaluation and general administration. Being the
officer in charge of the district, he would also be responsible for reporting
to his divisional HQ on the progress and problems of agricultural development
in his district, and receiving information and implementing instructions from
his divisional HQ.
50.       The district AAO (Extension) would mainly be responsible for
agricultural extension. He would also be involved in agricultural development
planning and evaluation as part of the overall extension effort in the
district. From time to time, he would go to the AFBs to give advice to
front-line extension workers and at the same time gather firsthand information
on problems in the field concerning extension. The field problems would
influence planning activities at the district HQ level.
51.       The district Subject Matter Specialists (SMSs) would give technical
advice to area extension workers and also front-line extension staff in the
Farmers' Organization, assisting them in planning and evaluation work for
their areas, and participating in agricultural development planning. The
district SMSs would be assisted by the divisional SMSs (VO, FO, AAO (Padi),
HES) who would visit them regularly.
52.       The SAAO, AAO (Extension) and the SMSs would work as a team to draw
up a district development plan, based on the feedback of farmers' needs from
the AFBs. District HQ staff would visit the areas of the district from time
to time to assist area staff and study field problems.
53.       In each AFB there would be one or more AAs (Extension), area JAAs
and area HDs. The number of front-line extension workers would depend on the
number of areas the AFB serves. In some cases, one AA (Extension) could
supervise two areas because of the small number of villages and better
communications in the areas; in others, one AA (Extension) could supervise
only one area.
54.       In the AFBs, the AA (Extension), the area JAA, and the area HD
should work together as a team to fix priority extension objectives and plan
the extension program for the area in line with the district HQ's development
plan. Based on their extension program they would 'qo work out the best
possible monthly travel schedule to ensure that the transport available is
efficiently used. The AAO (Extension) would assist them in working out
priorities for their -reas during the regular staff meetings at the District
HQ and in evaluating the success of their extension programs.
55.       In general, the activities of the extension team in the village
would be:
(a) helping f8rmeLs to identify their problems and needs and offering
suggestions and advice on farm management and home economics;



ANNEX 3
Page 15
(b) introducing new technologies and reinforcing technologies
already familiar to the farmers;
(c) securing participation and involvement of local leaders
in planning and implementing programs of change for farm
families;
(d) providing learning opportunities and farming experience
for adult farmers and rural youths;
(e) advising farmers on sources of farming inputs, credit and
marketing facilities;
(f) assisting rural committees in the planning and implementa-
tion of agricultural projects, and organizing farmers and
rural people in community development and self-help projects;
and
(g) planning, implementing and evaluating the extension
education programs for adult farmers, homemakers and
youths in rural areas.
56.       The AA (Extension) would primarily do extension work.  Although the
area JAA would be engaged on the implementation of subsidy schemes part of
the time, he would primarily be doing agricultural extension work. However,
to prevent duplication of work at the village level, their duties are divided
as follows:
Functions of the AA (Extension)
(a) Coordinating the provision of a package program, e.g.
improved techniques of padi production from land preparation
to harvesting.
(b) Working with and motivating groups of farmers in the villages
to adopt improved technology and practices, and suitable new
crops.
(c) Collecting base-line (and benchmark) socio-economic information
in the area.
kd) Holding meetings with farmers to give demonstrations and
discuss farm problems and needs.
(e) Identifying potential farmer leaders and recommending them
for traini-g at the ATCs.
(f) Organizing farm tours and demonstrating success achieved.
The AA (Extension) should work with the JAA (Area) whenever possible.



ANNEX 3
Page 16
Functions of the JAA (Area)
(a) Administration of agricultural subsidy schemes.
(b) Giving demonstrations and instructions to reinforce topics
dealt with by the AA (Extension) whenever modern inputs
(e.g. pesticides, weedicides, fertilizers, seeds, etc.)
provided under the subsidy schemes are being allocated to
selected farmers.
(c) Assisting the AA (Extension) in collecting information on
the farmers' problems.
Extension 1,ethodology
57.       Based on a field survey of actual needs and accessibility, the average
cc verage by one AA (Extension) would be 17 (536 i- 32 AA Extension) villages
consisting of 300 to 400 farm families. Traveling 4 days a week (i.e. Monday
to Thursday) and spending a night in each extension kampong, the AA (Extension)
could make his round of 17 villages in 1-1 1/2 months (i.e. about 4 villages per
week). The total number of working days is 299 per year. The average number of
days actually spent in extension activities at the village would be 15 days per
month. The months when farming activity slackens off (December, January and
February) would normally be used for scheduled leave and participation in train-
ing courses and seminars. Agricultural extension at the village level would be
based on the farm approach rather than on individual crops. Therefore, the
extension program should be synchronized with the work calendar of the major
crops.
58.       When he is not traveling, the AA (Extension) would be (a) at the
AFB writing reports on the progress of development in his area, holding meetings
at the AFB with the JAA (Area) and HDs to evaluate the progress of development,
plan the extension and travel schedule and consult with farmers; (b) attending
meetings at the district HQ to discuss problems and progress of development in
the area, and receiving advice on land technical matters and extension methodo-
logy; and (c) attending lectures on extension and agricultural subjects at the
district HQ (ESC) given by district or divisional HQ staff.
59.       The area JAA and HD should accompany the AA (Extension) on his visits
to extension villages whenever possible.  In each village, the amount of time
spent would be: 1/2 day + 1 night + 1/2 of the next day. On arrival the AA
(Extension) would be involved in visiting the farms to collect information on
the farmers' problems and assessing what progress has been made since the last
visit. He can take the opportunity of his farm visit to give on-the-spot advice
to the farmers. At night, the activities of the AA (Extension) would include
(a) discussing the farmers' problems and needs, and (b) giving talks to groups
of farmers on improved farming practices, using visual aids such as slide or
film shows. The information on farmers' problems gathered during the farm visit
and during these discussions would help the AA (Extension) work out the objec-
tives of the extension program and the specific objectives for his next visit.
The following morning, the AA (Extension) assisted by the area JAA may give a



ANNEX 3
Page 17
method demonstration to a selected group of farmers on an improved farming
practice e.g. fertilizer application to padi, application of Bordeaux mixture
to pepper, etc. (in these activities, he may occasionally be assisted by
district HQ staff).
60.       During his visits, the AA (Extension) should attempt to identify
innovative farmers with leadership potential in each village. Fifteen to
twenty such farmers could form the nucleus of an extension group; they could
attend short courses at the ATC and hopefully each would in turn spread his
knowledge to five or more neighboring farmers.
Benefits and Justification
61.       The current program is aimed mainly at priority areas, selected
on the criteria of their low and uncertain incomes and subsistence, high
dependence on traditional agriculture for livelihood, and hence, their urgent
need for agricultural extension services. The emphasis is more on reducing
uncertainty in food production and trying to achieve basic levels of self-
sufficiency. When these necessary conditions are satisfied, it will be
possible to attempt an enterprise-mix which enables commercial agricultural
production; this will depend on whether particular enterprises suit the farm
type and whether labor is available.
62.       The initial impact of the program will largely be increases in
local production, expected to be assimilated by the producers themselves
or marketed through the extensive network of rural shops. Cash crop culti-
vation will come after basic subsistence levels are satisfied.
63.       Primary level in marketing is largely in the hands of rural shop-
keepers; these are retail agents who operate in a multiple-function capacity
as retailers, creditors, and buyers of farm produce. They are in turn tied
to trading with their financiers at the second level, the wholesalers or
larger retail shops in towns, who are in turn virtually bonded by similar
credit obligations to the importers/exporters in the bigger towns.
64.       Marketing margins are difficult to compute, due to the multiple
activities of the single enterprise; agricultural produce is not graded to
any standard specifications, making meaningful price comparisons almost
impossible. However, a study of pepper marketing (Wee, 1972) indicated that
farmer prices tended to reflect exporters' prices at the end of the chain,
with marketing margins (for the same dealers) remaining fairly constant.
This makes projection of "likely" farmers' prices very difficult for now we
must contend not just with local variations in demand, supply, quality and
complex credit obligations, but also with the uncertainties of trading at the
buyers' end.
65.       The gradual expansion of the Cooperatives, Farmers' Organizations
and specialized agencies like the Federal Agricultural Marketing Authority
(FAMA), Pepper Marketing Board, and Lembaga Padi dan Beras Negara would bring
about better marketing arrangements and ensure more equitable incomes to
farmers; the likely increases would be largely conjectural and will be
wrought in transforming a traditional agriculture to one set firmly on the
road to modernization.



ANNEX 3
Page 18
Immediate Benefits
66.       Correcting the Present Heavy Bias of DOA Towards Subsidy Schemes.
The project would establish the staff and infrastructure for an effective
extension service and would correct the present bias of the DOA towards the
provision of subsidized material inputs to farmers. Evaluation studies have
indicated shortcomings of the "direct investment" approach and there is
inadequate evidence of significant improvements in the livelihood of farmers
in Sarawak.
67.       The Agricultural Extension program seeks to rectify these weak-
nesses and rationalize the whole process of aid to farmers. It emphasizes
the "package deal" of inputs (extension and advice, supply of farm inputs,
improvement of soil and environmental conditions, proper techniques and
marketing of output) which must work together. The system also provides for
continuous reassessment to help redefine our objectives in the light of
changing circumstances and needs.
68.       Productivity and Income Increases.  It is expected that the
heightened activity on the part of Research, Extension Education and Field
Extension Services of the DOA would produce concrete results by way of
increases in yields and incomes ranging from 25% to 100% depending on the
crop and soil conditions.
69.       The "breakthrough" in agricultural mentality should augur well
for expected productivity increases. A crude comparison of preliminary
figures showing disparity in yields between high- and lower-income farmers in
the same farm types indicates that yield potential of major crops could be
increased by as much as 25% for hill padi, 38% for pepper, 50% for wet padi,
27% for rubber, more than 100% for sago (at present growing in semiwild
conditions), and more than 100% for coconut. Each of these crops is often
the mainstay of farm families in providing whatever cash or food supplements
that may be obtained. With the direction of the extension effort being aimed
primarily at poverty areas, the potential for increases in productivity and
income of rural farmers - even if less than the figures above - should not be
underestimated.
Long-Term Benefits
70.       Spread effects from these "Centers of growth".  The targeted areas
for operation from the proposed 10 ESCs and their Forward Bases are but the
nuclei of the long-term extension program. The concentration of extension
resources and facilities in these centers would uplift them to the status of
demonstration centers and spark off the sociological process of "Diffusion of
Innovation" (Creating Awareness-->Interest-->Evaluation-->Trial and-->Eventual
Adoption).
71.       Towards Rationalizing the Whole Agricultural System.  The component
parts of the extension program and proposed structures have been designeA
both to correct the deficiencies of the present system and also to strengthen
and expand in the directions desired.

MALAYSIA
NATIONAL EXTENSION PROJECT
Seeds Component
Introduction
1.        The Government of Malaysia (GOM) has requested a Bank loan to help
finance improvements in its existing seeds industry for the multi-licaLi n
and distribution of improved seed to farmers as part oF an expanded and
strengthened extension service. The proposed component would make an impor-
tant contribution to the achievement of the GOM's current five-year develop-
ment plan (Third Malaysia Plan) which places emphasis on agriculture and
rural development.
Background
2.        General.  The strengthening of the agricultural research and
extension services of Malaysia should obviously be paralleled by an increase
in the production and distribution of improved seeds since the poor quality
and small quantity now available are a major constraint to increased crop
production. In particular, the poverty areas outside the major irrigation
project areas have received only very small inputs of improved seeds.
3.        The area devoted to padi totaled 1.48 million acres in 1974 of
which 58% was double cropped.  Output of rice was 1.17 million tons in 19729
1.27 million tons in 1973 and 1.41 million tons in 1974. Padi production has
been increased through the provision of irrigation, double cropping and
the use of improved varieties; however most of the improved seeds available
have been distributed in the irrigation development projects.
4.        Minor crops include maize, groundnut, soybean and sorghum.  The
growth of these crops is being encouraged, particularly in the poverty area->
under the national crop diversification program of the TMP in which the maJo-
thrust of agricultural development is directed towards food crops, animal
feed crops and fruit crops. The Crop Production Branch of the DOA is given
the responsibility of carrying out this program.
5.        Very little sorghum, a recent introduction in MIalaysia, is grown at
present but the targeted area for planting in Peninsular Malaysia by the
early 1980s is 10,000 acres. However, in order to achieve this target A
organized system of marketing would need to be developed, for the preseuL
demand is such that the returns from sorghum are low compared with o Lhef
crops.  In 1973 Peninsular Malaysia had about 14,000 acres under ground.im
5,000 acres under maize and 200 acres under soybeans: the targeted areas for
these crops in the early 1980s under the TIP are about 25,000 acres for



ANNEX 4
Page 2
groundnut and 10,000 acres each for maize and soybean. At present Sabah has
about 13,000 acres planted to maize, 1,700 acres to groundnut and only 85
acres to soybean, but under the TMP it is targeted that an additional 10,000
acre~. will be planted to maize, 1,000 acres to groundnut and 250 acres to
soybean.
6.        In Sarawak there are about 128,000 acres of wet padi, 153,000
acres of hill padi, 2,800 acres of maize, 1,300 acres of soybean and 1,000
acres of groundnuts. Only about 11,200 acres of padi land have drainage
or irrigation facilities of which 4,000 acres would be double cropped.
By the enid of 1980 an additional 10,000 acres of padi land would be provided
with irrigation/drainage while 20,000 acres of maize, 9,000 acres of soybean
and 8,000 acres of groundnuts are proposed to be planted; wet padi is
expected to increase to about 150,000 acres.
7.        Under the proposed seeds component the main emphasis would be given
to the production and distribution of improved seed of padi, together with
small quantities of maize, groundnut, soybean and, for Peninsular Malaysia
only, of sorghum.
Agricultural Research, Extension and Credit
8.        Research.  In Sabah agricultural research in all crops except
rubber is a function of the Research Branch of the DOA; there is only one
center for padi and other field crop research namely the Agricultural Re-
search Centre, Tuaran. The present research program at Tuaran includes the
assessment of nutrient requirements and investigations concerned with crop
husbandry practices and pest and disease control. No plant breeding work is
carried out but introduced varieties are tested under local conditions.
Foundation stock seeds are produced from selections of the introduced varie-
ties which mainly come from Peninsular Malaysia, the Philippines and Thailand
and also from selections of local varieties of padi. Although Sabah conducts
its independent research programs liaison is maintained with the Malaysian
Agricultural Research and Development Institute (MARDI).
9.        Before M4ARDI was established, crop research was also the responsi-
bility of the DOA in Peninsular Malaysia. MARDI was inaugurated in October
1969 and became operaLive in March 1971 with the transfer of a number of
staff and research stations from the DOA and the takeover of existing
research programs in rice, oil palm, coconuts, horticultural and field crops,
ane crop protection. The Act establishing MARDI gives it responsibility for
all  :ieicAfic, technical, economic and sociological research in Malaysia in
the  rod'  tion, processing and utilization of all crops except rubber.
10.       The recent allocation of a Bank loan in support of its activities
has involved reorgar 7ation and reorientation of research within MARDI. So far
the main emphasis ha~ been directed towards rice research, including: (a) the
selectionr and breedi;g of improved high-yielding rice varieties with short
straw, eirly maturity, disease and pest resistance, nonphotosensitivity and
improved milling and cooking qualities; (b) physiological studies on the growth
components affecting high yields such as lodging control, plant spacing and



ANNEX 4
Page 3
water requirement; (c) fertilizer trials; (d) crop husbandry practices such as
nursery procedures, depth of planting and age of seedlings; and (e) trials
concerned with disease and pest control.
11.       The three most popular HYVs of rice at present in use in Malaysia
are Bahagia, Mahsuri and Jaya. A number of new varieties of padi have recently
been released by MARDI and promising new selections have been identified which
not only have high yields but also good eating qualities and disease resistance.
Earlier rice breeding activities were based on the development of varieties with
wide adaptation but more recently emphasis has been directed towards varieties
suitable for local regional conditions. The most recent varietal releases of
Sri Malaysia 1, Sri Malaysia 2 and Pulut Malaysia 1, made in 1974, are making
slow headway in terms of farmer acceptance, partly because lack of adequate
extension led to their use in environments for which they are not suited.
12.       Only a small amount of work has so far been carried out by MARDI on
groundnut, maize, soybean and sorghum. The only improved variety of maize
presently in use in Malaysia is "Metro"; the main variety being grown is a
relatively low-yielding one. However, a large collection of maize varieties may
be expected towards the middle of the proposed seed project period.
13.       The nonavailability of a steady supply of quality groundnut seed
is a major constraint on expansion of crop acreage. At present almost
all of the seed supplied to smallholders is imported from Indonesia and
Thailand. Due to the persistant shortage of groundnut seeds farmers often
plant low-yielding varieties. Collection and evaluation of groundnut varie-
ties should be given priority by MARDI.
14.       No attempt has been made to introduce improved varieties of soy-
bean except for some limited testing of material from Illinois. No plant
breeding work has been carried out in Malaysia with sorghum.
15.       Because of the present unsatisfactory position with respect to
varietal availability of these crops the DOA proposes that, at least in
Peninsular Malaysia, seed production at the start of the project would
be confined to the production of improved padi seeds. The production of
seeds of groundnut, maize, sorghum and soybean would be introduced at the
end of the third year, when recommended varieties should be available from
MARDI.
16.       Extension.  An efficient extension service is needed to publicize
new varieties and demonstrate to farmers the advantages of new varietal releases.
The existing Federal and State extension services have, in the past, been unable
to give the necessary extension impetus largely because of the lack of suitably
trained staff and financial constraints.  Too often newly bred varieties of padi,
despite their proven yield superiority, have not been accepted by farmers for
some years following their release. The inadequacy of the existing extension
services in relation to the use of improved varieties is shown by the fact



ANNEX 4
Page 4
that, although only a small quantity of improved padi seed has been produced
in Peninsular Malaysia (page 7), often even this limited amount has not been
matched by farmer demand. Because of low quality a portion of the padi seed
produced by the DOA has remained unsold for planting purposes and has finally
been disposed of as milling grain.
17.       The project components to improve seed and strengthen Malaysia's
agricultural extension services would have a major impact on the acceptance
and use of seed of improved varieties.
18.       Institutional Credit.  The use of HYVs alone does not necessarily
lead to increased yields, for their use must be paralleled by that of other
inputs, especially fertilizers and pesticides. The availability of produc-
tion credit for inputs and of input subsidies is, therefore, important in
the context of an improved seeds industry.
19.       Various subsidy schemes were implemented during the Second Malaysia
Plan (SMP) to promote crop diversification and to contain the rising costs of
inputs to farmers. The subsidies are given for planting materials (seeds and
seedlings), fertilizers and pesticides. Of particular importance is the Urea
Subsidy Scheme for padi farmers, introduced during the second half of 1974 to
offset the sharp increase in the price of urea. The allocation for subsidies
has been increased markedly under the Third Malaysia Plan (TIP).
20.       Agricultural credit is supplied by Bank Pertanian Malaysia (BPM)
and the FOA in Peninsular Malaysia. In Sabah the Sabah Padi Board formerly
extended credit to padi farmers but this was discontinued in 1975 because of the
bad loan recovery situation. Institutional credit is not available in Sarawak;
however seeds are included in the subsidy programs of East Malaysia.
21.       Total loans by BPM approved under the SMP totaled M$159 million,
the bulk of the loans being for padi production (41.2%), agro-based indus-
tries (23.6%), estate development (15.3%) and tobacco production and market-
ing (10.3%). Credit is provided either directly by BPM or through estab-
lished local credit centers, mostly Farmers' Organizations.
22.       During the SMP the total credit provided by FOA was M$3.6 million,
mainly for agricultural inputs, rice milling operations and expansion of
agricultural production, processing and marketing.
Irrigation and Area Development
23.       Some large-scale rice irrigation projects were developed in Malaysia
a few years ago when rice production began to lag behind consumption and
imports were imposing a serious strain on the balance of payments. There is
a much greater demand for seed of improved rice varieties in the irrigation
areas than for the country as a whole, for the irrigation areas have received
much more intensive extension and demonstration activity. At present much



ANNEX 4
Page 5
of the improved padi seed used in the irrigation areas is multiplied by tile
farmers themselves from small parcels supplied by the irrigation authorities,
but these areas would be expected to provide a major outlet for seed produced
under the proposed seed project.
24.       Among the larger irrigation schemes are the Muda and Kemuba Irri-
gation Projects. The Muda Project, operative in 1970 and controlled by
the Muda Agricultural Development Authority (MADA) is located in the States
of Kedah and Perlis and covers a command area of about 240,000 acres. The
primary objective of the project is to provide an off-season water supply to
facilitate double-cropping of padi, little attention being given to other
crops. Prior to the project the farmers were using a large number of rice
varieties but now some 85% use improved varieties for the main season crop
and 90% for the off-season crop.
25.       The Kemuba Irrigation Project, operative in 1971 and administered
by the Kemuba Agricultural Development Authority (KADA), is located in
Kelantan and serves an area of about 47,000 acres. Like Muda, the primary
objective is to provide an off-season water supply to facilitate double-
cropping of padi. About 50% of the farmers use the improved varieties Mahsuri
and Bahagia for the main-season crop while practically all of the off-season
crop consists of these varieties.
26.       Two new irrigation project areas where there will doubtless be a
large demand for improved seed are Besut in Trengganu and the Trans-Perak
Scheme, each of 12,000 acres are designed for double-cropping of padi.
27.       Under the TMP (1976-80) it is proposed that a further 70,000
acres will be provided with drainage and irrigation facilities for padi.
The Existing Seeds Industry
28.       At present only a small quantity of relatively poor quality seed
of improved varieties is produced for distribution to farmers in Malaysia.
Most of the seed produced in Peninsular Malaysia is raised on DOA farms known
as Crop Production Centers (CPCs), but on rare occasions some padi seed has
been produced by private farmers under the supervision of CPC staff.  Only
small quantities of seeds other than padi have been raised on the CPCs.
Under the project it is proposed to produce 50% or more of the seeds by
private farmers (contract growers).
29.       In Sabah seed production has been virtually restricted to padi,
recently at a level of about 15 tons per year. The multiplication of impro-
ved padi seed in Sabah has involved both the Sabah Padi Board (SPB) and the
SDOA. The SPB has provided foundation seed to selected farmers for multipli-
cation, the farmers being given the seed, fertilizers and sprays. Thus the
farmers virtually only supply the land and labor. The SPB has also supplied
the machinery for land preparation, theoretically on a hire basis but in
practice often free. The SPB has then bought the multiplied padi seed from



ANNEX 4
Page 6
the farmer-grower at a premium price some 20% above normal padi market price.
The role of the SDOA in Sabah has been confined to assistance with quality
control.
30.       In Sarawak seed multiplication is undertaken by the Research
Branch at the various crop testing stations. The amount of padi seeds
produced (37.5 tons) is far from adequate to meet the requirements of the
State.  Very small quantities of maize, soybean and groundnut seeds are
produced on a few agricultural stations, e.g. Tarat, Semongok and Kabuloh.
The bulk of the seeds are 14 tons maize, 8 tons soybean, 4.2 tons ground-
nuts purchased from seed growers through Farmer's Organizations. The seeds
are of unknown quality and the supply is not assured.
31.       The quality of the seed that has been produced in Peninsular
Malaysia, Sarawak and Sabah has generally been poor. There has been no seed
certification in the true sense and indeed only a very limited type of
quality control. The seed produced has been subjected to only elementary
testing procedures because of inadequate facilities and lack of trained
personnel. Because of these constraints, too, testing for quality control
has been confined to padi seed. As far as Peninsular Malaysia is concerned
the main seed testing facilities are at Serdang near Kuala Lumpur; the
testing facilities available at the actual CPCs where the seeds have been
raised are so inadequate as to allow of only simple checks on germination and
viability.
32.       The quality of the seeds produced has been poor not only because of
the unavailability of adequate testing facilities but also because of comp-
letely inadequate processing equipment and storage facilities at the CPCs and
shortage of trained staff. There are very few artificial driers for padi
seed, most of which has to be sun-dried on large concrete floors, making it
difficult to control the moisture content and to avoid mixing the seed of
different varieties. Sun-drying the main-season padi crop can be reasonably
efficient, but drying of the off-season crop runs into the onset of the wet
season when high rainfall and high humidity are experienced. The storage
facilities at the CPCs are often small and frequently their construction and
design are so poor that seed deterioration, particularly due to rodents and
birds, is unavoidable. The CPCs, too, are often too poorly supplied with
farm equipment and machinery to undertake large-scale production of improved
seeds; at present harvesting and transplanting of padi are not mechanized.
33.       At present the CPCs used for seed production have a large number of
staff relative to their output of improved seeds, partly because the staff is
poorly trained and the operations are so labor-intensive. Production costs
of padi seed are very high, especially when compared with costs of a typical
farmer-grower of padi. It should be pointed out that the production costs
given in the table for improved padi seed do not include those associated
with the visits to the CPC of supervisory staff from DOA headquarters.



ANNEX 4
Page 7
Quantities of Seeds Produced in Peninsular Malaysia
by the Crop Production Branch of the DOA
(tons)
1972          1973           1974
Padi                           340            500            650
Maize                            15            18             22
Groundnut                         2             5              9
Sorghum                          8              2              1
Soybean                        0.3            0.1              2
34.       The costs of production of padi seed are relatively high at CPCs
(Vol. II: Project File, Part I, Section B) as compared with costs of a typical
farmer grower of padi because most field operations are still labor intensive,
and facilities to mechanize labor-intensive operations, such as transplanting
and harvesting, to expand production and to use outgrowers have been lacking.
There is sufficient field staff at CPCs to supervise and assist outgrowers,
should related facilities become available, and the requisite training is
provided.
35.       In Sabah, Sarawak and Peninsular Malaysia the foundation seed is
supplied by MARDI or local research stations. The absence of a proper monitoring
system has sometimes led to the release of padi varieties which, although high
yielding and disease resistant, did not have other qualities favoring general
acceptance by farmers, millers and consumers.
36.       Distribution of the small quantity of improved padi seed produced
in Sabah is handled by the SPB. As far as Peninsular Malaysia is concerned
the seed produced at the CPCs is sold to SDOAs, the irrigation authorities
like MADA and KADA, or directly to farmers. The SDOAs and the irrigation
authorities distribute the seeds to farmers through Farmer Associations (FAs)
or at selected distribution centers in villages where there are no FAs. The
farmers may either buy the seed from the SDOA, exchange their own padi seed
(on a weight basis) with the SDOA for the improved seed or be given seed free
under the Farm Crop Subsidy Scheme in localities covered by the Scheme.
37.       For seeds other than padi in Peninsular Malaysia the CPCs may sell
seed directly to farmers or to SDOAs for distribution to farmers. At present
the SDOAs provide free seeds of groundnut, maize and soybean to farmers
under the Farm Crop Subsidy Scheme in localities covered by the Scheme.
38.       For some crops, but notably groundnut and vegetables, a well organ-
ized dealer network exists based largely on imported seed. Most of the
distribution occurs through one or two large seed dealers, but there are also
a number of smaller dealers acting as retail distribution outlets.



ANNEX 4
Page 8
39.       There is at present no seed legislation in Malaysia, and it is not
envisaged that legislation would be required during the project as the govern-
ment would continue to be the only producer of seed. Legislation would simply
be government regulating government. As indicated earlier, only limited
control over the quality of the seed produced at the CPCs in Peninsular
Malaysia and by the farmer-growers of padi in Sabah is currently possible
because of the poor facilities and equipment and lack of suitably trained
staff. It should be pointed out though that preliminary standards for improved
padi seed have been prepared by the Technical Committee on Propagation Materials
under the authority of the Agricultural Industry Standards Committee./l The
prepared document prescribes the production and quality standards of rice seed
for propagation purposes. As far as seed purity is concerned the following
standards are laid down:
pure seed (minimum)          98%
inert matter (maximum)        2%
other crop seed (maximum)   0.10%
weed seed (maximum)           5 grams per kg
germination (minimum)        80%
moisture content (maximum) 13%
Project Definition
40.       The project would finance the development of a seeds industry in
Malaysia that would vastly upgrade its facilities and capacities for the
production, processing, quality control and distribution of improved seeds.
As presently defined in the preparation report, Peninsular Malaysia and East
Malaysia are included in the project.
41.       At full development the proposed facilities would process, store
and distribute each year for Peninsular Malaysia 2,000 tons of padi seed, 250
tons of groundnut, 90 tons of soybean, 30 tons of maize and 30 tons of
sorghum seed. For Sabah the targeted production (end of project) would
be 200 tons of padi and a total of 150 tons of maize, groundnut and soybean,
and 250 tons of padi and the development of minor crop seeds for Sarawak.
42.       The project would include:
(a) the installation of processing and storage facilities at all CPCs
(5 on the Peninsula, 2 in Sabah and I in Sarawak), together with small
seed testing laboratories;
(b) equipment for a major seed testing and quality control laboratory
at Serdang;
(c) farm machinery to mechanize production operations on about 800 acres
of CPC land;
/1 Standards Institution of M4alaysia. Draft standards for public comment.
Malaysian standard specification for rice seed for planting. Undated.



ANNEX 4
Page 9
(d) land development at some of the selected C.PC seed centers;
(e) a seed fund for the purchase of seed multiplied by farmer-outgrowers
under contract to DOA;
(f) fellowships for staff training;
(g) foreign technical assistance;
(h) the installation of processing and storage facilities at Papar for
padi together with equipment and facilities for seed testing and
quality control;
(i) the physical infrastructure facilities for the establishment of a
fully equipped CPC at Tenom for the production of seed of maize,
groundnut and soybean together with processing, storage and seed
testing equipment. In addition to Tenom two subsidiary crop produc-
tion centers, each of 10 acres, would be established solely for the
production of maize, groundnut and soybean seed. One would be at
Ulu Dusun Station in Sandakan and the other at the Kuhara Station
in Tawau. The main financing required under the project for the two
subsidiary CPCs would be for land development and a seed store at
each location;
(j) limited farm machinery; and
(k)  the physical infrastructure facilities for the establishment of a
fully equipped CPC at Paya Selanyau for the production of padi
seeds together with processing, storage and seed testing equip-
ment.
43.       The DOA would be entirely responsible for processing, storage,
testing and quality control.
Detailed Features
44.       Variety Release and Foundation Seed.  MARDI would supply the found-
ation seed. In order to ensure that the varieties released by MARDI are
acceptable to farmers, millers and consumers Regional Seed Assessment Commit-
tees would be formed in the major rice growing areas of the country. They
would include representatives from MARDI, SDOA, LPN, farmers (represented by
FOA), millers and consumers. These committees would advise the respective
Crop Advisory Committees which would forward their recommendations for the
National Seed Assessment Committee (NSAC) to decide on the release of any new
varieties for multiplication and distribution to farmers. Details of the
functions and organization of NSAC are given later under Organization and
Management (paras. 94 and 95).
45.       Seed Production.  Five CPCs have been selected in Peninsular
Malaysia to become seed production centers. Of these five the CPCs at Telok



ANNEX 4
Page 10
Chengai, Bumbong Lima, Titi Gantong and Lundang would undertake large-scale
seed production (multiplication). The fifth CPC, Ajil, would simply be a
processing and distribution center and would depend on farmer-outgrowers and
State Agricultural Stations for seed production. The production of groundnut,
maize, sorghum and soybean in Peninsular Malaysia would be undertaken mainly
at CPCs in Titi Gantong and Lundang.
46.       In Peninsular Malaysia the project would rely heavily on farmer-
growers for seed production, but only for padi and groundnut, as these two
crops are not subject to cross-pollination problems. It is anticipated that
by the fifth year of the project half of the padi seed and 70% of the ground-
nut seed in Peninsular Malaysia would come from contract growers.
47.       Peninsular Malaysia seed production would initially be confined
to padi. Maize, soybean, sorghum and groundnut production would be intro-
duced during the third year of the project, by which time it is hoped that
improved varieties would be released by MARDI.
48.       In Sabah all of the padi seed would be raised by farmer-growers under
contract to the DOA rather than on CPCs; a processing and distribution center
will be set up at Papar. Assistance of the SPB would be sought in the selec-
tion of appropriate farmer-growers. The maize, groundnut and soybean seeds
in Sabah would be entirely produced on the CPC at Tenom and the subsidiary
CPCs at Ulu Dusun in Sandakan and Kuhara Station in Tawau.
49.       In Sarawak all of the padi foundation seeds required for multi-
plication at other agricultural stations and farmer growers under contract
to the DOA would be undertaken at Paya Selanyau. In addition certified
seeds for distribution would also be produced. Seeds of other crops produced
on farmer fields or agricultural stations would be processed whenever feas-
ible.
50.       The farmer seed growers would enter into an agreement with the
Seed Production Unit of the CPC seed center on an official contract basis
to grow a specified acreage of one padi variety. The terms of contract
would cover details of field standards required by the Seed Certification
and Testing Unit. The field inspectors of this unit would carry out field
inspections during the growing season to determine the eligibility of the
crop to meet the required quality standards and advise the growers of
rogueing and other requirements to maintain the defined standards. Crops
would be harvested by the grower but under supervision of Seed Production
Unit staff.
51.       Under these contracts the DOA would provide foundation seed at
special prices which would be higher than the current commercial price, on
credit against delivery of harvested seed to processing plants. On delivery
the grower would be paid the sum agreed under the terms of the contract. It
is envisaged that seed growers would be paid a premium of 30% above the
prevailing market price of normal padi as fixed by LPN prior to each cropping
season. Payment will be made from the seeds fund.



ANNEX 4
Page 11
52.       Training of seed growers on seed production and field aspects of
quality control would be provided by AA and AT staff from the CPCs.
53.       The farmer-growers selected in Peninsular Malaysia would be respon-
sible for their own land preparation but in Sabah land preparation machinery
would be available on hire from the SPB.
54.       Under the project tractors and machinery would be provided so that
all field preparation for planting at the CPCs would be mechanized. Part of
the padi planting (by the transplant method) and harvesting would also be
mechanized at the CPCs through the provision of some mechanical harvesters and
transplanters. Details of farm machinery to be supplied under the project
and the phasing of its purchase are given in Table 1. However, all weeding,
rogueing and spraying for disease and pest control would be done by hand.
55.       Funds would be provided under the project for land development at
three of the CPC seed centers in Peninsular Malaysia and at Tenom, the two
subsidiary CPCs in Sabah and one CPC in Sarawak (Annex 6, Section B). The
greatest expenditure would be at Titi Gantong for land clearing, and the
construction of drainage and irrigation facilities, and farm roads, since part
of this CPC is still under rainforest.
56.       Seed Processing and Storage.  The locations of the five CPCs that
have been chosen to become the seed centers are Telok Chengai, Bumbong Lima,
Titi Gantong, Ajil and Lundang; each would be provided with processing and
storage facilities. Criteria that were used in their selection are:
(a) availability of suitably drained and irrigated land for seed
production;
(b) access to potential seed markets: distance and transportation
resources;
(c) seed demand potential of the area;
(d) presence of other agricultural institutions and services that
could provide back-up guidance and supervision of production,
processing and marketing of seed; and
(e) general suitability of the site for the building and operation
of the processing and storage facilities.
57.       For Peninsular Malaysia the targeted seed production under the
project at full development is 2,000 tons of padi, 250 tons of groundnut,
90 tons of soybean, 30 tons of maize and 30 tons of sorghum seed, giving a
total of 2,400 tons per year. This output is expected to provide the follow-
ing proportions of the total annual seed requirement: padi land double-
cropped (calculated on the basis of seed exchange every third year) 33%, padi
single-cropped 11%, total padi areas 22%, groundnut 28%, maize 33%, sorghum
and soybean each 50%.



ANNEX 4
Page 12
58.       The capacity of the proposed processing plants in Peninsular
Malaysia would be slightly in excess of the targeted production of 2,400 tons.
Within the double-cropped padi areas a processing plant with a capacity of
500 tons is proposed for Telok Chengi, one of 200 tons for Bumbong Lima, of
300 tons for Titi Gantong and of 200 tons for Lundang. The capacity of the
plant at Ajil, in a single-cropped padi area, would be 100 tons. Thus the
total potential output capacity of the five plants would be 2,500 tons per
year.
59.       Two seed processing and storage facilities would be provided under
the project in Sabah. One would be at Papar for padi which, as a double-
cropped area, would have a yearly output of 200 tons. The other, at Tenom,
would have a capacity of 150 tons and would be for processing maize, groundnut
and soybean.
60.       The estimated seed requirement for planting the areas targeted for
each crop by 1980 under the TMP for Sabah are maize 89 tons, groundnut 36 tons
and soybean about 4 1/2 tons. The proposed plant at Tenom would, therefore,
be easily capable of meeting the full estimated requirements for these crops.
There are about 83,000 acres of land under wet padi at present in Sabah,
10,000 acres of which are double-cropped. The present annual seed requirement
for planting all of the double-cropped land is, therefore, around 100 tons.
The immediate potential is for increased double cropping where most farmers
presently practice single cropping using local varieties.
61.       In Sarawak, the estimated seed requirement for planting the area
targeted for each crop by 1980 under the TMP are padi, 600 tons, maize,
226 tons, groundnuts, 395 tons and soybeans, 175 tons. The proposed CPC
at Paya Selanyau would be capable of producing 50 tons of seeds and proces-
sing up to 250 tons of padi. The project would propose to produce 250 to
300 tons of seeds at full development.
62.       The processing plants would have batch-type drying facilities (forced
heated air flow system) consisting of either two or four drying bins according
to the capacity of the plant, cleaning and grading, chemical treating and
bagging facilities. Details of processing plants ranging from 500 tons to
100 tons capacity would be prepared with the advice of a consultant. The seeds
receiving, processing and storage operations would be in a sequence so as to
minimize handling costs. The estimated costs of the proposed buildings,
processing equipment, storage facilities and ancillary equipment at each of
the seed centers in Peninsular Malaysia, Sabah and Sarawak are given in
Tables 5, 6 and 7.
63.       The final design specifications for tender documents would actually
be prepared by an expatriate consultant provided under the project in conjunc-
tion with the Crop Production Branch of the DOA. The consultant would also
assist with the evaluation of bids for the seed plant equipment.
64.       The proposed phasing of construction of the processing plants and
storage facilities are shown in Table 1.



ANNEX 4
Page 13
65.       The project would provide for in-service practical training of
staff in the operation and maintenance of seed processing and storage plants
as well as short-term (3-6 months) overseas fellowships and a limited
number of longer-term fellowships.
66.       Seed Quality.  Seed quality control would be the responsibility of
the DOA alone. Seed legislation would probably not be introduced during the
five years of the project, although some attention would be given to drafting
such legislation. During the course of the project "certified" seed would be
produced in the sense that it would have minimum standards of quality guaran-
teed by the DOA. Each of the seed centers would have the basic equipment and
facilities needed for quality control and a main seed standards laboratory
would be established at Serdang near Kuala Lumpur.
67.       The project would also involve three types of in-service staff
training and fellowships for overseas training in aspects of seed quality
control:
(a)  short local training courses for officers in varietal identification,
field inspection procedures and laboratory seed testing procedures;
(b) short-term (3-6 months) overseas fellowships for training in
quality control procedures;
(c) limited graduate and postgraduate fellowships for longer-term seed
technology training.
68.       The estimated fellowship costs and phasing are shown in Annex 6,
Part F.
69.        Seed Distribution.  The DOA would be responsible for the distri-
bution of seeds under the project in Peninsular Malaysia and for seeds other
than padi in Sabah and Sarawak; the SPB in Sabah and the DOA in Sarawak would
be expected to assume responsibility for padi seed distribution in these
States. A formal network of channels of distribution would be organized
involving the following outlets:
(a) the Inputs Distribution Units of the SDOAs;
(b) the Farmers Development Centers of the FOA;
(c) the Inputs Distribution Units of specific agencies like
MADA and KADA;
(d) the Crop Production Centers;
(e) dealers in agricultural inputs located in the rural areas.

70.       Technical Assistance.  The DOA has had very limited experience in
many  sD.   , of improved seed production but especially in the specification,
installation and operation of processing and storage equipment. The services
of -- - reign expert would be required for a period of two years; part of his
time (three months) would be spent in both Sabah and Sarawak, and the remainder
in Peninsular Malaysia. He would be needed from the start of the project as
one of his first functions would be to assist the DOA to prepare specifica-
tions for the processing facilities and to prepare the necessary procurement
documents. Also included in his terms of reference would be:
(a)  to assist the Seed Project Director with the organization and
implementation of seed distribution;
(b)  to assist in formulating training courses in seed processing
and storage;
(c)  to assist in the selection of institutions and seed production
centers for staff training abroad; and
(d)  to assist in the planning, design, and selection of facilities and
equipment.
71.       Training and Fellowships.  The project would involve specialized
training of staff and also of farmer seed growers in various technical
aspects. In-service staff training involving courses of 1-2 weeks duration
would be carried out with the assistance of the foreign technical expert.
Training on varietal 'dentification and plant characteristics of existing and
new varieties would be carried out by MARDI staff during short courses held
at their research stations. The training of seed testing staff in seed
analysis, germination and other related aspects would take place at the major
seed testing laboratory at Serdang.
72.       Farmer seed growers would receive training from AA and AT staff on
seed production and field aspects of certification.
73.       A number of fellowships would be provided for staff training overseas,
primarily to provide for short-term visits to foreign seed production centers
but also for some longer-term training at institutions specializing in seed
technolLgy.  It is envisaged that some 14 man-years of fellowships would be
pro-v'da7 under the project for Peninsular Malaysia (Annex 6, Section F).
7F-       Seed Promotion and Extension.  The rate of uptake and use of improved
se^-  w-''  depend heavily upon the effectiveness of the DOA's promotion program,
.e. ex,.  sion, subsidies and seeds pricing policy.  Seeds are presently being
distributed on the following basis: (a) cash payment relative to cost of
prcCd ct_,., (b) exc  nge of seed for padi on a one for one weight basis, and
(c) ffee to farmers  no do not have the resources or incentive to purchase
improve' s-'eds. The State extension services of the DOA would play the leading
role in -his program and for this purpose would work in close association with
Farmers' Organizations and authorities like MADA and KADA. Field days at the
CPCs and field demonstration plots would be used to show the advantages of
using quality seeds of recommended varieties of crops.

Organization and Management
76.       General.  In all three territories, Peninsular Malaysia, Sabah and
Sarawak, the respective DOAs will be responsible for the implementation of
the Seeds Project (see Seeds Production Organization Chart XTB17048). To
ensure that the activities of the project are on schedule and properly moni-
tored and directed as envisaged Project Leaders will be appointed to assist and
be responsible to the Project Directors of the respective DOA.
77.       In Peninsular Malaysia, the Crop Production Branch of the DOA would
be responsible for the implementation of the project. In Sarawak and in Sabah
the respective DOAs will be responsible for the implementation of the project.
MARDI in Peninsular Malaysia and in collaboration with the respective Research
Branch of DOA Sarawak and Sabah will be responsible for the development of new
varieties and for the production of foundation seed. Responsibility for the
approval of newly bred varieties, for release to the DOA for multiplication and
distribution to farmers would be vested with the National Seeds Assessment
Committee (NSAC) in Peninsular Malaysia, while Seeds Committee with similar
functions would be set up in Sarawak and Sabah separately.
78.       Seed distribution in Peninsular Malaysia would be carried out by
several agencies including FOA, the Irrigation Project Authorities like MADA
and KADA and the SDOAs. Similarly in Sarawak and Sabah the SDOAs there would
fully utilize all existing channels for project seed distribution for example
the Field Branch of SDOA Sarawak, the Extension Branch of SDOA Sabah, the Sabah
Padi Board and Farmers Organizations in the two territories.
79.       Organization of the Seed Production, Processing, Certification
and Testing Section of the Crop Production Branch. This reorganized section
within the Crop Production Branch of the DOA would assume responsibility for
executing the project. It would be headed by an Assistant Director (Seed
Production) and made up of three units (a) Seed Production Unit, (b) Seed
Processing, Storage and Distribution Unit, and (c) Seed Certification and
Testing Unit. A senior agricultural officer (SAO) would be assigned as head
of each of the three units. The heads of the first two units would report to
Lhe Assistant Director (Seed Prodrction), while the head of the third would be
directly responsible to the Director of the Crop Production Branch in order
to maintain the independence of the regulatory control functions of the Unit.
The organization of the seed component is summarized in Chart WB17278.
80.       The Seed Production Unit would be responsible for production aspects.
It would determine the country's annual requirements of breeder, foundation,
registered and certified seed and arrange for the regional production at CPCs
and/or seed growers' fields, All the foundation seed and 50-60% oi the certi-
fied seed would be produced at the CPCs; the remaining 40-50% of the certified
seed would be produced on seed growers' farms selected by the Seed Production
Unit. A seeds fund for seed procurement, administered by the Administrative
Office of the Crop Production Branch of the DOA headquarters and functioning
under instructions from the Seed Production Unit, would be provided under the
project. A register of seed growers would be maintained by the Seed Production
Unit. The staff requirement for the unit for Peninsular Malaysia is presented
in Annex 6, Section B.



ANNEX 1
Page l/
81,       The Seed Processing  Storage and Distribution Unit would be
responsible for all aspects of the project concerning processing, packaging,
storage and distribution of certified seeds. In this respect the head of
this unit and his staff, with the assistance of the foreign expert, would
design each processing plant, specify the equipment, prepare tender documents,
and supervise construction of civil works and the installation of equipment.
The unit would then manage and operate the processing plants and related
storage facilities.
82.       The Administration Office of the Crop Production Branch would main-
tain operations accounts on a commercial basis for the processing plants0
83.       The processing and storage facilities would be managed within the
total organization of each CPC seed center. The responsibility for the
control of seed processing, storage and distribution would be through one AO
or an experienced AA. He would be responsible to the officer-in-charge of
the CPC seed center.
84.       The Seed Certification and Testing Unit would have an independent
status regarding its regulatory control functions and would be the sole seed
certification authority in Malaysia. It would be directly responsible to the
Director of the Crop Production Branch of the DOA. The duties of the unit
would cover field inspection, control of seed quality during processing and
storage, seed sampling and testing, sealing, labeling and certification. It
would also be responsible for field testing of breeder, foundation, registered
and certified seed samples to check genetic purity, varietal purity and the
presence of seed-borne diseases which may not be discernible in the laboratory.
85.       The Seed Certification and Testing Unit would delegate powers to
its inspectors, samplers and analysts for access to and inspection of fields
producing seed under the project, the project seed centers where seeds are
processed and stored, and the premises of stores of private dealers selling
seeds produced under the project.
86.       In addition this unit would formulate the seed quality standards
that would be empowered under Ministerial Regulations. Eventually a seed
legislation act would be required and the unit would be responsible for
its formulation and implementation.
87.       A major laboratory for seed testing and certification would be
established at Serdang. In addition basic seed testing facilities would
be provided at each of the CPC seed centers.
88.       Organization of the CPC Seed Centers.  Each CPC seed center would
be provided with staff from the three above mentioned units. The AG in
charge of each seed center would ensure the coordination of the activities of
the different units within his center. The officers in charge of each of the
three units within the seed center would report to their respective controlling
officers at headquarters through the officer-in-charge of the seed center.



AliNEX 4
Page 18
89.       The Seed Certification and Testing Unit would be represented at each
seed cc  cr by a number of field certification and laboratory testing staff.
These staff would be responsible through the officer-in-charge of each CPC
seee center to the head of the Seed Certification and Testing Unit at head-
quacK ers.
90.       National Seed Assessment Committee (NSAC).  The Government would
underLake to set up a NSAC to perform the following functions:
(a) to review the characteristics and performance of new varieties
recommended by MARDI as well as the probability of their acceptance
by farmers, millers and consumers and thereby assess the suitability
of the varieties for inclusion in the seed production program;
(b) to ensure liaison and close collaboration between the different
agencies associated with the seeds industry, namely IIARDI, DOA, LPN,
FOA and millers; and
(c) to review and revise, if necessary, the seed production targets
for various crops.
91.       Pending the enactment of seed legislation the NSAC would have
advisory functions only. The Committee would include representatives from
MIARDI, the Crop Production Branch and the Extension Branch of DOA, FOA and
the Federal Agricultural Marketing Authority (FAMA) and would co-opt other
members as necessary. The LPN, for example, would attend meetings concerned
with the release of -adi varieties.
92.       Contract Growing and Seed Pricing.  With the introduction of seed
growers into the project a contract growing system for seed procurement would
be established. The DOA would contract growers to produce seed under terms
and conditions mutually acceptable both technically and financially. Under
these contracts the DOA would provide foundation seed at special prices
which would be higher than the current commercial prices, on credit against
delivery of the harvested seed to processing plants. It would also provide
field inspection and laboratory testing services at no direct cost to the
farmer producer.  The staff of the Seed Certification and Testing Unit would
ensure that only seeds from crops of acceptable standard are purchased by the
seed centers. It is anticipated that in the case of padi farmer-growers they
would be paid a premium of 20-30% above the market price of normal padi as
fixed by LPN prior to each cropping season. A seeds fund would be provided
to finance such a scheme. Assurances would be sought that the Federal Govern-
ment would finance and replenish the seeds fund as required to cover operating
deficits and subsidies.
93.       To promotc ~ihe quick acceptance and use of certified seeds, the
improved seeds shoul- be sold to selected farmers at normal seed market prices
during the first thria years of the project.  During the initial phase of the
project COM may wish to subsidize the purchase of seeds to promote their
introduction and make them available to farmers who do not have adequate
resources. After this time a premium of around 30% would be charged.



ANNEX 4
Page 19
94.       Audits and Accounts.  Separate accounts would be established and
maintained to cover each part of the project investment and operations in
accordance with sound commercial accounting practices. This would allow the
efficiency of various operations to be monitored. Each account would be
audited annually by an independent auditor and copies of audit reports would
be sent to the Bank within six months of the close of each fiscal year.
Benefits and Justification
95.       Economic Benefits.  At full development improved seeds marketed
under the project in Peninsular Malaysia would be sufficient to sow about
200,000 acres of padi, 7,000 acres of groundnut, 3,000 acres of maize, 5,000
acres of sorghum and 5,000 acres of soybean each year. The improved padi
seed produced in Sabah would be sufficient to sow about 20,000 acres. The
capacity of the processing plant proposed for Tenom is 150 tons soybean per
year. If it is assumed that production consists of 60 tons of groundnut, 86
tons of maize and 4 tons of soybean, it would be sufficient to sow about 1,680
acres of groundnut, 9,600 acres of maize and 220 acres of soybean. The esti-
mated increased yields due to the improved seeds for padi and the national
incremental annual income resulting from the use of the seeds are set out in
(Annex 7, Section A). After deducting the investment costs and the operating
costs the economic rate of return would be about 5%.
96.       Financial Benefits.  The estimated net annual incremental farm
income per acre accruing to farmers using the certified seeds would be about
US$588 for padi. As far as the main product of the seeds project, padi seed,
is concerned benefits would accrue to around 750,000 farmers in Peninsular
Malaysia and 110,000 farmers in Sabah.
97.       Ecology.  It is unlikely that the project would have a significantly
adverse effect on the ecology of the country although the use of HYVs does
require the application of chemical fertilizers and sprays for pest contol.

NATIONAL EXTENSION PROJECT
Training Component
Introduction
1.        The work of agricultural field staff of DOA in the past was heavily
oriented towards regulatory functions, subsidy schemes and survey work for
which limited levels of practical skill and technical knowledge were sufficient.
In recent years great emphasis has been placed on agricultural development
and the demand for new and better trained staff throughout the agricultural
industry has grown very rapidly, with priority for new staff being given to
high intensity cropping areas, research, export crops, etc. Three new
agricultural institutes to train extension staff have been established during
the past four years, but even so, the increased production of the training
institutes has not met the new demand for operative level staff and the low
priority poverty areas have been least fortunate in the allocation of new staff.
2.        The training institutes have focused on training new agricultural
technicians and thus neglected in-service refresher, up-grading and specialized
training of existing field staff. Such in-service training is urgently
needed to improve the technical knowledge of existing field staff of DOA and
other agricultural agencies in basic agricultural science and crop technology,
and to provide training in operational skills in such fields as extension and
communication methods and farm management, and, for supervisory staff, in
administration and personnel management skills as well.
Training Component Financing
3.        The training which would be financed under the project, to meet
the needs of DOA outlined above, is described in the following sections:
1. Peninsular Malaysia; 2. Sabah; and 3. Sarawak. The project would assist
GOM with financing the foreign exchange comDonent (US$6.24 million), of which
US$2.39 million is civil works, US$1.51 million is vehicles and equipment,
and US$0.47 million is technical assistance and fellowships. Physical
contingencies are US$0.46 million and price contingencies US$1.41 million.
Details are contained in Annex 6, Section E and in the supplementary report
No. 1555a-s-MA and are summarized below.

1. PENINSULAR MALAYSIA
BACKGROUND
Staff Numbers
4.        Staffing of the extension service of the Department of Agriculture
is made up as follows:
Rank                     Title                   Qualification    Number
