formal training
Training Institutions
5.        Professional Level.  B.Sc. level training in agriculture is provided
at the Faculty of Agriculture of the University of Malaysia in Kuala Lumpur,
and at the University Pertanian Malaysia (UPM) at Serdang. In the past only
a degree course in general agriculture was taught, but new courses in agri-
business and in economic resources will graduate their first students in 1978
and 1979 respectively.
6.        Subprofessional Level.  At Serdang, UPM has a well integrated
training complex providing diploma and degree training in agriculture.
Recent review of course programs has made it possible to increase capacity
for M.S. level training with a wide range of specializations. An Agricul-
tural Institute (AI) is situated at Serdang on land adjoining UPM and the
headquarters station of MARDI.
7.        Operative Level.  Certificate training, which is a federal govern-
ment responsibility, is provided at six training institutes which are well
distributed throughout the Peninsula. Three of these were constructed
recently with IDA assistance (Loan 599-MA). Since 1966 the certificate
course has been a 3-year course (it was an 18 month course prior to 1966)
and trainees graduating from the institutes find employment with MARDI,
FELDA, RISDA, FOA, DOA, and the private sector. The demand for Agricultural
Technicians (AT) during the Third Malaysia Plan (TMP) is expected to exceed
possible supply by over 6,000 (see para. 9).



ANNEX 5
Page 4
8.        Suboperative Level.  Staff at this level are employed for specific
tasks with limited responsibilities and State Departments of Agriculture
and other employing agencies run their own training programs, of a few months
duration, for suboperatives. The Ministry of Education has two vocational
schools which offer a two-year course in basic agriculture training for sub-
operatives; however, they are required to complete one additional year at an
AI (after 2 years service) to become operatives. There is no problem with the
supply of such recruits at the present time.
Problems of Operative Training
9.        Student enrollment at the training institutes (AIs), as a percentage
of capacity, has lagged behind construction of AIs during the period 1967-76
and in 1976 the five operational institutes had only 49% of their places filled.
This is due to a combination of the following factors:
(a) Entrance to the institutes is through scholarships offered by prospec-
tive employers. Thus, Federal and State Departments of Agriculture,
as well as other statutory bodies, recruit trainees and send them to
the institutes for certificate training. Both financial limitations
on the number of scholarships and administrative delays in recruit-
ment of trainees by federal and state DOA have affected the number
of trainees being sent to the institutes;
(b) lack of amenities, e.g. housing, transport, etc., and the ineffectual-
ity of many field extension staff contribute to their effectiveness
and some dissatisfaction;
(c) the minimum entrance requirement for the course is the lower Educa-
tion Certificate with credits in national language, general science
and mathematics, but this same examination achievement qualifies
school children to advance in the school system to form IV. By
leaving school to enter the training institutes, trainees sacrifice
their opportunity for further scholastic advancement. This acts as
a strong disincentive to those considering entering the training
institutes, and contributes to a high wastage amongst students; and
(d) at present there is no avenue of advancement within the service
available to ATs beyond the level of senior technical assistant.
This low career ceiling is a disincentive to those considering
employment with the DOA and causes considerable loss of trained
staff to employment elsewhere in the agricultural industry.
10.       Despite a three-year training course at the institutes, there is
frequent criticism of trained operatives within DOA on the basis of insuf-
ficient technical knowledge or farming skills to provide farmers with the
help they need. While this problem is not solely the responsibility of the
training institutes, training activities are limited at present for the
following reasons:
(a) generally, teaching staff are young with limited, if any, field
extension experience and little or no training in agricultural
teaching methods;



ANNEX 5
Page 5
(b) posts at the training institutes are not sought after by more
experienced staff, as lecturers at the institutes do not qualify
for various allowances which supplement the salaries of equivalent
staff working in the field, and staff at the institutes feel them-
selves to be out of the main stream for promotional opportunities;
and
(c) some specialization is possible in the third year of the course to
tailor the instruction to the needs of the larger employers, but
the total course covers a very wide range of subjects and generally
resembles a diluted degree course in an attempt to meet the require-
ments of all agencies. A review of the curricula for this three-
year training on a more functional basis would permit a shortening
of the course (while still permitting specialization in the latter
part of the course) - particularly in respect of the very large
number of hours spent by students in practical work.
Demand for Operative Training
11.        The projected requirement of new operatives to meet the needs of
the DOA and all other government agencies during the TMP totals 8,700. The
largest projected needs (excluding the Fisheries Development Program) are in
DOA, Division of Forestry, FOA, RISDA, FELDA and MARDI in that order.
12.        Students already enrolled at the institutes who will graduate in
1977, 1978 and 1979 total 769. Assuming a full intake of 1,680 in 1977 (who
will graduate in 1980) the total number of new operatives produced by the
end of TMP will be 2,449 which is short of the projected demand of all
agencies and all institutes. However, the DOA has given its assurance that
additional operatives will be made available to meet project requirements.
At a meeting of the National Development Planning Committee in October 1975,
it was agreed that necessary funds would be provided for the continuing
expansion and development of AI, but there are no new AIs under construction,
and any planned now would have little impact on the number of new staff
produced within the lifetime of this project. The DOA is aware of the
deficiencies and problems of operative training at the AIs and is taking
action to remedy this shortfall.
In-Service Training
13.       Assistant Technicians (ATs).  Prior to 1966, the certificate course
was of only 18 months' duration with a lower entrance requirement than the
present course; thus all technical assistants with more than eight years of
field service have rather limited background training. Rapid advances in
agricultural technology have meant that many of the existing field staff do
not have sufficient technical knowledge to fully understand the biological or
agronomic principles underlying the recommendations they pass on to farmers.
14.       The in-service training of ATs has received relatively little
attention for the following reasons:



.ANNEX 5
Page 6
(a) of the DOA's six training institutes, five have only become opera-
tional during the last nine years as follows:
Bumbong Lima - Pelau Pinang  1967
Kuala Lipis  - Pahang          1971
Parit        - Perak          1973
Ayer Hitam   - Johore          1974
Besut        - Trengganu       1976
In order to meet the growing demand for additional operatives, the
AI have concentrated on preservice training and have performed
little in-service training; and
(b) the nature of the field extension agents' job has been oriented
towards regulatory functions. They were able to perform these
duties without up-to-date technical knowledge, but now major
emphasis would be focused on agricultural extension work.
15.       Supervisory Level.  Supervisory staff have diploma or degree
qualifications but, due partly to the nature of their responsibilities and
poor communication between research and extension at district and field
levels, their knowledge of recent advances in crop and livestock production
technology is not always up to date. In addition, while diplomas or degrees
in agriculture qualify most supervisory staff for entry to the posts they now
hold, their supervisory and administrative responsibilities require skills
for which they have generally had very little training. Supervisory staff
would therefore benefit from training in management skills and from regular
contact with research workers so that they are made aware of research work,
and become skilled in identifying field problems that existing research
expertise could solve.
TRAINING PROPOSALS
Teaching Staff at AI
16.       The following actions would be taken to improve the standard of
teaching at the AI.
(a) Conditions of Service. Conditions of service of AI staff are
being reviewed to see what action is required to make teaching
positions at the AI more attractive career posts.
(b) Teaching Methods. Senior teaching staff from the AI would be
assisted to attend training courses at UPM in adult teaching
methods and skills of teaching practical agriculture. In addition,
the Extension Liaison Unit (ELU) would have a continuing respon-
sibility for increasing the effectiveness of teaching at the AI
and conducting in-service training courses.
(c) Extension Experience. Provision would be made to ensure that
staff who teach field-oriented subjects at the AI have first-
hand field experience.



ATNNEX 5
(d) Involvement in Field Programs. Teaching assignments at the
AI would be arranged so that staff have free days on a regular
basis so that they can assist with in-service training of
extension staff in in the field in a subject matter specialist
capacity.
In-Service Training of Field Staff
17.       In-service training of extension staff would be conducted in the
field by supervisory and subject matter specialist staff at regular monthly
training sessions. However, in addition, there is a need for training to
upgrade staff trained prior to 1966 (who received only 18 months of basic
training) and refresher and specialized training for all extension staff.
The need for special short courses is expected to increase to keep staff
abreast of technical advances, new policies and new demands from farmers.
18.       The present capacity at the AI (see para. 6) could be used immedi-
ately for this training on an ad hoc basis. Teaching staff and preservice
students at the AI would benefit from having experienced ATs as part of the
student community. However, the numbers of staff requiring in-service
training exceeds the AI capacity to accommodate them. Special institutions
(Regional Extension Training and Development Centers, RETDCs) would therefore
be established under the project, whose primary function would be to provide
in-service training of field staff.
RETDC
19.       Two RETDCs would be constructed at the start of the project, the first
at Titi Gatong-Perak and the second at Telaga Batin-Trengganu./l Two more
would be financed under the project, but their construction would be delayed
until the effectiveness of the first two had been assessed and a thorough
study made of the staff training needed by other agricultural agencies,
e.g. FOA, FELDA, FELCRA, Bank Pertanian, State Development Corporation, etc.,
to determine what kind of training could best be given at the RETDC.
20.       Each RETDC would have residential accommodation for 40 students,
with a small library and conference room, classrooms, a home economics unit
for formal courses, and a farm demonstration unit to assist with practical
instruction on livestock, farm machinery, etc.
21.       The RETDCs would provide broad background training of three types:
(i) basic agricultural science and crop technology; (ii) specialist training
in such fields as soil management, conservation and reclamation, seed produc-
tion, livestock production, marketing; and (iii) operational and management
skills. Courses would include the following:
/1 Details of costs and facilities of the RETDCs are shown in Table 1
and in Annex 6.



ANNEX 5
Page 8
(a) farm management (records, business analysis, cash flows,
farm planning and capital appraisal);
(b) personnel management;
(c) project planning, monitoring and evaluation;
(d) report writing;
(e) public speaking;
(f) communications; and
(g) extension methodology.
22.       The RETDC would also provide a base for special seminars or work-
shops, e.g. to bring groups of subject matter specialists and research
workers together or to enable extension workers facing similar cropping
situations and problems to exchange views.
23.       The permanent staff of each RETDC would comprise one Supervisor
(SAO Grade), one Course Director (AO grade), one AO for Monitoring and
Evaluation, one for Communications, one to cover technical courses and
another the courses in management and operational skills, three subprofessional
instructors, and three technicians and supporting artisan staff to handle the
production of mass media materials, and to assist as required with other
aspects of the work of the RETDC (Annex 6, Part I, Section C, Table 1.1).
The permanent staff would form the core of the teaching staff at the RETDC,
but much of the teaching would be done by SMS, national and regional specialists,
and staff from MARDI, UPM, INTAN and other national agencies.
24.       Training of field staff is a federal function for which the Director
of Extension would be responsible. The ELU would cooperate in the preparation
of course material, curriculum development, and liaison between the RETDCs
and the agencies they serve.
25.       The training programs at the RETDCs would be highly flexible
to accommodate the variety of training,courses and the logistical difficulties
of getting students from a wide area of the Peninsula for short courses, and
arranging visits by staff who would supplement the permanent teaching staff
of the RETDC. The siting of the RETDC close to, or adjoining, existing
MARDI stations where possible would facilitate sharing of scarce specialized
staff; and joint use of facilities would be more economic. Sufficiently
senior and experienced officers would be appointed as Supervisors of RETDCs
in order to handle the administration and maintain the necessary liaison with
state extension services and agencies outside DOA. Supervisor posts would be
at senior grade level.
26.       To promote coordination between the RETDC, the state extension
services, and the research institutions, a Management and Curriculum Committee
would be established for each RETDC. The committees would comprise:



ANNEX 5
Page 9
(a) the Director of Extension;
(b) the Director of Training;
(c) the Supervisor of the RETDC;
(d) the State Directors of Agriculture in the region;
(e) a representative of MARDI;
(f) the Head of ELU; and
(g) representatives of agencies which might be served by the RETDC,
e.g. FOA, FELDA, RISDA, etc.
27.       The Committee would have two tasks:  to decide on policy and manage-
rial issues to set a framework for the RETDC Supervisor, i.e. allocation of
space at the RETDC for different agencies and different training programs -
and to approve curricula and budgets for training programs. Subcommittees
made up of state extension workers, research specialists and staff of the
ELU, RETDC, ESC system would study the type of courses needed for the region
and course content. The presence of the Director of Extension (DOA), the
Director of MARDI and the Head of the ELU on the Management and Curriculum
Committees would facilitate integration between the RETDC courses and field
requirements.
Training of Subject Matter Specialists (SMSs)
28.       Thirty-two new SMSs would be appointed under the project (see Annex 1).
Scholarships are included in the project to enable serving officers to attend
graduate courses such as plant protection, soils, agronomy, extension and farm
management. New recruits would receive appropriate practical training
followed by work with existing SMSs in the same area of specialization.
SMSs would continue to participate in study visits inside Hlalaysia and abroad
as appropriate. The work of the SMS and the scholarships provided for their
training are described in Annex 1.
Management Training
29.       The following measures would be taken to train supervisory staff.
(a) State and district level supervisory staff would receive training
in personnel management, finance, administrative management, and land
use planning for agriculture.
(b) The project includes fellowships for short training visits to
enable state and district extension officers to see how extension
programs are operated in other countries as well as fellowships
for specialized graduate and postgraduate training.

2. SABAH
BACKGROUND
Staff Numbers
30.       The numbers of trained agricultural staff employed by DOA are shown
below (DOA staff employed by the Farmers' Associations are shown separately):
Agricultural Staff
Rank                           FA         DOA      Total DOA
Professional                  1          13           14
Subprofessional              15          22           27
Operative                    13          42           55
Suboperative                 27         105          132
Home Demonstration Section
Subprofessional               -           2            2
Operative                     1          11           12
Suboperative                  1          67           68
31.       The project calls for an additional 100 JAA, 25 AA and 4 AO to
strengthen the extension service.
Training Institutions
32.       Professional and Subprofessional.  Sabah relies upon UPM at Serdang
in the Peninsula to provide degree and diploma level training. UPM will be
able to produce sufficient graduates and diplomates to meet Sabah's projected
needs during the TMP.
33.       Operative Level.  The Agricultural Vocational School (AVS) was
established in 1974 at Timbang Menggaris on 2,600 acres of land of which only
400 acres have so far been developed. The school has fine new buildings with
hostel accommodation for 160 men and 24 women students, and provides a three-
year, certificate-level course which comprises 2,500 hours of practical
instruction out of a total of 3,250 hours. Student intakes have been well
below the 60 per annum capacity of the school - the present student body
comprises 34 first year, 48 second year and 30 third year students (including
20 women).
34.       The site of the AVS is remote from essential amenities such as
schools and clinic, and has no telephone connection. The road to the school
from Kota Balud is of a poor standard (although some repair work is in progress)
and these factors have made it difficult to recruit and retain good teaching
staff at the AVS. Also, housing is needed at the institute for junior staff.



ANNEX 5
Page 12
35.       The Principal of AVS is, at the same time, the State Agricultural
Training Officer. The duties of both jobs, and the lack of telephone communi-
cation with the AVS, require the Principal to commute constantly between
Kota Kinabalu and Timbang Menggaris.
36.       Suboperative Level.  The Agricultural Training Center (ATC)
has capacity for 60 students and is situated on land adjoining the Agricul-
tural Research Station at Tuaran. At present it is being used to provide
nine-month training courses for JAAs, JHDs and AHDs, who, in the past, were
recruited directly into the extension service without prior technical
training.
37.       The ATC buildings are old and in need of modernization; laboratory
equipment and teaching aids are very limited. Although the ATC is well
sited in relation to the research station, no land is available for the
expansion of the ATC or for additional staff housing to enable the ATC to
increase its contribution to training activities. New in-service training
facilities would be added to the AVS at Timbang Menggaris to provide a
combined in-service and preservice training complex.
38.       Rural Training Centers.  There are two Rural Training Centers for
farmers, one near Kota Balud, and the other at Danmai in Kudat District.
These offer three 12-week courses per annum in general agriculture for young
farmers who are selected by the district AO. The buildings of these two
centers are old and cramped and require renovation and upgrading.
39.       Research Training Centers.  There are simple teaching facilities at
two special crop research stations, one at Ulu Dusun for oil palm and the
other at Quoin Hill for cocoa; Ulu Dusun has a hostel but Quoin Hill does
not. The need for in-service training of extension staff in special crop
technology makes it necessary to upgrade these facilities and add hostel
accommodation at Quoin Hill. These facilities would also be available for
farmer training.
40.       Teaching Staff.  Problems of qualified teaching staff pervade all
agricultural institutions in Sabah. Almost all existing staff of the AVS
and ATC are newly qualified, but have no formal training in teaching methods
or field extension experience. The posts of Director of the ATC and RTC, and
senior lecturer positions at the AVS should be filled by graduates rather
than by diploma holders as at present. Efforts to recruit graduates from the
Peninsula have been unsuccessful, so until sufficient DOA staff can be
trained for these positions, it is planned to recruit contract officers from
the private sector and to send suitably qualified DOA staff for further
training. During the TNIP, 9 graduates and 13 diploma holders are expected to
return from overseas training and some of these will be appointed to teaching
positions.
Rural Training - Home Economics
41.       The purpose of home economics in rural areas is to show families
various ways of improving their homes by teaching them skills such as sewing,
elementary tailoring, cooking, child care, hygiene, nutrition, family planning



ANNEX 5
Page 13
and home economy. Home Demonstrators (HD) are sent to the various villages
throughout the State to organize short courses of six weeks' duration,
for a minimum of 25 students. Materials and equipment for the students
are supplied by the Department.
42.       HDs have no transport themselves and have to borrow vehicles from
the District AO when they can, thus they waste much of their time walking
long distances or waiting for buses. Shortage of transport also hampers
adequate supervision of their work. Housing and transport would be provided
under the project.
43.       Animal Husbandry and Veterinary Training.  In-service training
for veterinary staff is provided at the veterinary stations at Kepayan and
Sebrang, each having one classroom and residential accommodation for 12
students. In the past six months, preservice training for new junior staff
and in-service training of serving officers have been conducted by veterinary
staff at these centers. In addition, the DOA would expand facilities for
poultry training at Likas and Tawau, and at Tuaran for piggery training.
In the past, academic levels of trainees have varied considerably and staff
shortages in the field have limited the extent to which staff have been
available for short upgrading courses. Future demand for new operative level
staff should be met by the output of trainees from the AVS who have specialized
in livestock during their third year. These recruits will receive further
training in veterinary medicine before being posted to the field.
Training in Nonagricultural Skills
44.       Few DOA staff have had training in management skills and a weakness
in management is also common in the FAs (see Annex 2); at present FAs are
managed by diploma level staff who have had a general agricultural training
but very little preparation for the administrative duties and management
decision making which their positions entail. A similar situation exists in
the Padi Board. There is, therefore, an urgent need to provide:
(a) in service training in extension and communication methods for
all field staff;
(b) training in management for supervisory staff of the extension
service, FA and Padi Board managers; and
(c) study visits for senior extension staff to countries with well
developed extension systems.
Training of Tractor Operators
45.       DOA and the Padi Board employ several hundred tractor operators,
most of whom have had no training in the proper care for, and use of, farm
machinery. No facilities exist in Sabah to provide instruction in tractor
operation and maintenance, though the need for this type of training is
sufficient to justify DOA establishing a special training program for it.



ANNEX 5
Page 14
TRAINING PROPOSALS
Agricultural Vocational Schools (AVS)
46.       A high priority at the AVS is to make it a more attractive place
for suitably qualified staff to reside. To this end, a telephone link with
Kota Balud would be installed and internal connections made to houses and
administrative buildings on the campus; campus roads would be improved, and a
small bus would be provided to travel daily in and out of Kota Balud to carry
patients to the clinic and children to school as well as collecting the mail
and bringing provisions for the AVS. Fifteen houses for junior staff would
be built to attract nonteaching staff to work at the school, and to increase
the operational effectiveness of the institution, a further 700 acres of the
school farm would be cleared, developed, and provided with the following:
additional teaching aids and equipment, one lorry, one four-wheel drive
vehicle, farm equipment and seven air conditioners. Details and costs
of these items are shown in Annex 6, Part I (Section C) and Part II
(Section E).
47.       An improvement in the teaching capability at the AVS (see para. 53)
would strengthen the preservice course and increase its output to meet the pro-
jected demand for new operative level staff. Necessary steps would be taken by
Government to ensure adequate selection procedures and availability of scholar-
ships to achieve full annual intakes at the AVS.
Agricultural Training Center (ATC)
48.       New facilities for the ATC would be constructed at the AVS so that
the campus at Timbang Menggaris would become a combined preservice and in-service
complex. The Principal of the AVS would be in charge of the complex to ensure
maximum coordination of use of teaching expertise and facilities; because of
its special role, teaching programs at the ATC would be administered separately,
with the Head of the ATC being responsible to the Principal for the organiza-
tion, planning and operation of ATC courses. The total cost of the new facili-
ties would amount to M$1,840,600 (Annex 6, Part II, Section E).
49.       The increase in the student population at Timbang Menggaris would
exceed the capacity of the present water supply; therefore, the project provides
for improving the water supply system.
50.       The present ATC facilities at Tuaran would be utilized for two
purposes: (a) additional accommodation for research staff, and (b) residen-
tial courses, seminars and meetings in specialist subject areas for which
research and headquarters staff would provide most of the instruction.
51.       Rural Training Centers (RTC).  Two staff houses, a hostel and a class-
room block would be built at each of the two RTCs, to supplement existing dilapi-
dated facilities which would receive essential repair. (Details and costs are
shown in Annex 6, Part II, Section E, Table 2.2.)
52.       Livestock Training.  One Division I house would be built at the
Sebrang Cattle Station to enable a full-time instructor to be posted to the
station.



ANNEX 5
Page 15
53.       Teaching Staff.  Scholarships would be provided in the project to
enable ten teaching staff from the AVS, four from the ATC, and the Principals
of the RTCs, to attend instruction on adult teaching methods, and skills for
teaching practical agriculture. Selected staff of the SELU would receive
specialized training in agricultural teaching methods, and would provide follow-
up assistance at the training institution in the introduction of improved
teaching methods and course design. Teaching staff at AVS, who have no field
extension experience, will assist extension workers for short periods. To
help staff improve the practical application of their teaching, M$110,000 are
included for teaching aids for the various training institutions.
54.       Training of Rural Women.  Twenty-three houses for EDs would be con-
structed at strategic field locations and designed so that they can easily
be used as classrooms for small groups of women. One four-wheel drive vehicle
would be provided in each district for use in support of home demonstration
programs, and 150 sewing machines would be included in the project for issue
to HD upon completion of their training. One scholarship is also included
for the further training in rural home development methodology of one senior
officer from the home economics section.
55.       Management Training.  Special courses would be provided for State
and district supervisory staff in personnel and financial management, and land
use planning for agriculture. Nine scholarships are included in the project
for FA managers to receive training in principles of business management.
56.       Training in Extension and Communication Methods.  A high priority
would be given to the training of supervisory staff of DOA in extension and
communication methods. The unused capacity at AVS would provide this training
until the new ATC is able to take over this function. The extension specialist,
provided under technical assistance (see Annex 1), would assist with the
planning and execution of this training, and would also be involved in follow-up
work in the field to help with the introduction of better extension methods.
Senior officers from the State extension service would benefit from fellowships
for overseas study tours to gain experience of improved extension systems.
57.       Training of Tractor Operators.  A training unit staffed by a profes-
sional level mechanization instructor, 2 AAOs and supporting staff would be
established which would visit the premises of larger employers of tractor
operators.
58.       There are no available staff within DOA at present with the necessary
skills to head this unit and the project would provide two man-years of technical
assistance for a farm machinery specialist who would lead this unit, develop
an effective training program, and train counterpart staff. Equipment for
the farm mechanization unit would include a four-wheel drive vehicle, tools
and teaching aids (Annex 6, Part I, Section C).
59.       Training Responsibility:  The training activities necessary to
strengthen the manpower capability of DOA will require the appointment of a
senior officer at headquarters, to be responsible for the detailed planning
and implementation of training programs. The post of Department Training



ANNEX 5
Page 16
Officer will therefore be created and a suitable officer appointed at the start
of the project. A position is already available within the DOA for this purpose.

3. SARAWAK
BACKGROUND
Staff Numbers
61.       Current grades and staff numbers of the DOA are shown in the
following table together with the new staff required under TMP, including
those to be added under the Project (shown in brackets).
New staff
Present   needed
Rank               Title                          Qualification    no.      by 1980
Professional       Agricultural Officer (AO)    Degree              60     121 (15)
Subprofessional   Agricultural Assistant (AA)  Diploma              80     138 (27)
Operative          Jr. Agri. Assistant (JAA)    Certificate        811   1,048 (89)
Training Institutions
62.       Professional Level.  In the past, no degree or diploma level training
in agriculture has been provided in Sarawak and recruits were sent by DOA to
UPM in the Peninsula for this training. In 1974 UPM took over the facilities
of the School of Agriculture at Semongok and commenced diploma level training
in agriculture which will provide a sufficient output to meet the needs of the
TMP. However, students will continue to be sent to the Peninsula for general
degree training in agriculture; for specialized training they would be sent
outside Malaysia. Forty-nine students selected by the DOA are currently
undergoing degree level training and these should be sufficient to meet the
new professional officer needs of the DOA during the TMP.
63.       Operative Level.  When UPM took over the facilities at Semongok, the
School of Agriculture was moved to temporarily to Tarat which has accommodation
for 80 students with limited teaching space and farm facilities. A general
course in agriculture and one in home economics are taught at Tarat: animal
husbandry and fisheries training is performed on-the-job in an unsatisfactory
ad hoc fashion. To meet the great demand for new staff, training of operatives
is currently confined to a one-year course which does not provide sufficient
preservice training for new JAAs. There is, therefore, an urgent need to
establish an agricultural institute similar to those in the Peninsula and
large enough to meet the demand for preservice training of operatives for the
agricultural industry in Sarawak.
64.       Young Farmers.  There are ten Agricultural Training Centers (ATCs),
throughout the State, eight of which offer a 14-week course for students who
have completed primary school, and two provide a one-year course for young
farmers with secondary education. The Young Farmer Training Program is



ANNEX 5
Page 18
suffering from insufficient follow-up by extension staff once the young
farmers have completed training, and consequently many abandon farming.
Demand for Operatives Training
65.       Projected demand for trained operatives will more than double the
present number by the end of the TMP. Almost half of the 1,048 new operatives
will be needed to meet the projected expansion of SLDB which requires staff
with an agricultural training for its expanding credit program.
In-Service Training
66.       Duties of extension staff include a number of purely regulatory
activities (see Annex 3). Lack of extension education experience combined
with limited preservice training (one year compared with three years for
similar staff in the Peninsula), and shortage of training facilities for
formal in-service training courses, gives rise to an urgent need to provide
these facilities and train field staff at all levels in specific technical
areas, and communications.
Training of Supervisory Staff
67.       Supervisory staff in the extension service have qualified for their
present positions either by gaining an appropriate academic qualification in
agriculture, or on the basis of their years of field service, but few have
received any training in management and supervisory skills. Furthermore, as
the emphasis of their work in the past has been on regulatory activities,
most supervisory staff have inadequate experience of the operation of effective
extension systems. There is, therefore, a great need for training of these
staff in supervisory skills, and giving the more senior staff opportunities
of gaining firsthand experience of effective extension systems.
TRAINING PROPOSALS
Training Institutions
68.       Agricultural Institutes:  A training complex comprising an Agri-
cultural Institute (AI) and an Rural Extension and Training Development
Center (RETDC) would be established at Semongok, 12 1/2 miles from Kuching,
where 300 acres of land are already reserved for this purpose adjoining the
chief Agricultural Research Center and University Pertanian's Sarawak campus.
The Al would have accommodation for 290 AI students plus accommodations for
30 in-service training resident students and would offer a two-year certificate
course with an annual intake of 125. The present one-year basic training
course at Tarat would be continued until the new AI is fully operational and
has graduated its first certificate students. Assuming the new AI would be
completed by 1979, the projected output of new operatives would be as follows:

69.       No difficulty is foreseen in recruiting sufficient candidates with
Senior Cambridge Certificate for training as operatives to fill projected
annual intakes at the AI as, by 1979, it is estimated that over 2,000 boys
and 1,000 girls will be leaving secondary schools in Sarawak with the necessary
qualifications for entry to the AI.
70.       RETDC.  The siting of the AI/RETDC at Semongok would enable students
to benefit from practical instruction utilizing the specialized facilities of
the nearby agricultural establishments - the Research Center, brackish water
station, pig and poultry farms, fishponds, etc., and this would reduce the
investment necessary at the AI/RETDC in the establishment of an institution
farm. As the proposed new AI will be fully committed to preservice training
for a number of years to come, an RETDC would be constructed at Semongok. The
RETDC would be operated in conjunction with the AI, but it would be designed
and staffed to handle the in-service training for DOA as well as for other
agricultural agencies in Sarawak. The RETDC would have a capacity of about
30 students for 12 three-week courses per year (360 students per year).
Although use of RETDC facilities would be planned separately from the AI, the
two institutions would be able to share catering, library, auditorium, farm
and recreational facilities and the Director of the AI/RETDC campus would be
in charge of both institutions. Some benefits would also accrue from teaching
staff being able to serve both institutions. Details of facilities, equipment
and costs of the AI and RETDC are shown in Annex 6, Part I (Section C) and
Part II (Section E).
Teaching Staff
71.       Present teaching staff of the AI and the additional teaching staff
needed for the new, expanded AI - particularly in the specialized fields of
livestock and fisheries - would be available to assist with teaching at the
RETDC. Teaching staff requirements are shown below:

72.       Present teaching staff at the institute at Tarat will form the
nucleus of the teaching staff of the new AI, and will move to the new AI
as soon as the facilities are completed. New staff will be recruited for
the new teaching posts and those not having field experience will be posted
to the field for a period to gain first hand experience of field extension
conditions before they commence teaching. Also, all permanent teaching
staff of the AI/RETDC would attend a course in agricultural teaching methods
at UPM. Scholarships for this purpose are included in the project. The
siting of the AI at Semongok will enable the teaching staff to maintain
constant contact with workers at the Research Center, and will enable maximum
involvement of staff from UPM and the Research Center in the teaching of
special courses at the AI and RETDC.
73.       The curriculum and syllabuses for the new two-year course which
will be taught at the Al are being compiled for approval by the Departmen-
tal Education Board. The curriculum would provide ample practical training
to supplement classroom teaching, and every student would be expected to
undertake a farm project, including a complete set of input-output and
financial records, as part of the course.
74.       Subject Mfatter Specialists (SMS):  In addition to formal training
at the RETDC, which will concentrate on strengthening background knowledge
of agricultural technology, extension methods, etc. in the field, in-service
training of staff would be achieved by regular training of junior field
staff by their supervisors. Supervisors would be assisted by SMSs who would
act as a two-way communication channel between extension and research. Six
additional SMSs would be financed under the project who would be specialists
in one of the following: crop protection, hill rice agronomy, pepper agronomy,
horticulture, pisciculture and soils. Details of their operational functions
are described in Annex 3. Twelve man-years of scholarships are included in
the project for postgraduate studies for these SMSs.



ANNEX 5
Page 21
Supervisory Staff
75.       Extension Training.  Study visits are included in the project to
enable supervisory staff at headquarters and district level to travel to other
countries to see successful extension programs in operation. Supervisory staff
at district level would have opportunities to visit selected areas of the
Peninsula where successful extension schemes have been introduced. It is hoped
that, from these visits, staff would gain ideas and inspiration to devise
innovative approaches to extension in their own areas.
76.       Management Training.  Supervisory staff at district and headquarters
level would attend courses at the RETDC for instruction in basic management
skills - particularly in the fields of human relations and staff motivation,
work planning, and administration. A peripatetic team from INTAN would provide
this instruction and some follow-up to help supervisory staff with the analysis
of problems and planning solutions in their own office.
77.       Courses in business management would be arranged for managers of
farmers associations, padi board officials and DOA staff having responsibility
for business dealings. Suitable courses are currently offered by INTAN and
funds would be included in the project for transportation of selected candidates
to these courses.
Training Implementation
78.       The Assistant Director, Extension Education and Training Branch of
the DOA would be responsible to the DDA for the implementation of the training
component of the project (see Chart in Annex 3). The Principal of the AI/RETDC
would be responsible to the ADE for the operation of the AI/RETDC. A curriculum
committee, made up of representatives of the major employers in the agricultural
sector and chaired by the DOA, would meet at least semi-annually to approve
programs of courses at the RETDC and matters of policy regarding the use of the
RETDC facilities.
Agricultural Extension Communication
79.       Even when the number of extension staff has been increased and exten-
sion methods improved, access to rural communities will constitute a major
constraint to the communication of new ideas. This is because of the limited
road access together with time-consuming and/or expensive water or air transport.
Extension communication media have, therefore, a great potential role. The
Information and Communication Section of the DOA has limited facilities at its
headquarters in Kuching for the production of printed materials in the form of
agricultural booklets/leaflets. Its issues of the Farmers' Bulletin, which has
a circulation of 4,000 copies in each of four languages, are printed by commer-
cial firms. It has no production capability for radio programs, or visual and
audio-visual materials. A number of radio programs are now produced in colla-
boration with Radio and Television Malaysia, Sarawak, but these are on an ad
hoc basis and, of necessity, are only of a general nature. For maximum effect,
radio programs need to support field extension programs with some group listening



ANNEX 5
Page 22
arrangement in the target communities. Also, visual and audio-visual aids
and more printed materials have to be produced for use by the field extension
agents.
80.       For capital and operating costs, and for fellowships M$1,038,600 and
M$210,000 respectively are included in the project to strengthen the Information
and Communication Section of Sarawak's DOA. This will include Lhe establishment
of Radio Program Production and Visual/Audio-Visual Production Units, and the
strengthening of the present "Editorial/Publication Unit."
81.       The Radio Program Production Unit will produce regular and frequen,
radio programs in the state's four major languages (Bahasa Mala-sia, Iban,
Chinese and English). The component would include a small studf. 0 with necessary
equipment, additional staff and operating costs, and scholarship.s for four staf f
in program production techniques. There should be no problem in finding suii:able
broadcasting time for the new programs. There is no space for the new studio
within the existing DOA headquarters.  The studio would be sited on land near
the state complex which houses the DOA headquarters.
82,       The Visual/Audio-Visual Unit will produce visual materials (such as
flip charts, transparencies, photographs, etc.) and audio-visual materials (such
as tape-slides). These materials will be used by the field extension agents.
The staff of this unit could be housed in the office part of the recording
studio. In this way, the recording equipment can be shared, especially in the
production of tapes for the tape-slides. Additional staff and scholarships
have been included for this unit.
83.       The present "Editorial/Publication Unit" needs strengthening, espe--
cially in the form of proper printing equipment for in-house printing, and
technical editorial staff. This will enable the unit to produce more up-
to-date publications (booklets/leaflets, posters, technical and semitechnical
bulletins, information newsletter, etc.) for consumption by the field extension
agents. Purchase of printing equipment, appointment of addit-oral staff (one
AO, two AAOs, two Home Economics Supervisors, two AAs, four Translators and
one driver), operating costs and scholarships have been included for the project.
84.       The establishment of the Radio Program Production Uni-t, Visual/Audio-
Visual Unit, and the strengthening of Sarawak DOA's present Information and
Communication Section, will greatly help the field extension agents and other
DOA staff in carrying out their duties because their work will be supported
by various communication media/materials, such as radio programs, visual and
audio-visual materials and printed materials - an integrated communication
approach. Details of costs are shown in the staff supplementary tables
(Report No. 1555a-S-MA).

PENINSULAR MALAYSIA
PILOT PROJECT TO DEVELOP THE USE OF TELEVISION IN
SUPPORT OF AGRICULTURAL EXTENSION
BACKGROUND
86.       The effectiveness of the DOA Agricultural Extension Service is
limited by levels of training of the field extension staff, difficulties of
access to farming communities, and attitudes and awareness of farmers - this
is particularly so in the designated poverty areas of the country which
generally have the least developed communications infrastructure. While the
proposed Extension, Training and Seeds Project would provide for a substantial
increase in the numbers of accessibility, farmers' attitudes and lack of
understanding amongst rural communities will continue to present major
problems to the achievement of agricultural development goals in these
poverty areas.
87.       In this context the use of mass media in support of field extension
programs has a high potential value, and, with the successful introduction of
the Education Television Program, Malaysia is in a pre-eminent position (in
relation to most other countries in Asia and Africa) in that television
receivers are now established in schools in rural areas in all parts of the
country. While the prime purpose of these television sets is now, and will
continue to be, for use for in-school education, at the same time they offer,
at marginal costs, a means of testing and evaluating the use of this powerful
medium in support of rural development initiatives. Most important amongst
rural development activities is the development of agriculture, but more
experience in the use of television in support of agricultural extension is
needed before a large scale program can be launched. Recent projects in
India and Japan have produced valuable information on the use of television
as a tool for farmer training, and also for assisting with the in-field
training of agricultural extension staff, and much of this experience would
have relevance in Malaysia. The well developed agricultural broadcasting
unit of radio Malaysia provides an excellent base from which a unit for the
production of agricultural television programs could be developed, and
officials of the Ministry of Education and its Educational Media Service
(EMS) are willing to cooperate with advice and assistance in the development
of programs to utilize the existing television broadcasting and receiving
equipment for broader rural development purposes.
PROPOSAL
Pilot Project
88.       The Extension Training and Seeds Project would include a component
pilot project to develop the use of television in support of agricultural
extension. The Pilot Project would have the following objectives:



ANNEX 5
Page 25
(a) To test the effectiveness of different types of television programs
on farmer audiences in different farming environments;
(b) to test methods of involving field extension staff in organized
farmer viewing sessions as a feature of specific extension programs,
and
(c) to study ways in which television could be used as a means of
supporting in-field training of extension staff.
89.       For the purposes of the Pilot Project a number of pilot areas would
be selected in different parts of the country (including one each in Sabah
and Sarawak) which would be representative of different farming environments,
but which would be reasonably accessible for the purpose of monitoring and
evaluation. Within these pilot areas DOA extension staff would receive
special instruction so that they become fully aware of the objectives of the
Pilot Project and can participate as required in the necessary field work.
90.       A current constraint to the extensive use of television in support
of rural development programs is the availability of broadcasting time.
However, for the purposes of the Pilot Project, 40 minutes of air time would
be allocated on three days a week, and additional air time would be available
during school holidays and at weekends. Special allowances would be necessary
for participating field extension staff who would be expected to work during
weekends with farmer viewing groups. If the results of the Pilot Project are
positive and a much wider use of television in support of agriculture and
other rural extension programs is found to be justified, an expansion of
television broadcasting capacity would be required. However, this would not
form a part of this project.
Program Production
91.       A production team would be established which would comprise two
Producers (AO level), two Assistant Producers (AA level) and two Script
Assistants (AT level). This team would be attached to the Development and
Agriculture Division of Radio Malaysia which already has very considerable
experience in the production of agricultural programs for radio, and has
already begun producing general agricultural programs for television. Only
a small amount of equipment would be necessary, for the purposes of the Pilot
Project, to supplement what this Division already has. The Development and
Agriculture Division would designate one senior officer to organize and
administer all program production work. Two man-years of fellowships are
provided in the project for the training of Producers outside Malaysia; this
would be supplemented by supervised on-the-job training conducted by staff of
the National Broadcasting Training Center.



ANNEX 5
Page 26
Evaluation
92.       An evaluation team would be established from existing staff within
ELU which, while conducting evaluation work, would maintain close liaison with
the program production team and with field extension officers responsible for
extension work in the selected pilot areas. In addition, UPM may be able to
assist with this evaluation work.
Planning and Administration
93.       The Pilot Project described above would be an entirely new venture
which would require maximum possible communications between DOA, ELU, Ministry
of Education, EMS, Radio Malaysia and State Extension Offices of the States
in which pilot areas are selected. A planning and liaison coamittee has
been established which would have the responsibility for the detailed planning
and administration of the Pilot Project. This committee has the following ex
officio members who would have administrative responsibility for the Pilot
Project.
Director General of Agriculture
Representative of Radio Malaysia (RTM)
Director EMS, Ministry of Education
Head of ELU
Representative of the Ministry of Agriculture
In addition, the following, or their representatives, would have ex-officio
membership of the committee for information and advisory purposes:
Directors General of Veterinary Services, Fisheries, Drainage and
Irrigation, MARDI and the Rice Research Institute, and the Chairman of
the Land Development Authority, RISDA and FELCRA.
Implementation
94.       During the first year of project implementation staff for the Pilot
Project would be selected and trained, pilot areas selected, and detailed
plans prepared. Program production and the training of field staff in
selected areas would be carried out in year two so that actual broadcasting
could commence in year three. Details of staff, equipment and costs of the
Pilot Project are shown in the supplementary report No. 1555a-s-MA.

MALAYSIA
NATIONAL EXTENSION PROJECT
Implementation Summary
Extension Implementation
1.        The project would represent a modest and realistic strengthening
of the existing extension services within the context of an established
and operational organization which is basically sound, but deficient because
of a shortage of staff, inadequately trained staff, and poorly equipped
staff. Over the five-year project period existing staff would receive
regular periodic in-service training, the number of field level staff would
be increased from 780 (a ratio of 1 to 1,300 families) to 1,340 (a ratio of
1 to 800 families), provision would be made for increasing and maintaining
the technical knowledge and communication skills of all existing and addi-
tional staff of the Department of Agriculture (DOA), as well as other agri-
cultural agencies through regularized institutional in-service training.
2.        The project would provide for an average 70% increase in field-
level extension workers (from 780 to about 1,360) in 12 states consisting of
almost 100 districts over the five-year project period; in Peninsular
Malaysia the increase would be phased out as follows: year 1 - 66 (13%
increase), year 2 - 78 (14% increase), year 3 - 124 (19% increase), year 4 -
114 (15% increase), and year 5 - 18 (2% increase); a total increase of 500 to
900 agricultural technicians (ATs) in 10 states or an average of 40 ATs per
state and less than 6 per district (see the supplementary report No. 1555a-s-MA).
Most of the 400 additional ATs in Peninsular Malaysia would be graduates of
the Agricultural Institutes (AIs) and would represent 15% of the projected
output during the project period. In an effort to increase Al output, the
DOA has already initiated a program to provide 100 scholarships in 1977 and
proposes to provide additional scholarships each year. In view of the
priority attached to the project and the modest numbers required, adequate
additional ATs would be available. Similar increases would occur in.Sabah
and Sarawak.
3.        IQ order to provide effective service to rural families in the more
remote areas, additional offices and housing would be required; therefore
32 Extension Subcenter (ESC) bases for extension would be provided in
Peninsular Malaysia (30 in the 10 states under the project plus 2 in Kelantan).
Sites for ESC's offices, stores and housing would be on existing state owned
lands and in some cases on lands yet to be acquired. ESCs would utilize
existing or modified public works department designs (JKR). Each ESC office,
store and housing complex would cost about US$70,000 in Peninsular Malaysia
(Annex 6, Part I, Section B, Table 1.3).



ANNEX 6
Part I
Page 2
4.        In Sarawak each of the 10 ESC office, store and housing complexes
would cost US$145,000. The additional cost in Sarawak is the result of
higher construction cost in East Malaysia and accommodations for a larger
number of staff to be based at each ESC. These ESCs are considered the
basic essential amenities required to enable field staff to live and work in
remote rural areas.  Additional offfices, stores and housing would also be
required in all states to upgrade facilities for existing staff.
5.        ESCs are neither a luxury nor an innovation but simply the basic
requirement for an effective and efficient distribution of minimum office,
store and housing needs to make extension services available to the majority
of farmers even in the poorer and less accessible areas. The federal and
state governments are committed to the proposed extension staff, facilities
and equipment to meet the minimum requirements of achieving the Third Malaysia
Plan (TMP) objectives of assisting the poorest rural sector of Malaysian
society. The project field staff (AT) increase of 560 under the project
represents only 54% of that proposed under the TMP (1,045 ATs).
6.        In addition the project would provide 101 man-years of fellowships
for upgrading extension management and technical staff in 13 states (including
Kelantan) (Annex 6, Part II, Section F). The project also provides five years
of technical assistance in extension organization, management, training and
communications (Annex 6, Part II, Section D).
Training Implementation
7.        Training, both preservice and in-service, is the key to strengthen-
ing and maintaining an effective extension service and is thus accorded a
high priority by the federal and state governments. The rationale for provid-
ing training facilities is sound, particularly for Sabah and Sarawak, which
because of their geographical and political isolation from Peninsular Malaysia
and the relative undeveloped nature of their agriculture and agricultural
infrastructure, require both adequate preservice and in-service training
facilities of their own.
8.        The Regional Extension Training and Development Centers (RETDCs)
are required throughout Malaysia to ensure adequate and regularized in-service
training on a continuing basis for DOA staff as well as agricultural staff of
other agencies. Initial priority would be given to in-service training of DOA
field-level extension staff (Vol. II: Project File, Annex 6, Section E). The
first RETDC would become operational late in year 2, thus in the interim period
in-service training would be conducted on an ad hoc basis at existing AIs.
9.        Four RETDCs would be strategically located in the Peninsula so that
training facilities are readily accessible to all states with a minimum of



ANNEX 6
Pcrt I
Page 3
travel. Construction of one RETDC would commence late in year 1, one in
year 2, and one each in years 4 and 5. The 4 RETDCs each with a resident
capacity of 40 would conduct a minimum of 10 three-week courses per year with
an estimated throughput of 2,500; also the facilities and staff would be used
to conduct short-term (one to several day period) specialized seminars and
workshops. At full development the RETDC would accommodate all of the 2,100
DOA service staff (1,700 existing plus 400 additional) for at least one
three-week course each year. The RETDCs would also provide specialized training
programs for staff of other agricultural agencies after DOA's requirements
are met. A manpower and training requirements study of other agricultural
agencies would be conducted during the first year of the project.
10.       The total capital cost for each RETDC in Peninsular Malaysia would
be US$500,000. Similar facilities at proportionally higher cost (reflecting
higher construction and equipment cost) would be provided in Sabah and Sarawak
in combination with the proposed Agricultural Vocational School (AVS) and Al
facilities.
11.       Provisions have been made for training teaching staff (Annex 5,
p. 10 and Annex 6, Part II, Section F). Alternative modes of training were
considered (Annex 5, pp. 7, 8 and 16). In view of political, financial and
technical considerations the RETDCs as proposed in the preparation report
were considered satisfactory.
12.       Extension Liaison Unit (ELU) staff and consultants would be the
primary source of course and instructional materials; however, they would be
supported by training specialists to be provided by the project and a Manage-
ment and Curriculum Committee described in Annex 5, pp. 8 and 9.
13.       The nature of agricultural services, particularly extension,
requires a continuous revision of course material, subject matter and curri-
culum design program based on current requirements; thus a predetermined and
fixed program and curriculum would not be appropriate. The project provides
34 man-years of extension fellowships for training, to plan, organize and
prepare RETDC training programs.
14.       The seed production system would be designed to operate on a viable
basis yielding a rate of return of at least 14% at full development. Detailed
cost and implementation schedules are contained in Annex 6, Part II, Section C.
Two man-years of technical assistance would be provided to assist with the
design of facilities, selection of equipment, preparation of tender documents
and construction of facilities during the early stages of the project (Annex 6,
Part II, Section D). Thirty-one man-years of fellowships would be provided
for training of seed production and processing technical staff (Annex 6,
Part II, Section F).



ANNEX 6
Part I
Page 4
15.       A "seeds fund" as working capital to purchase farmer outgrower
seeds is not required until year 3 (the first year of projected outgrower
production) in Peninsular Malaysia and the total amount of the "seeds fund"
would be less than US$200,000. In Sabah the required working capital or
"seeds fund" would be US$60,000.
16.       In Sarawak, which would eventually have a higher percentage of
farmer outgrowers, the "seeds fund" would be required from year 1 and would
amount to US$156,000 over the project period.

MALAYSIA
NATIONAL EXTENSION PROJECT
Bene4Fits and Justification
Section A - Financial Results
1.        The benefits of the training and extension components of the
proposed project are difficult to isolate and quantify for financial analysis.
However, the benefits in terms of increased production and farm incomes would
be substantial enough to justify the project. The government crop production
centers (CPCs) are expected to become financially self-sustaining by full
development, eliminating the need for current GOM subsidies. The seed sub-
project thus produces benefits directly quantifiable for financial analysis.
2.        Estimates of seed production by the CPCs are shown in Section A,
Table 1.2. The calculations of the financial rates of return are shown in
Section A, Tables 1.4 and 1.5. Table 1.3 shows estimated revenues for
the CPCs.
3.        The following assumption has been made with respect to final distribu-
tion of CPC seed produced:
of total seed produced p.a., sales distribution is divided between
commercial sales, sales to GOM for subsidy schemes and a percentage
allowance for reject and damage sold on a consumption basis. The
percentages of annual distribution of total production are as
follows:
1977    1978    1979    1980    1981    1982-2002
Commercial sales               35       40      45       55      65         75
Reject and damage              15       15      15       15      15         15
GOM subsidy schemes            50       45      40       30      20         10
Production Estimates
4.        Production estimates are assumed to rise from 1.0 ton/acre for padi,
maize, soybean and sorghum, and 1.3 tons/acre for groundnuts. These production
estimates are based on production levels presently being obtained at the exist-
ing CPCs. For Peninsular Malaysia, production levels will be 2,400 tons of
seed p.a., with padi the primary seed produced (2,000 tons p.a.), by full devel-
opment. These production levels are assumed to remain constant thereafter.



ANNEX 7
Page 2
5.        In Sabah production levels will reach 430 tons p.a. by full develop-
ment, with padi and maize the primary seeds produced. In Sarawak production
by full d-'-lopment will be 175 tons p.a., with padi being the only seed
produced initially. Other seeds such as maize and groundnut will be phased
into -roduction once full development is achieved. (See Section A, Table 1.2
for estimated CPC production levels.) It is estimated that by full develop-
ment the CPCs will produce about 35 to 40% of the Malaysian requirement for
seeds (see Annex 4, paras. 61, 64, 65).
Prices
6.        The project is intended to reduce dependence on imported food
grains. As all seeds produced are therefore intended for domestic sale,
current Malaysian domestic farm-gate prices per ton of seed have been util-
ized for the financial analysis. All input prices used are current Malaysian
f.o.b. prices for foreign exchange goods, and current domestic prices for
local goods. Staff salaries have been costed in accordance with present GOM
salary scales for DOA employees.
7.        There is at present no set pricing policy for seed sales in Malaysia
(Annex 4, para. 74). The financial analysis has been intended to develop a
model by which the CPCs can become financially independent of present GOM
subsidies for seed production by full development. In accordance with proposed
GOM policy (Annex 4, para. 93) a 30% price premium has been added to prices
for seed sold on a commercial basis. This price premium begins in 1982 and
continues thereafter.
Results
8.       The estimated financial rate of return for the total seed subproject
is 14%. For the Peninsula's CPCs the calculated rate of return is 26%,
which shows the centers can become financially self-sustaining and eliminate
the need for present GOM subsidies.
9.        In Sabah and Sarawak there are at present no large-scale crop produc-
tion centers. The project is therefore providing infrastructure already present
in Peninsular Malaysia, leading to high short-run capital costs. Basic opera-
tional costs in Sabah and Sarawak are also much higher than in the Peninsula,
as basic inputs such as fertilizers, etc. are scarce and must be imported at
a higher cost. Transport costs within Sabah and Sarawak are higher than in
the Pan4rLsula, as these states do not have as well developed a road system
(in m_ny cases transport is confined to waterways).
10.       The result of these cost differences is a low financial rate of
return fo  the Sabah and Sarawak CPCs.  However, the analysis shows that, given
improved management and technology, both of which can be gained through opera-
tional e. .rience an  by transfer from the Peninsula, these CPCs can operate
at or above 100% cosL recovery in the future (see Annex 6, Part I, Section B,
Tables 1-6 and 1.7, C sh Flows for Sabah and Sarawak CPCs). The project is
providing the basic infrastructure for seed production, and once experience
has been gained, output can be augmented without increasing the capital base
of the CPCs. This operational experience will also lead to a reduction in
per ton operating costs.



ANNEX 7
Page 3
11.       Another factor supporting the establishment of CPCs is that locally
produced seed is cheaper than imported seed. The following table compares
the costs of imported seed to the cost of seed produced locally in Sabah and
Sarawak.

12.       As the cost comparison shows, by project completion in 1982 it is
cheaper for Sabah and Sarawak to produce their own seed than it is to import
seed. Assuming incremental production increases by 1992, and no change in f.o.b.
price projections, the cost of locally produced seed is greatly decreased when
compared to imported seed. Again assuming sufficient transfers of management
skills and technology from Peninsular Malaysia, further cost reductions are
feasible at full development of the seed centers and with operational experience.
On-Farm Benefits
13.       The cost of locally produced seed for the farmers is substantially
less than that of imported seed. Assuming a premium price for HYV seeds
(see para. 7), on a I ha farm, a farmer will pay about M$17.00 for adequate
supplies of local seed (see Section B farm models: Tables 1.4 through 1.6) as
compared to about M$23.00 for adequate supplies of imported seed./l On a
statewide basis these savings would be considerable and would generate substan-
tial on-farm savings.
14.       The supply of seed available for local farmers will also be increased,
eliminating possible seed shortages that might occur if imported seed is not
readily available. These factors justify bearing the high short-run cost of
seed production in Sabah and Sarawak in anticipation of the long-run benefits.
Section B - Economic Analysis
General
15.       A summary economic analysis of measures to strengthen the national
extension services proposed under this project has been carried out using
available knowledge, to assess the economic viability of the project. Although
the benefits of such a project are not readily quantifiable,t2 an analysis has
been done to check that with reasonable assumptions a good rate of return can
be expected.
16.       Padi is the main crop and the primary seed that will be produced as
a result of the project, and hence is used as the base crop producing benefits.
The Peninsular Malaysian CPCs devote 83% of production to padi, with the
remaining 17% devoted to groundnuts, maize, soybean and sorghum. In Sarawak
/1 Assuming a 1 ha farm, an adequate supply of seed is approximately 55 lb.
/2 The extension and training components of the project yield no quantifiable
benefits in isolation. Benefits realized from these project components
would arise through increases in foodstuff production throughout Malaysia.



ANNEX 7
Page 5
the CPC will initially produce 100% padi, with other crops (groundnuts, maize,
etc.) being phased into production gradually once full development is reached.
In Sabah maize and padi are being produced in equal quantities for domestic
use, but the economic analysis is based on only main season wet padi.
17.       Indicative farm models showing increased production and inputs over
the project maturation period are shown in Tables 1.4 through 1.6. The
analysis is conservative in that only the main season wet padi crop is included
and most farmers also grow small areas of off-season and dry rice, or other
crops. Section B, Table 1.7 shows the increase in man-days of labor for the
Peninsula farm model (appropriate adjustments were made for the Sabah and
Sarawak farm models). The economic rate of return calculation is derived in
Section B, Table 1.1. All investment costs were aggregated from Annex 6, Part
II, Section A. Section B, Tables 1.2 and 1.3 show production levels of padi
in Malaysia for 1975 and 1968-75, respectively.
Production Estimates
18.       Total padi area cropped is expected to increase by approximately
200,000 acres in Peninsular Malaysia, 25,000 acres in Sabah, and 31,000 acres
in Sarawak by 1983. Per acre padi production is expected to increase from
present levels at an average rate of 3% p.a. giving yield increases as follows:
Peninsula - 1.0-1.34 tons/ac; Sabah - 1.16-1.56 tons/ac; and Sarawak - 0.66-
0.9 tons/ac resulting in annual incremental increases in padi produced of
approximately 260,000 tons in Peninsular Malaysia, 30,000 tons in Sabah, and
23,000 tons in Sarawak. These yield increases are feasible given that:
(a) the seed centers produce sufficient quantity and quality HYV seeds
for use by farmers;
(b) farmers will have ready access to these HYV seeds;
(c) the increase in extension services under the project will provide
rural families with information about improved crop husbandry so as
to maximize yields using HYV seeds.
Should a controlled water supply become available, these yield estimates
could be expected to increase further. Nevertheless, substantial increases
in yields are attainable by improved crop husbandry and seeds.
19.       The project also provides for the production of other seeds, primarily
maize, soybean, groundnuts and sorghum. The main beneficiary of this seed
production will be Sabah, where the CPCs will produce an estimated 150 tons of



ANNEX 7
Page 6
maize seed per annum, which will in turn sow approximately 150,000 acres and
produce 200,000 tons of maize by full development. In Peninsular Malaysia the
additios.aL_  ea to be cropped using these seeds amounts to 450,000 acres,
producing 550,000 tons p.a. by full development./A The Sarawak CPC will
initlally produce only padi seed, with other crop seeds (maize, groundnuts,
etc.) phased into production by full development.
20.       Additionally padi production over the project maturation period of
ten years would increase total Malaysian padi production by 16% over 1974-76
levels (see Section B, Table 1.3). This would reduce Malaysia's potential
future dependence on imported food grains and seed.
Prices
21.       Padi Prices.  The proposed project would reduce Malaysian dependence
on imported rice (primarily high grade Thai and Chinese rice), which has
amounted to approximately 150,000 to 200,000 tons p.a. in recent years. Padi
has been valued in terms of Bank projections for the price of Thai rice (5%
broken, f.o.b. Bangkok). As the grades produced in Malaysia are closer in
quality to 15% broken Thai rice than to the 5% export grade for which long-
term projections are made, the f.o.b. Bangkok prices have been reduced by 10%,
maintaining the price ratio between the two grades that has prevailed in
rezent years. A detailed breakdown of the f.o.b. price estimates used for
padi is presented in Section B, Table 1.9.
Project Life
22.       An evaluation period of 25 years was used for the economic analysis.
This time period assumes a 25 year life for buildings, with no terminal
salvage value. Terminal salvage value is included for vehicles and equipment.
With proper operation and maintenance, which has been included in the costs.
Project facilities should easily remain in use for this period.
Results
23.       The results of the economic analysis are shown in Table 1.1,
Section B.  They indicate that to achieve a rate of return of 25%, a total
rise in padi production of only 3.8% is required by 1987 over and above the
average production of 2.0 million tons for the base period 1974-76. Padi
production is expected to increase over the period by much more and it would
be reasonable to assume that more than 3.8% of the total rise would be justi-
fiably attributable to the impact of the proposed project.
/1  Sce Section A, Table 1.2 for estimated CPC production levels of other seeds,
Esiimated acreage to be cropped is calculated on the assumption that one
ton of CPC seed u11 crop 100 acres.



ANNEX 7
Page 7
24.       The present extension, training, and seed production infrastructures
in Sabah and Sarawak are substantially less developed than those in Peninsular
Malaysia. The project is thus providing a much more extensive expansion of
infrastructure in these two states. For this reason project benefits will
not be realized as rapidly there as in Peninsular Malaysia, as the base
infrastructure will take longer to be established, but by full development,
however, Sabah and Sarawak will realize substantial benefits (relatively
similar to those in Peninsula states) as a result of the project. HYV seed
will be produced at a lower cost than imported seed (see para. 11 and asso-
ciated table), and the establishment of Farmers' Associations in Sabah and
ESCs and AFBs in Sarawak will provide an excellent means by which extension
workers can reach farmers with a supply of HYV seeds and infrastructure needed
to apply modern farming techniques. The increases in production will be the
result of using HYV seeds and necessary inputs (primarily fertilizers and
agro-chemicals) (Section B, Tables 1.4-1.6). Access to this package of tech-
nology, and appropriate information on how to make proper use of it, will
be supplied by extension workers to be provided under the project.
Farm Incomes
25.       The proposed project would contribute to raising real net padi
farm incomes of project farmers by US$80-100 per family (US$15-19 per capita)
assuming present farm-gate prices. The income increases, given the small size
of padi farms, represent the maximum impact attainable through increased
extension and seed production services. While the project would raise only a
few padi farmers above poverty income levels, it would provide a 14 to 26%
increase in income to the largest identifiable poverty group in Malaysian
society.
26.       On a regional basis, real net padi farm incomes for project farmers
are estimated to increase by US$99 per family (US$18.70 per capita) in
Peninsular Malaysia, US$83 per family (US$15.40 per capita) in Sabah, and
US$89 per family (US$16.80 per capita) in Sarawak (see Section B, Farm Models,
Tables 1.4 - 1.6).

Notes:  The increase in annual value of crops (1977 prices) required
from Year 10 onwards (on 1974-76 as a base period) to achieve a
25% rate of return is M$59.9 m/ 1.362 or M$44.0 m.
The 1974-76 base period padi production averaged 2.0 million
tons, valued (in 1977 prices) at M$262/ton, i.e. M$524 million.
Thus the required Year 10 value of production increase is 8.4%
of the base period value of production. Allowing for the pro-
jected rise in the real value of padi (from M$262 to M$581/ton
in 1977 prices), the total production increase, attributable
to project extension and training, required over the ten-year
period to achieve a rate of return of 25%, would be only 3.8%.
